This highly insulated box has openings that align exactly with the original openings, the reveals of which are lined with laminated timber. The interior walls are painted white to reflect the sunlight and this is reinforced with an enormous mirror that covers the

entire slanting ceiling of the north half of the open space.

The building is referred to as the Posehuset, which means “bag house”. This is a direct reference to the somewhat eccentric previous owner of the property who had a penchant for collecting plastic bags. The studio has the quality of looking as if it has always been there, while at the same time being completely new. It contains the character of the region, but also belongs to the 21st century.

The intimate relationship between an interior and its immediate context can be observed in Florian Beigel’s design for the

Half Moon Theatre. The auditorium was a collection of enclosed spaces that were formed in an unused and neglected gap on the busy Mile End Road in London. The distance between the actors and the audience was consciously reduced to encourage deliberate interaction. Thus, the open and connected interior spaces were conceived of as a continuum of the street, as if the journey from the outside world was almost accidental and the audience members had unintentionally wandered into the drama.

Figure 1.26 The plan of the Half Moon Theatre in London shows how stability has been created through the insertion of a collection of pure rooms into a disordered and leftover space.  
![](images/d4f03108f45f56dbf646fc01def1aa24e7fdf6840e804c7a3d960ff9e05e7f27.jpg)

Leça Swimming Pools

Location Porto, Portugal

Original date 1966

Designer Álvaro Siza

![](images/c474cad4763ea0b37ece897268b6db25bb117341a3592fe755f38f4f2aa43fc7.jpg)

The context that a building, interior or situation occupies can inspire the nature of the adaptation or design. The natural and artifi cial elements that constitute the site can provide the impetus for the remodelling of the environment.

Figure 1.27 The swimming pools are squeezed between the massive infrastructure of the road system and the energy of the Atlantic Ocean.

Through careful study, combined with intuitive reaction, the designer can create a new environment that appears to be more than an extension of the context: it actually completes it. The situation is so inspiring that any interventions seem to be born of that environment, to have evolved within

that place, rather than having been designed and placed there.

This is exactly how the swimming pools by Álvaro Siza on the Portuguese coast just to the north of Porto appear. They are situated on the Leça de Palmeira beaches, on the northern coastline of Matosinhos, crushed next to the rocks between a duel carriageway and the Atlantic Ocean. The industrial port can be observed across the sands.

Siza embraced this situation by integrating the pools with the rocky coastline. The

Leça Swimming Pool complex consists of changing rooms, a café and two swimming pools, one for adults and one for children. The building is sunk below the level of the road; this immediately creates a disconnection between the pools and the infrastructure of the city. The visitor enters the complex via a smooth concrete ramp which is parallel to the road, and as the path slopes downwards, so the disengagement with the city begins. A concrete barrier, just a shade lighter than the natural stone, reinforces this detachment. The visitor passes through the quite brutal changing rooms made of the same coloured concrete, and is then encouraged to turn towards the sea.

This is the fi rst real engagement with the magnifi cence of the water, and the experience is intensifi ed by the delayed gratifi cation. The two pools merge with the rocks and they appear to be of a similar magnitude. The children’s pool, which is situated towards the land, is bound on one edge by a curving concrete wall and the other by the rocks, whereas the adult pool is positioned further out towards the sea. Three edges are made from concrete, while the other is the natural formation of the rocky coastline. The water is refreshed daily as the tide fl ows in and out of this natural location. The orthogonal concrete interventions do not seem out of place in this tranquil and organic setting. There is a clear view towards the Port of Leixoes, one of the most important cargo ports in Portugal, which handles around 14 million tonnes of goods and 65,000 passengers each year. The shared industrial language creates a strong relationship between the pools and the quays and harbours. Thus the Leça Swimming Pools appear to have the quality of emerging from the nature of the area and the culture and industry that has developed there.

Figure 1.28 The rocky coastline and the facilitating concrete structures share the same robust language.  
![](images/7883ff66b867693a582e34f647e771d91b42ab11736b7e03ca94cc4dd2bd6c4f.jpg)

## Half Moon Theatre

Location London, England

Original date 1979–85

Designer Florian Beigel, Architecture Bureau

![](images/1938aed1f6d78258cb5fca974f120be10a911bca6257f8329aba6b928b72b21e.jpg)

Figure 1.29 Carefully   
placed walls among the unruly and abandoned structures create the   
pure space necessary for theatrical   
productions (previous page). Figure 1.30 The section shows the relationship that has been   
established through   
the building, between the street at the front of the site and the garden at the rear.

The Half Moon Theatre Company was a radical and innovative group that developed creative and alternative ideas to the established order of theatrical productions. They challenged the conventional nature of the auditorium, and questioned the space between the theatre and the street, the cast and the audience. The theatre company developed progressive productions that drew on the most direct forms of theatre of the past, such as the Italian commedia dell’arte, and the Elizabethan theatres with galleried courtyards such as Shakespeare’s Globe. The guiding principle was to break down the barriers between the actors and the audience and encourage both to be seen by each other.

Beigel and Christou proposed a design for the new theatre that exploited this concept; they developed an auditorium space that resembled a typical street scene. Thus the Half Moon Theatre was basically a spatial progression starting from the pavement of the Mile End Road in the East End of London, through a gateway into an open-air scenic space or public courtyard. The journey continued through a steel curtain and into the main performance area, which again both fi guratively and metaphorically resembled a scenic street. From here, large doors led into a small garden and the Young People’s Theatre pavilion. This was a dense and layered series of spaces, some inside and some outside; each was distinct but held

together by their proximity and the manner in which people moved between and occupied the journey.

The street scene was so particular and strong because it was actually a series of pure spaces within the awkward shape of a once derelict area. This was achieved with the careful placement of new three storey block walls containing rectangular openings.These acted as linings, defi ning the exact dimensions of the rooms and also importantly providing acoustic control. This complex surface geometry to the sides of the auditorium effectively scattered the sound to ensure good acoustic diffusion. The new high ceiling also created a problem of late-refl ected sound, and an absorbent sky, that is fairfaced woodwool slabs above what appeared to be a fl oating perforated metal ceiling, provided the acoustic control. The pattern on the fl oor, which was a slightly different angle to the geometry of the space, reinforced the dynamism of the journey, and encouraged movement within the platonic solidness of the auditorium. Beigel and Christou created not a building, but the space between buildings. The theatre responded to the context by extending the street into the heart of the space, encouraging a dynamic relationship between the urban elements of the immediate surroundings and the intimate nature of the theatre.

![](images/99e7b292e1f9ffb4b0e0fe5fd6dd023a98e55867828ff06baf94a437f4d4106a.jpg)

# José Guerrero Museum

Location Granada, Spain

Former function Townhouse

New function Art Gallery

Remodelled 2000

Designer   
Antonio Jiménez   
Torrecillas Figure 1.31 A very   
direct relationship   
exists between the   
museum and the   
Cathedral. This is   
especially visible from the top fl oor of the   
gallery where, through the large horizontal   
window, an intimate   
connection is   
established between the exhibition and the ornamental cornice of the Cathedral.

![](images/fc83adb8c1ca625858ebc7874afccec48e33dcdfccd383f477fb0d4dc75a0e09.jpg)

José Guerrero was an Abstract Expressionist painter; he created big vibrantly coloured images that suggested massive landscapes, primitive architecture or possibly even atmospheric events. He spent his early years in Granada before emigrating to the USA, where he hung out with many prominent members of the American avant-garde including Willem de Kooning and Mark Rothko. Guerrero returned to Granada towards the end of the Franco regime when a more relaxed and tolerant period had emerged.

Antonio Jiménez Torrecillas created a museum dedicated to the work of the painter in a 19th century house in the centre of Granada. The approach that the architect took was to exploit the highly intimate relationship that the building has with its constricted context, while providing space and light for the explosive nature of the paintings. The building is accessed through the narrow and contained streets of the old city. These lanes and passageways evolved in this location because they provide shelter from the worst of the summer sunshine and protection from the cold winter winds. The nature of this urban landscape means that there is no opportunity for the whole building to be viewed from the front; the prospect is always oblique, from the side, without access to the complete picture. It is impossible to see the complete façade.

The building itself is a fairly traditional three storey townhouse of this period, brick with arched windows. The architect’s fi rst intervention was to position a new marble plane over the ground level façade; this mimics the original frontage, but also signals the change of use. The original interior was stripped away, and replaced with a very simple organisational device: Torrecillas placed an unadorned freestanding folded wall into the space. This acted to push the services, such as the elements of circulation and the offi ces and the facilities to the edge, thus allowing the centre of each fl oor to be dedicated to exhibition. As the visitor negotiates the journey through the building, they are presented with glimpses of the views from the existing windows. These are constrained and engaged by the context, as the walls of the cathedral opposite are just a few metres away across the street. As the vertical promenade continues, so the level of natural light increases and the intimate relationship with the cathedral is amplifi ed.

The fi nal and most dramatic intervention is the placement of a new storey on the top of the existing building. This is completely contained and has just one very large window, which provides a spectacular vision of the cathedral wall. The ornamental cornice, bathed in sunlight is just an arm’s length away. The view is treated with the same reverence as the pictures, with a carefully positioned bench encouraging the visitor to contemplate the magnifi cence of the situation. Torrecillas has provided the Jose Guerrero Museum with a building that echoes the excitement of the work of the great painter with a statement of equal remarkable intent.

# Kuala Lumpur Performing Arts Centre (KLPAC)

Location   
Kuala Lumpur,   
Malaysia

Former function Railway shed

New function Performing arts centre

Original date 1906   
Remodelled 2004

Designer Baldip Singh Bhullar

![](images/4c82260db5f49ab54e21ccad9691a6ef17050a799b62dac1f568d24ce8417c0a.jpg)

Figure 1.32 The language of the original building is derived from European train sheds. This is in stark contrast to the new orthogonal interventions.

All buildings display the narrative of their existence. The language of the building will tell the tale of its life. It will express the culture of those that constructed it, and will speak of the time of its assembly. The style will express the attitudes of those that commissioned it and the capability of the architect. The materials will divulge the ambitions of the owners, the shape will convey the needs of the inhabitants and the condition the attitude of those that cared about it. It will belong to a particular place, time and culture. These clues or cultural references describe the nature of the building; they reveal its identity.

When an architect or designer adapts the building for a new use, they consider whether to embrace these allusions or reject this evidence and intelligence. The building contains many layers of information, which can be enhanced and brought to the fore, or suppressed, hidden, ignored or just forgotten. Rodolfo Machado describes the process of remodelling as interpretation. He considers the already built to be a script or written indication about which the designer will develop an attitude and understanding. It is the information that already exists within the existing building that will provide the impetus for the design of the adaptation. And thus a palimpsest is created, one in which the past, the present and the future all interact.

The KLPAC is situated in a leafy suburb of Kuala Lumpur. The Centre, which is the only independent democratic performing and learning environment in the country, contains two theatres, a fi lm studio, rehearsal spaces and a café. It is housed in a converted railway building, which was constructed during the British colonial era in Malaysia. This facility served the needs of the Federated Malay State Railway from 1906 onwards. The single spanned space is a massive 30 metres wide and 60 metres long. It is a huge English-bonded red-brick arched depot, with a series of arched openings at ground level and circular

windows above. The language of the original building is obviously from this imperial period, in that it directly references the railway sheds of Great Britain.

Malaysia has made a successful disconnection from its imperial past and repositioned itself as a multicultural, multi-ethnic, open country. This attitude led the architects to develop an approach that actively demonstrated this disentanglement, and so the new elements of the Performing Arts Centre contrast strongly with the original building.

A huge steel and glass box has been placed off-centre over the brick shed; this breaks the symmetry of the original building and yet also reinforces the strength of both. The modern components are of the same magnitude as the host building, but the architectural style is obviously much more recent. All materials used for the adaptation were of local Malaysian origin, and they were designed so that a local workforce could construct them. The design is deliberately neutral,

Figure 1.33 The walls of the auditorium are lined with detritus from the site. These remnants from an earlier age also act as acoustic baffl es and help to effectively distribute the sound.  
![](images/1206e1d78383e88b1a64cc0ba5a93f5dc664ebe91bdf7468b34302b111fd2616.jpg)

Figure 1.34 An   
ornamental staircase stranded in its original position as an   
evocative reminder of the original purpose of the buildings.

which allows the performances within to refl ect the pluralistic society of contemporary Malaysia.

The detritus of the previous existence has been left in its exact position. The low brickwork walls, which have been retained to delineate the grounds, have new openings, these are made in a modern manner using exposed concrete lintels, and an extraordinary painted cast-iron staircase stands lonely in a courtyard, its open steps leading nowhere.

The original building has been retained as a testament to its time while the new elements are contemporary and exhibit the forward-thinking mentality of this progressive country.

![](images/cc9cde18616ecb5d1f0a37910d5cfa556aaa45bd81ada6f499c75a37241f7b44.jpg)

Figure 1.35 The walls of the original building have been left in situ to control the external space.  
![](images/f7f747df7fe09600035ac4c4e4d9aa1ed64ca03b6538c37588f15a5943cf91fe.jpg)

## Proposed Function

## Introduction

Architects and designers must be very certain that the new function will fi t happily into an existing building if the conversion is to be both convincing and appropriate. However masterful or clever a piece of design is, if it is not suitable for the new users of the space, then it just doesn’t work. The designer needs to be very confi dent that the requirements of the new users will fi t adequately into the remodelled building, and will conscientiously make a great deal of effort to understand what these are. The client may not always fully understand the requisites themselves and sometimes request something that is very similar to their present situation, not fully appreciating the opportunities that are possible. This could be a chance to reorder the manner in which they operate as well as reorder the space. The buildings that we occupy shape us all, and we are constrained by the limitations of the spaces we inhabit, so a new building or interior is an opportunity to pose questions about organisation, relationships, expectations and requirements.

6a Architects were asked to convert a collection of disused buildings in Spitalfields, London into a contemporary arts exhibition centre. The project involved the amalgamation of two former mercers’ houses and a 1970s concrete-framed office building. The buildings had been empty for over a decade because of the difficulty of finding the right sort of organisation that could possibly fit into such an odd assortment of structures and buildings. The spaces are certainly beautiful, but very difficult from a curatorial perspective: the wall panelling and decorative plasterwork make it difficult to show paintings, the cellular plan creates security problems, and the environmental conditions are difficult. However, this was the opportunity for the

arts centre to embrace more contemporary forms of art, to abandon the absolute requirements for white walls and clean box-like spaces, and to occupy a place of character and worth and encourage the art to interact with the space.

The gallery occupies a contained block that fronts two streets with a central courtyard that separates the two distinct buildings. The north-facing building consists of two houses, which date from the 1690s. These were remodelled in the mid-18th century and the façades still retain the ornate Rococo frontage and domestic scale of its use as a silk merchant. The buildings had suffered from neglect and the consequences of a ruinous fire in the 1970s. The restoration undertaken after the blaze was sensitive although it was at this point that the two houses were knocked together on each of their five floors. The south-facing structure – situated on the charmingly named Frying Pan Alley – was a prosaic 1972 concrete-framed office building.

The approach that the architects took was to retain the charm and character of the buildings and then, rather than heavily contrast the new interventions with the old building, they created new elements that strangely looked as if they had always been there, and yet had a contemporary character. The majority of the work to the older properties was based upon the restoration of the interiors; here the architects acknowledged not only the original building, but also the reconstruction work after the fire. There was one exception to this: in the 1920s the linings of one particularly ornate room on the first floor had been somewhat fortuitously dismantled and sold to the Art Institute of Chicago. The panels were returned to the UK in the 1980s, and as part of the project, reinstalled in their original position.

Figure 1.36 There is very little reference to the transformed Raven Row gallery on the exterior of the building.

Figure 1.37 The   
interiors of the existing buildings were   
carefully restored,   
creating a domestic   
atmosphere in which to display work.

The basement floor of the newer building was lowered by one and a half metres. This was then connected to the newly covered courtyard, thus providing the useful ‘white-box’ exhibition space. This also linked the ceilings of the entire ground floor with a relatively similar height. All of the services are cleverly pushed to the edge of the plan, thus freeing up the centre for exhibition and display. The upper rooms of the gallery retain their domestic character, while the spaces within the more banal structures offer more anodyne and

![](images/998cc9cd3a64b5865566ed002b4b08ec6fc1ff8958da8b26faaabf4a1bb35ea1.jpg)

anonymous exhibition areas. The architects have developed an arts centre that does not have the traditional requirements of a gallery; that is, a huge amount of blank white walls. Instead they have created a mixture of different spaces that allows for a variety of artworks. This design was based upon an intimate knowledge of the needs of the users of the building. Through analysis, discussion and interpretation the architects were able to remodel the interior and create a gallery that was exactly what the client needed.

![](images/f3b3c4f9ea295a4de3bf2c5b3d2c39f3f1699a14dde258362bc8bef97b954e1b.jpg)

# St Peter’s Seminary

Location Scotland

Original date 1966

Designer Gillespie, Kidd & Coia

![](images/ddf93748ce12436ab10dc057d1cd446deac4c5e1b3371078f652d2915c652cfc.jpg)

Figure 1.38 The great dining room of the seminary commands a romantic and brooding presence.

Most buildings have resilience, in that they are suffi ciently robust to allow a remodelling to occur. An uncomplicated building may have a number of distinct lives; it may undergo many quite simple transformations. An obvious instance of this is the 18th- and 19th century warehouse, of which there are innumerable examples all over the western world. Many which have outgrown their initial use have been converted into galleries, apartments, shops or offi ces. However, a building that is designed so specifi cally to accommodate the needs of the users, that is so exactly measured to the rhythm of their requirements, can be incredibly diffi cult to adapt. Some buildings are unambiguous

and precisely designed around the functions and the activities that take place within them, meaning that the character of the space is extremely distinct. To make the building anywhere near approaching habitable, almost impossible concessions would have to be made by the new occupiers of the building.

One such building is St Peter’s Seminary. It was designed by the Glasgow practice Gillespie, Kidd & Coia, and was consecrated in 1966 as a training college for Roman Catholic priests. It is a powerfully modern cast concrete or béton brut building set within the wild countryside of southern Scotland. It contains the

traditional elements of a seminary: chapel, refectory and cells, all of which are organised around an open court. In the time since construction, the manner in which priests are educated has changed – rather being cloistered away in isolated communities, they are encouraged to gain experience of real life and enter into small parishes. In 1980, the Catholic Church closed St Peter’s down and the building was used as a drug rehabilitation centre for a few years. However, since 1987 the building has lain empty and has been allowed to become derelict. The building is very exact: it responded so completely to the needs of the users, so acutely that it has proven impossible to convert it into anything else. There have been a number of proposals to convert the building, including plans for a hotel and for domestic accommodation, but the inconvenience of the site, the cost of the proposals, and the very specifi city of the building itself defeated these.

The neglected building has started to be used as a canvas by artists: graffi ti adorns the walls and pop videos have been fi lmed in the dirt and detritus of the ruin. This Brutalist masterpiece is also a place of pilgrimage for architects, who stare in wonder and understanding at the exposed structure. The ruin is a place of romance. It has a direct connection with memory, with

Figure 1.39 The seminary has been allowed to fall into ruin and become a playground for artists and musicians.

![](images/e20d748c97fe58792d199babb1e111b9c17b6a4757f305894df53a4458d56aa9.jpg)

the society of an earlier age and importantly with a lack of utility. In an age of gentrifi cation and usefulness, something that is both broken and without function contains an air of intrigue. It allows the visitor to engage their imagination and connect with the past and the future. A ruin could be regarded as an acknowledgement of the force of history, of nature, climate and culture. It is a sign of a superseded society, a monument to a distant time of different priorities and values. The preservation of a building in a ruinous state recognises this. Perhaps sometimes the best approach to an obsolete building is not to do anything, to let it crumble and decay, to let it be.

# Dornbusch Church

Location Frankfurt, Germany

Original date 1962

Remodelled 2005

Designer Meixner Schlüter Wendt

![](images/0ce5b40df3beb17b007f27a1a9500c09f98a002ce584221a733b07bfb92bccb1.jpg)

Figure 1.40 The great relief on the south side of the church serves as a reminder of the areas of the building that have been taken away.

It is the responsibility of the architect or designer to acquire a highly specifi c knowledge of the needs of the client and the users of the remodelled building. This knowledge allows the designer to tailor the design of the adaptation to their exact requirements. The designer can propose a strategy that the client may not have considered; they can suggest a method of organisation that the users of the building had not previously thought of. The designer has the ability to garner a strategic view of the problem, which the client or the user may not be able to see.

The Dornbusch Church is situated in a residential area in the north of Frankfurt. The original building was constructed in the 1960s, at a time when fi re stations and churches seemed to look very similar: a great hall of suffi cient size to hold several engines or a large congregation, fl anked by a tower that was either used to dry the hoses or support the bells.

This particular tall white bell tower became symbolic of the progressive attitude of the area, while the congregation of over 12,000 were housed in an enormous glazed block.

Figure 1.41 Diagram created by the architects to show the reorganisation of the buildings and spaces.

Figure 1.42 A large exterior space for recreation has been created between the tower and the church. The position of the pews and other furniture has been marked on the ground to provide an amusing pattern to play within.

However, by the beginning of the 21st century, the building was in poor condition and the congregation had dwindled to less than a third of its original size. A proposal was made for the complete demolition of the building with the construction of a new much smaller prayer room to replace it.

The architects Meixner Schlüter Wendt felt that this was not the right approach and after intensive consultation with the community, undertook a series of planning studies from which they were able to develop an overview of the project. They proposed a solution that was far more advantageous to the congregation, the people of the town and to the immediate urban area. This alternative scheme involved just the partial demolition of the building, and the remodelling of the remaining structure to create a considerably more clever and complex solution than just a simple prayer room. Thus the immediate settlement contains what could be regarded as an archetypical church with a landmark tower, a churchyard or public square, and a community hub.

The nave of the church was substantially reduced; half of the building was simply taken away. This act of subtraction concentrated the internal organisation of the building. A highly coloured stained glass window, which had once been on the periphery of the space, has become the focus of the reordered church. An elaborate wall that appears to be carved in relief now fi lls the raw edge of the building – this provides both stability and also articulates the narrative of the change. The sculptural surface seems to almost echo the elements that have been removed from the building, thus acknowledging the passing of time and the changing of attitudes.

![](images/fcb77320973ff395d60c3316c3a155dd69a55f6c727e2a1b24f8af5655064709.jpg)

Other changes to the building were fairly minimal, basic conservation and decoration. However, on the outside of the building the remodelling is very obvious. The demolition created a great separation between the church and the tower. This exterior space is marked with the memory of the original nave, the layout of which is painted onto the grey asphalt, thus creating a surface that is obviously reminiscent of the lost building, but strangely also looks somewhat like a playing fi eld. The public space is approachable, it encourages people to use it and yet is under the protection of the church – an evocative memento, perhaps, of the place that the church once held within western society.

![](images/f7971e9a9ddc3baf671d46c3eee8ad2c144977226f4dca9e8a47ab62799fe68a.jpg)

# The Cement Factory / La Fabrica

Location Sant Just Desvern, Barcelona, Spain

Former function Cement works

New function

Family home and studio

Remodelled 1975

Designer Ricardo Bofi ll

![](images/99fd51279904abfcc21cf9f69c78ae8154a6913f1df639d3fc6a89456752ba8d.jpg)

The Modernist adage that form follows function does not generally apply to building reuse projects. The form of the building already exists. The spaces are defi ned: the walls are in position, the roof is in place and the relationship between these elements and the immediate context has long been established.

Figure 1.43 The mature foliage reinforces the character of the ruinous building.

Of course it is possible to change all of this. Walls can be demolished, new elements constructed and fresh relationships established – but the essence of the building will still exist. The character of the place will still be present. This is the charm of the remodelled structure. It retains the character of the original programme and inhabitants, and combines this with the needs of the new users. Adaptation is always a delicious compromise between the two.

The vast complex of a disused cement factory on the outskirts of Barcelona was, in the mid-1970s, converted into an offi ce and a home for the architect Ricardo Bofi ll. This extraordinary and romantic project was the vision of the young architect, who had not only grown up in the construction industry, but had come of age in a country just emerging from the oppressive years of the Franco regime. It was an extraordinary time in Barcelona, a city with a vivid and progressive attitude that had nurtured such artists as Gaudí, Picasso, Miró and Dalí, but in that critical post-war period also saw the rise of Brutalism. So the sight of the almost surreal complex of concrete structures, did not daunt Bofi ll, but apparently actually fi lled him with a ridiculous kind of magical hope.

![](images/00b064e81c48a75cde19beb7ac31fe5c792afc420e03f8fa68f3bffc6abcd192.jpg)  
Figure 1.44 Plan of the cement works showing how the big spaces are quite modestly occupied.

The uninhibited cement works was immediately adjacent to the site where the architect was constructing the Walden Seven housing complex at about the same time. This uncompromising multi-level building-city is candidly monumental: it contains 18 towers, 446 apartments, bars, shops and 2 swimming pools. It actually overshadows the factory and this contrast of scale between the two structures reinforces the sculptural quality of both.

The cement factory had been abandoned and was partially in ruins, and so the adaptation process began with further demolition. This removed much of the detritus and additions that had accumulated over the years since the original construction. This defi ned a series of distinct spaces, which were little more than cleaned, thus the memory of the structure’s former use, the industrial aesthetic and the spatial quality is preserved in the raw concrete walls. Small additions, such as new walls to complete spaces, openings to allow for light and access, and vast amounts of greenery completed the project. It then came to the problem of how to occupy this vast edifi ce; distinct spaces had evolved from the process, each had a particular and defi nite quality. The occupiers considered the nature of these new volumes while also contemplating the activities that would happen within them. Thus a symbiotic solution was reached; one which accentuated the conditions and character of the building while ensuring that the users completed tasks in the most sympathetic surroundings. So, for example, the original factory hall was transformed into the conference and exhibition room, and with reference to the ceiling height of over 10 metres, it is called ‘La Catedral’. Slightly distinct from the offi ce in the upper part of

Figure 1.45 Massive curtains simply hang within the architect’s own living room; they serve to soften and control the space.

the factory is Bofi ll’s own home. It has the same raw quality, but is a perfect cube with a series of arched windows. The decoration is sparse yet as equally uncompromising as the building; simple very long white curtains hang from the ceiling and the fl oor is made from untreated timber.

Bofi ll did not necessarily embark upon the process of remodelling the concrete factory with a preconceived idea of how the fi nished project would be. Through a process of discovery and recognition he allowed the form of the building to evolve, and from that the manner in which it was occupied to emerge.

![](images/a2f6b765ac9d37dd2f814601733b0dd6ee78882333c039156097942367fa6150.jpg)

# Rucksack House

Location Leipzig, Germany

Date 2004

Designer Stefan Eberstadt

![](images/eb0c3df2c8bda1d16159173e7764149f83155d23af68a3048d71fffa81520410.jpg)  
Figure 1.46 Extra space is created within a cramped apartment by carefully suspending a new room from the exterior walls.

The lack of usable space within any property can be a problem for the inhabitants. There are a number of quite straightforward solutions to this: an addition to the side, or a new level above, but if the space cannot be extended in this straightforward manner – if it is for example, an apartment, then other more extreme ideas have to be considered.

Stefan Eberstadt developed the Rucksack House in the mid 2000s. It is a very basic but clever method of extending a fi nitely contained dwelling by basically attaching a new room to the outside of the building. This space simply hangs from the exterior walls in a very similar manner, as the name suggests, to the way in which anyone would wear a rucksack. The Rucksack box is compact, just nine square metres of fl oor-space and fairly lightweight. It is suspended from steel cables that are anchored to the roof or to the façade of the existing building. The room is made from a welded steel cage, the interior of which is lined with a light birch veneer. The exterior is clad exterior grade plywood with an absorbent resin surface. The window

openings are fi lled with a lightweight acrylic glass. The concept is that the box is owned by the occupier of the home, and so when they move on, so does their extra room, thus that extra space is always theirs. The Rucksack House was fi rst constructed in Leipzig in 2004, then in Cologne in 2005 and then moved to Bamberg in 2011.

hung from an open window and into which babies and toddlers were placed. This allowed the child, who might otherwise be cooped up in a small, badly ventilated apartment, to enjoy fresh air, sunshine and a glorious view of their surroundings.

The idea of constructing an extra space by suspending a structure on the outside of the building is not new; balconies for example have long been in existence. The Rucksack House may be the most thoughtful, but is possibly not the most audacious.

In the 1920s, at a time when there was a belief that sunshine and fresh air was critical to development and health, the Cubby House Window Cage was developed. This was a metal cage that was

Figure 1.47 The lightweight nature of the Rucksack House means that windows can almost be placed wherever the occupier wants them.

![](images/48b7d3bc767654f4b36ac968f7305d11b7624aedd14ca2f86ba92f25dd3e9ae8.jpg)

## Sustainable Adaptation

## Introduction

One of the greatest concerns for the 21st century population is the search for a sustainable future. There is a growing global awareness of limited resources, expanding ecological footprint, and climate change caused by excessive carbon emissions. The architect and designer can help facilitate this exploration through the remodelling of the existing structures. Building reuse is an extremely sustainable method of development. The environmental movement’s international slogan, Reduce/Reuse/Recycle, is a manifesto for the way to live in the 21st century, and is especially pertinent to interior architecture and the adaptation of structures. The embodied energy in these elements can be saved through upgrade and reuse, and through adaptation the amount of natural resources required to construct a building is greatly reduced. To take this notion somewhat further: the embodied energy in an original building is huge, in that there was a massive amount of energy expended to produce the original materials and construct the building. If this building is just demolished and replaced with something new, and even if the most efficient current, contemporary building is created in its place, it would take up to 30 years in savings from a badly performing to a prime-performing building just to recover the embodied energy within the original materials.

Sustainable use and reuse is a subject that contains two distinct but linked components. A building can be adapted or remodelled using sustainable methods of construction to provide an interior environment that is energy efficient. The designer or architect can also design a place that can be used in a sustainable manner: a place that encourages social interaction, doesn’t waste energy and contains a healthy atmosphere for the end users.

The old post office in Bolzano, Italy was adapted by the architect Michael Tribus in 2006 into the headquarters for the local government Department of Planning and Environment. It is the first administrative building in Italy to be awarded the energy certification of Passive House and Gold Climate House consumption class.

The original building was a somewhat dreary and dirty three storey concrete structure with vertical channels set between the cast panels. It was constructed in 1954, in that slightly panicky period after WWII. The overriding purpose of the remodelling project was to modify the building to create the most energy-efficient office block possible. The whole process was heavily monitored and the behaviour of the resultant building was also carefully measured.

Two floors were added to the structure, thus creating a building five storeys high. A massive 350mm of thermal insulation was applied to the exterior of the walls; this keeps the building warm in the winter and also prevents overheating in the summer. The over-cladding was painted white to stop excessive solar gain. The external window reveals were then carefully angled to optimise the access of the sunlight in the winter and reduce it in the summer. This has created a dramatic angular decorative effect on the exterior of the structure. The building was also provided with a green roof, a heat-recovery system and an active cooling system.

The changes were not just constructional; the interior organisation was modified to make the most effective use of the natural light at the perimeter of the spaces. This was achieved by relocating the circulation and services towards the centre of the

building. Some open-plan office space was introduced to encourage healthy interaction between the users of the building and the enclosed offices were provided with acoustically insulated walls. These significant measures to save energy have reduced the running costs by 90%, thus creating a building that is environmentally, economically and socially sustainable.

It may not be the easiest approach, but it is a very sustainable process to reuse existing buildings. Built environments that have evolved over many years contain an abundance of memory and can often be multidimensional and diverse. Tangible and intangible culture, events and structures can stimulate social cohesion and the feeling of being part of a community. Specific decisions are important to ecological adaptation. The individual and society can consume less, conservation and restraint can be encouraged, thus demand is reduced and efficiency increased.

![](images/f0ea784ba7c05e13104e07a3679ff79e380162d338bd7e5c1690e76111c032b5.jpg)

Figure 1.48 The ExPost Building in Bolzano was the fi rst Italian reuse project to be awarded Passive House status.  
Figure 1.49 The heavily insulated exterior walls of ExPost are articulated to achieve maximum control of the natural light.  
![](images/83352f2d095337e76090370d6e202f6f9b9bde74d50d6aafe22f94e78768c370.jpg)

# Granby Four Streets

Location Liverpool, UK

Date 2013 – ongoing

Designer Assemble

![](images/23393f137da330bb899c1119bc23c66de83aecf4371621bebadaf12d43c682f6.jpg)  
Figure 1.50 Conceptua image of Granby Winter Garden, courtesy of Assemble.

Art activism can be seen as a key element of social protest; art-making can often be regarded as subversive in its very nature as it can challenge existing and established systems. The artist who works outside the system chooses to make art and precludes what conventional wisdom would deem to be productive work. Political art with a social conscience can be regarded as having a sense of responsibility or concern for the problems and injustices of society. It is not uncommon for artists, designers and architects to produce work that questions collective wisdoms and attitudes. It could be argued that one of the main purposes of art is to convey an idea and open the mind of the viewer to seeing the subject in a

fresh, provocative way: it is the responsibility of the artist to shake people out of their complacency.

Over the last generation, the position of the designer or architect has radically changed. Just as the house is considered to be much more than a machine for living in, so architecture and design is far more diverse than the Corbusian model of the ‘masterly, correct, and magnifi cent play of masses brought together in light’. Architects and designers are people who can solve problems, and these problems have expanded beyond the traditional expectations of the profession. Nontraditional models of practice mean that designers fi nd their own projects, they can act as client/developer/user all in one, they use processes of self-organisation to make things that subvert traditional hierarchies.

Assemble are a collective based in London who work across the fi elds of art, architecture and design. Their practice seeks to address the typical disconnection between the public and process by which places are made. Their architectural spaces and environments promote direct action and embrace a DIY sensibility. By employing unconventional means, their projects are self-initiated, self-made or self-organised: they subvert the typical hierarchies and procedures that surround the realisation of architectural spaces. Assemble, regard the built environment as important. They feel that they can make a difference, not through the traditional craft of architecture and design, but through non-antagonistic, small, local interventions, whether are social, physical and/or temporal. As custodians of the built environment, they use nontraditional models of practice to generate a feeling of civic responsibility within the local population of urban users and dwellers.

Assemble worked with the local community of the Granby Four Streets project to transform a corner of Toxteth in Liverpool. A small collective of local residents refused to move out of this row of red-brick terraced houses which were scheduled to be demolished. The houses were originally constructed for artisan workers in the 1900s, but had become dilapidated after a long period of deliberate neglect. Initially the residents of the neighbourhood worked on their own to once again bring positive life and character to the area. They set up a community land trust which was reinforced by quite small scale but productive ideas such as organising a monthly market selling cakes, vintage clothes and Caribbean food, and planting fl owers with a guerrilla gardening project.

Assemble were invited to help improve the houses and neighbourhood. They worked very much with what was there. The project did not include massive new structures but was based upon a clear understanding of the quality and worth of the existing. The sustainable and incremental vision for the future of the area included the refurbishment of the housing and the public space, and the provision of new work and enterprise opportunities by recruiting young residents to work on site, training them in job skills.

An environmentally sound approach was taken. Rather than refurbish every home to the same specifi cation, each property was carefully examined and the best possible solution for the individual structure was pursued. So for example, one house, which was so dilapidated that the ceiling had collapsed, was provided with a doubleheight living room. Another pair of adjoining houses which were completely uninhabitable were converted into a winter garden and fi lled with palm trees and ferns.

The project was awarded the highly prestigious 2015 Turner Prize, which is an annually presented British art award to celebrate new developments in contemporary art. This is the fi rst time that the prize has been won by an architectural and design collective, and they were commended for offering an alternative model for how society can work. The project and the practice refl ect an international movement where socially engaged art or social practice is recognised.

Figure 1.51 Granby Workshop Products, Turner Prize Exhibition, Tramway, Glasgow, 2015, courtesy of Assemble.

![](images/984d728c82f6d5854398bc4e1a5a8cb728b58b9555ae077f80966947960b117f.jpg)

## The Barn

Location Herefordshire, UK

New function Studio

Date 2016

Designer Connor and Darby

![](images/3da576b164ef8a81454cabc70cd159920688b4ca045caf0b9ef6eda685249fdf.jpg)

![](images/72ed5f42256df2fc7ae2b2179fcbff2b76adbd712f83ad4b1b8d227a63a5dc3c.jpg)  
Figure 1.52 The derelict barn was completely encased in locally sourced matte-black corrugated metal.  
Figure 1.53 The existing walls of the original barn have become decorative objects within the new building.

David Connor was one of the most expressive of the postmodern interior designers in 1980s London. He revolutionised design with a small collection of domestic and retail projects. These remodelling projects were as anarchic as the clients that they were designed for and included the Seditionaries shop for the ultimate punk couple, Vivienne Westwood and Malcolm McLaren, and an apartment for the pop punk, Adam Ant. The liberation of money meant that interior design was at the time on the cusp of changing from a very proper pursuit for classically trained designers into something considerably more fashionable, provocative and at times outrageous. Connor’s enormous and expressive freehand drawings for these interiors

epitomise this approach. Thirty fi ve years later he has completed, with Kate Darby, a much more serious project: an ecologically sound architect’s studio.

There are a number of options of what to do with an existing building that is in quite an extreme state of disrepair. These range from demolishing the building and then reconstructing it to look somewhat like an identical but pristine version of the original, to simply accepting the condition of the structure and attaching new elements to complete the whole. Another approach is to construct a completely new and independent structure around or within the original building, thus preserving the existing but getting the benefi t of a new structure. The new can contrast strongly

with the old, so each is different and strongly contrasts with the other, and yet are intrinsically linked by virtue of occupying the same site, responding to the same environmental conditions and being dictated to by the same parameters.

The approach that Connor and Darby have taken is to very carefully preserve an utterly dilapidated farm building and then sublimely wrap this ruin with a pristine black corrugated metal skin. The conservation of the original was somewhat obsessive. The tumbledown structure was indeed ruinous. There was no roof, and just three walls had survived, but everything was saved in exactly the state that it was found, down to the much too low doorframes, the broken glass in the windows, and even the cobwebs.

The new structure sits precisely around the original building and responds intensely to its form. The doors and windows are positioned immediately in front of those of the original cottage, so the user has to peer through the gaps in the cracked and uneven glass, past the old and dusty cobwebs and through the new triple glazed windows to view the outside world. The only place where this acute relationship is broken is in the location of the missing fourth wall. Here the architects have installed an enormously tall window, which faces east and provides a fantastic view past a tremendously old oak tree in to the surrounding pastoral landscape.

The new structure, which is almost childlike in its simplicity, is very sustainable. The corrugated metal was locally sourced. The nearby market town of Leominster has a long tradition of working with sheet metal. By virtue of its position on the border between England and Wales, the town was once highly prosperous mainly due to the woollen trade, but also because of linen

and leather. As this dwindled during the Industrial Revolution, it began to specialise in agricultural machinery and, most importantly for this study, corrugated metal for farm buildings. The minimally detailed black corrugated cladding of the studio is supported by a simple steel frame and hides almost 40cm of insulation. Thus the studio has a relatively constant temperature, which is supplemented when necessary by a wood-burning stove, the fuel for which is collected from the woods behind the building. The remodelled building, though obviously highly contemporary, doesn’t look out of place among the historic collection of farm buildings: its scale, colour and form are all sympathetic to the surroundings and the view from the huge east-facing window allows the occupier to feel at one with the landscape.

## Architecture Archive

Location Somerset, England

Former function Barn

Remodelled 2014

Designer Hugh Strange Architects

![](images/e4e977639724526b9cf2b7e7a0d976c2de923b1cbfe530ced4733d27beda461f.jpg)

![](images/f79cf04dbcc43849886e41de5724031ebaa420edb64863ccf4da353cde8219e7.jpg)

Figure 1.54 A little courtyard separates the offi ce from the archive. A modest concrete bench is set into the retaining wall.

Figure 1.55 The inserted archive is buil to fi t exactly into the confi nes of the original building.

An archive needs a particularly stable atmosphere. The life expectancy of a collection is signifi cantly affected by the environmental conditions in which it is stored, and if problems within buildings occur they can cause signifi cant damage to that collection. A range of environmental factors can affect the preservation of artefacts, including light, pollution, movement (including vibration), insects, temperature and humidity. Of these factors, a number are relatively easy to manage. Radiation in the form of light, which can cause paper to yellow and ink to fade, can be controlled with covered windows and enclosed storage. Dust and atmospheric pollutants, and vibration damage which

can produce decay, need to be monitored; and insects defi nitely need to be eliminated. But it is temperature and humidity that can have the most signifi cant impact upon the condition of archive collections. Relative Humidity (RH) is a ratio expressed as a percentage of the amount of water vapour held in a specifi c amount of air compared with how much it could potentially hold, so for example, when completely saturated the RH of the air is 100% and condensation becomes inevitable. The RH is directly linked to the temperature; the higher the air temperature, the greater amount of water it can hold, so when air cools it can hold less water and thus there is a greater chance of condensation. So, day-to-day fl uctuations in temperature, caused by natural factors such as sunlight or artifi cial reasons such as heating can lead to a fl uctuating RH and make condensation unavoidable. Thus within all archives (and also libraries and museums) it is important for the temperature and humidity to remain constant. The RH can be controlled with the use of air-conditioning, but this is not always a desirable answer. A natural solution, one which is more sustainable and has less environmental impact, is often required.

Hugh Strange Architects have inserted a perfect little self-contained architectural archive within the derelict walls of a disused farm building. This is just one of a collection of highly considered, environmentally sound new and reused buildings on the working farm. The new sloping structural insertion is actually in two parts, linked by a tiny courtyard. The upper area is an offi ce or studio with casement doors opening out onto the small terrace; the lower part houses the architectural archive. It contains necessarily clean and plain walls, and is lit by roofl ights. The structure is set back from the retained walls and rises above them, in a sort of simulacrum of the form of the original building.

The most prominent feature, as seen from the farmyard, is the fi brous cement roof that extends for the full length of the building;

the ridge and eaves drop from end to end in parallel with the gentle fall of the ground.

This gesture maintains the impression of a unifi ed form but the fabric supporting the roof actually comprises two independent enclosures framing the covered entrance yard between them. The new and complete insertion was constructed from locally sourced cross-laminated timber panels; these range in depth from 300mm for the wall panels to an even thicker 420mm for the roof. This simultaneously provides the building with insulation and thermal mass, thus creating the environmentally stable internal atmosphere necessary for an archive.

The insertion is deliberately very pure and simple; everything else required of the building is expressed as clearly additive; conduits, curtain rails, even windows are all attached to the face of the structure. Underfl oor heating, powered by an air-source heat pump, is set beneath inset wide boards in the middle of the fl oor to provide constant temperature; this is supplemented by a black wood-burning stove which acts as a centrepiece to the space.

Figure 1.56 Long section through the two buildings with the covered courtyard in the centre.  
![](images/3dae5f94f78ac0b7d17bf83031487cc328b2982ad5ff8e209a59dcefeb2df37a.jpg)

![](images/19143f9e666409fd229ee46f674276e5b83a351841ee332728aed3123c51880e.jpg)

Figure 1.57 Plan of the building; the circular silos are still in use.

For prolonged study, a bespoke display system has been installed on the long, windowless wall to the farmyard; it is this area that benefi ts best from the provision of toplight. Designed by the architect in collaboration with upholsterer Jude Dennis and joiner Lars Wagner, it comprises a series of large hinged plywood panels that open to reveal a surface of packing fabric on which selected artefacts from the archive are displayed. In a manner characteristic of the project as a whole, these workaday materials have been elevated through the attention paid to their assembly.

## Number 50 Martin Place

Location Sydney, Australia

Original date 1928

Remodelled 2015

Designer   
Johnson Pilton   
Walker

BVN Donovan Hill

![](images/616d97a3a5e5061da3678fe70483f88fff3eded7932aebad17d494c5b149dbfd.jpg)

Figure 1.58 The abundant entrance hall has been extended to encourage the admittance of natural light, and placed centrally within the great space are two futuristic elevators.

A sustainable environment can foster good health and positive attitudes through ideas of community, fellowship and social structures. The architect or designer can deal with the physical aspects of the environment, but without also addressing the social relations, the project may not fully succeed. Lefebvre discussed this in The Production of Space when he commented that when ‘no heed is paid to the relations that inhere in social facts, knowledge misses its target’.<sup>5</sup> Social structures are important, whether recreational or within the workplace, and the designer can facilitate these within the commercial environment. Contemporary ideas about community and cooperation

can be installed within the remodelled building. By deliberately encouraging interaction, and discouraging excessive solitary working, the offi ce environment can become a productively healthy place. The nature of the interior can ensure that knowledge workers will focus more upon inherent communal activities such as communicating, sharing and developing ideas. Over the last 30 years, the proportion of internal offi ce space given over to individual workspaces has dropped signifi cantly in comparison with the increase in spaces for informal collective activities such as gathering points, social hubs and meeting places. (It is interesting to note that this increase in spaces for

social interaction has actually spilled out from the offi ce, and it is now quite normal for meetings to occur offsite in a coffee bar or park.) Thus the architect can help to create empowered and self-reliant people who are equipped with powerful information technology, enjoy their work and are more productive.

Figure 1.59 The circulation route is a dynamic force within the corporate atmosphere of the atrium.

When it opened in 1928, Number 50 Martin Place in Sydney was not only the city’s tallest building, but it also contained the world’s largest banking chamber. It could be described as a rare Australian example of the American-infl uenced inter-war Beaux-Arts style. The 12 storey façade was constructed from a solid red granite base, topped with four soaring Ionic columns and pilasters clad in pink-glazed ceramic tiles. It was originally crowned with a double attic and a most extraordinary rooftop rifl e range. The interior was equally triumphant, with richly veined marble columns placed at strategic points within the grid of the immense decorated banking hall.

Johnson Pilton Walker and BVN Donovan Hill remodelled the building; it is still a bank, so the primary function has not changed, but the methods of work have dragged the building into the 21st century. The fi rst job was to strip the extraneous accretions from the building, including the plant room that had been installed in the 1980s and completely blocked the atrium. This ensured that natural light once again illuminated the huge banking hall. The building was constructed in an era when offi ces depended on natural ventilation and light to work. This was exploited even further: the width of the atrium was increased by about 50% by cutting the fl oor plates back from the internal supporting columns, thus ensuring a massive increase in the natural light fl owing into the building and the air circulating throughout it. A very contemporary insertion was placed within

![](images/fb81540c7bb50e2572eb68a699ce467e80d9b1f19ebb6261aa15b29e94c74e68.jpg)

the conserved atrium: two futuristic, circular glass lifts, which whizz visitors up to a two-fl oor stainless steel canopy on top of the building. This dome was designed as a fi fth façade; it can be viewed from neighbouring buildings as a shimmering lantern. Another contemporary technological addition with a sustainable approach was the replacement of the 1980s air-conditioning ducts and their massive ceiling voids, with water-based chilled beams that take up much less space.

The building could be described as being deliberately functionally inconvenient: that is, the building is organised in a slightly disruptive manner, so that the users have to navigate their way around the building in a less streamlined manner and therefore meet each other. There are a series of strategically placed, specifi cally designed destinations that the users of the building have to pass through. These meeting points encourage staff to speak to one another, to share ideas, to have spontaneous conversations. This reduces the need for formal meetings and can help solve problems more quickly. For example, there is a staircase that rises through the atrium, and like a department store that forces shoppers to take a roundabout route through different fl oors of merchandise to reach their destination, this interna circulation route runs through the fi fth fl oor coffee station. Likewise the kitchens: instead of the previous three per fl oor, there is now just one every other fl oor, thus increasing the chance of people just bumping into one another and the serendipity of meeting: giant water cooler moments.

And so the remodelled structure has symbolically been updated. It has embraced ideas of sustainability not just in the reduction of the amount of energy used, but also in the creation of a community among the users of the building.

![](images/91731a0e06ef14674cff53c5ba192833427b5345f0f93e31a24ec3b57c8fb324.jpg)

![](images/be02d64b63e7c19f5f8705ada653af2b2da413584b885e32e6b5dd457e8cd225.jpg)

## Chapter 2: Strategy

“I am for richness of meaning rather than clarity of meaning; for the implicit function as well as the explicit function. I prefer ‘both-and’ to ‘eitheror’, black and white, and sometimes grey, to black or white. A valid architecture evokes many levels of meaning and combinations of focus: its space and its elements become readable and workable in several ways at once. But an architecture of complexity and contradiction has a special obligation toward the whole: its truth must be in its totality or its implications of totality. It must embody the dificult unity of inclusion rather than the easy unity of exclusion. More is not less.” <sup>6</sup>

## Introduction

Designers and architects will employ a strategy when they design or adapt a building. This strategy is the guiding force that generates the form of the architecture or the interior. It is the set of rules that the architect or designer develops to ensure that they are controlling the design process. From the analysis of the existing situation, the architect or designer will establish a position or opinion. This strategy may be a simple method of organising space and form, it may govern the manner in which structure is distributed within the building or it may be based upon complex contextual factors.

The strategy that is developed when working with an existing building is founded upon the presented situation combined with the needs of the new users. A rapport is established between the old and the new. This association between the new and the old will always be the uppermost consideration. This is because the original structure is already there, and thus any architectural or design interventions will have to take into consideration its presence.

The architectural strategy employed within the adaptation can generally be classified into three distinct categories: Intervention, Insertion and Installation. Of course there are exceptions to this, but this general taxonomy is a fairly good guide to the strategies of remodelling. The groupings are based on the amount that the new parts of the building associate with the original elements. These classifications were presented in volume 1 and so it is expedient to use them again within volume 2.

Intervention is a strategy in which there is a great level of amalgamation. The new elements will so completely alter the existing that it will lose its original integrity.

The changes can be additional, in that they are new constructed features, or subtractive, pieces of the original building may have to be removed to facilitate the adaptation. This strategy is often used with buildings that have already fallen into disrepair, or that need large changes to facilitate the new use.

Insertion is the act of introducing a new element into the existing building. The insertion is generally a single identifiable element that is built to fit exactly within the confines of the host building. The fit is normally exact and removal of the new elements would probably be fairly difficult. The new element may not always be actually within the boundaries of the existing building; it could be next to or even surrounding it. This strategy does not generally alter the existing building that much, and usually results in a very distinct difference between the original buildings and the new insertion.

Installation is adding of new parts or elements to the existing building, but not altering the basic structure. It allows for these new pieces to be subsequently removed and the building restored to its original condition. This strategy is often used in historic buildings where great care has been taken over conservation.

This chapter will explore each of these categories in much greater depth. Situations, happenings and other types of installations will be discussed alongside more traditional examples of adaptation or building reuse as a way of reinforcing the concept while widening the subject.

## Intervention

## Introduction

The etymology of the word intervention is from the Latin inter meaning between, and venire, to come. It is an imposition: to come between, to interrupt, to interfere, to intrude. The most common, non-architectural, use of the word in the 21st century is as a deliberate act of involvement in the affairs of another person: an interpersonal intrusion intended to reform the life of a friend or family member judged to be going wrong. Thus intervention is a highly charged and extremely active expression that means to change something, to create difference, to generate an alteration, and in doing so, to make things better.

An architectural intervention is comparable. The architect or designer will impose a series of related changes and elements into an existing building to facilitate the needs of its users, and in doing so will make it structurally sound, easier to use, and provide it with the required aesthetic quality. Interventions within remodelled buildings can be both pragmatic and visionary. They can exhibit the narrative of the existing building and express the character of the new users; they are wilful, knowing and intentional. The sheer amount of integration between the old and the new means that neither is any longer independent of the other; the interventions will permanently alter the building. There will be direct and irreversible changes to the existing to facilitate the imposition of the new.

The architect will not usually make a single intervention, but a series or collection of them and this invariably involves two processes. The first is stripping back: the removal of extraneous additions or unnecessary parts of the building. This can be a brutal and uncompromising process, which may destabilise the structure. These changes to the existing building

are permanent. The second part of the process is the imposition of newly designed elements. These are designed to fit exactly within the confines of the original building; indeed they may provide the structural support necessary to carry the sabotaged building. The integration is so extreme that neither is now independent; they have become a single entity. This does not generally mean that that they will use a similar language, an intervention rarely resorts to pastiche. The new elements are generally easily distinguishable and of their own contemporary time and place.

In late summer 1988, the Portuguese capital Lisbon suffered one of the most violent fires in its history: the ‘fire of Chiado’. It destroyed 18 buildings in total. Some offices, others residential properties. In the aftermath of such devastation, the city council invited the architect Álvaro Siza to develop a masterplan for the neighbourhood. The historic area, which is very close to the centre of the city, was once somewhat impenetrable, and the approach that Siza took was to develop a series of interventions that would control and liberate the area. It was once inaccessible, closed off, and this strategy opened it up to residents and visitors. Siza reopened an ancient link by creating the Carmo Terrace, a walkway that joins an ancient convent with the street below. This was a brutal stripping away of the firedamaged buildings plus others that Siza felt were obstructing the regeneration of the area. Within this new pedestrian route, or architectural promenade, are a collection of paths, ramps, patios, courtyards, waterfalls and steps. The new route releases this once isolated area without destroying the historic character. It is a series of spaces that appear to be completely natural to the environment and also highly specialised. It has the quality of having always existed, and yet is definitely contemporary. These interventions released the area. They

breathed life into the once closed and dense historic urban neighbourhood through firstly removing things that were causing obstruction, then with the addition of new elements, providing the new route. The interventions were of the appropriate magnitude, the language was sympathetic and they provide the area with a suitable and optimistic future.

![](images/b26a4b25adacea23d1ab9531e1b0137d5990dc05e698b58a6456f5d0415755c3.jpg)  
Figure 2.2 The Prada Foundation in Milan contains a symbol of great luxury: a 24-carat gold leaf covered building.

## Astley Castle

Location Warwickshire, UK

Former function 12th century fortifi ed manor

New function Holiday house

Date 2012

Designer   
Witherford Watson   
Mann

![](images/af62138e19430b1776e1743aafac8f91263509fbe75c8e7fa3db705122809a9f.jpg)

Ruins are perceived as romantic. They allow the disenchantment with an overmaterialistic society to be indulged and permit the viewer to both engage and disengage their imagination though a connection with the ruin and a disconnection with the intact structure. The ruin also provides a physical connection between man and nature – as the undergrowth begins to take over, so the relationship between the building and the earth is emphasised.

Figure 2.3 The interventions that complete the manor house contain a magnitude that complements the original structure.

Astley Castle is a fortifi ed manor house that dates from the 12th century but was almost entirely rebuilt in the 1500s by the Grey family. This important family played a signifi cant role in Medieval English history; Lady Jane Grey reigned for just nine days in 1553. The house was utterly devastated by fi re in the late 1970s, and had been empty and ruinous since. At the beginning of the 2010s, the ruin was acquired by the Landmark Trust. This is a charitable organisation that collects endangered historic buildings, restores them and then makes them available to all by letting them as holiday homes.

The approach that the architects Witherford Watson Mann took to the project was to recreate a sort of completeness, to restore the sense of the entire building, while still retaining the consciousness of history. This was achieved with a collection of small slightly contrasting additions that facilitate the new users, but didn’t overwhelm the building. Witherford Watson Mann explained: “We haven’t restored it, nor left it as a broken, romantic relic. We re-established a kind of wholeness, making it stable, binding it together; but we retained a feeling of incompleteness, leaving it porous, its wounds still open.” <sup>7</sup>

![](images/6a0266f2ea43cd88087e0ddef9bbc3332b83f46ccfb5b6ccfd025f9f3fb5fa6f.jpg)  
Figure 2.4 The open upper fl oor living room.

The 19th century additions, which had been particularly damaged by the neglect after fi re, were demolished. This left the earliest structures plus the later Grey additions of the 16th century, and those from the 18th century, to be carefully repaired. The subsequent architectural interventions deliberately contrast with the original building, but not to the extreme. They are subtle, complementary and are recognisably related to the original structure. They facilitate the needs of the holidaymakers who will occupy the building, but without compromising the sense of history that permeates the castle. The aim was not to make a drama of the contrast between new and old but rather to engineer a reading in which successive periods of building activity contribute to a holistic effect.

Placing the bedrooms within the thick walled spaces of the ground fl oor reinforced the appreciation of a fortifi ed castle. New brick walls provided suffi cient contrast with the original sandstone to make clear the distinction between old and new, and thus

explicitly expose the palimpsest of the situation. These new walls were constructed using a diaphragm construction to ensure compatible size with the original sandstone walls, which were up to two metres thick in places. The brick infi ll walls slip between the gaps in the walls and fi ll the empty spaces. They also provided support for the much more lightweight upper fl oor, and it is up here, in the much brighter rooms that the living spaces are positioned. Another intervention was the new wooden structure that was stretched over the walls of the 16th- and 18th century parts of the house, but instead of completely containing the spaces, it merely indicates the outline, thus creating a sort of courtyard or external reception room.

The new additions feel as if they complete the building. The deliberate subtlety of the new interventions and the lack of a massive contrast between them and the original structures certainly allows the sense of history to sit lightly within the building. There is also an awareness that that history is ongoing, that changes could be made in the future. The building could be extended, adapted, changed and that the simple robustness of this project can allow for that.

Square the Block
<table><tr><td>Location</td><td>Date</td><td>Artist</td></tr><tr><td>London, UK</td><td>2009</td><td>Richard Wilson</td></tr></table>

![](images/3c28952cbda1a45bba35a91e9615ce4f27241ba8e7e99fa6dbd75ffa9cf297a8.jpg)

Richard Wilson is one of Britain’s most respected and challenging contemporary artists. He is internationally celebrated for his interventions in architectural space which draw heavily for their inspiration on architecture, engineering and construction.

He has made some of the most memorable and arguably the best ‘outside’ works of recent years. This is opposed to public art, which Wilson thinks has connotations of something second-rate that is situated in municipal parks. All of Wilson’s work builds upon its context through surprise, incongruity, humour and a pervasive sense of unease. He questions the relationship between materials and construction, and deliberately confuses the viewer by a juxtaposition of one element against another in an unconventional manner. Probably his most memorable sculpture was Turning the Place Over. This sculptural intervention was created in Liverpool in 2008, during its tenure as City of Culture. Here Wilson cut an oval shaped section from the façade of a disused offi ce building; the piece was then attached to a pivoting rod which revolved. The cut section was then returned to the façade. The revolution was not quite true to the building, so as the sectional piece turned it appeared to fl oat free from the façade before settling back into its original position. This created a sense of surprise, relief and then recognition among the viewers. Another dramatic artwork is 20:50, a permanent installation in the Saatchi Gallery, London. A huge one metre high tank was installed in one room of the gallery and fi lled with pure black sump oil, the sort that is recycled from car engines – indeed this is where the title of the piece comes from. A slanting walkway was constructed which allows the visitor to walk into the centre of the tank, and is invited to contemplate the highly refl ective surface of the oil, the relationship between the oil and

Figure 2.5 A close up of the sculpture in situ (previous page).

the ceiling and the unnerving beauty of the repulsive material. It could be argued that Wilson is working in a tradition of changing the perception of a building by altering it, from the picturesque follies of the 18th century, through Gordon Matta-Clark’s cut buildings to Rachel Whiteread’s castings. This alteration disorientates the viewer, prompting questions about perception, understanding and commonly held views.

Square the Block is a major architectural intervention which has been installed on the corner of one of the London School of Economics’ most prominent buildings. The sculpture appears to an abstraction of the corner that both mimics and subtly subverts the existing façade. The stonework looks as if it has been jumbled and crumpled up, then reattached to the building. It deliberately upsets the perception of the building from something quietly solid and dignifi ed into something unsettling and fallible.

The corner of the building was actually originally chamfered, which allowed Wilson to construct elements that projected from it. To construct the artwork, the artist, working with engineers Eckersley O’Callaghan, created copies of a number of fi ve storey vertical slices from the neighbouring buildings. Moulds were taken from these and the resultant casting fi xed to a threedimensional aluminium truss, which was six storeys in height and approximately triangular. The pieces of the sculpture were constructed from the water-based acrylic composite Jesmonite. This was selected because it was economical to produce, light enough to be carried by the existing building structure and had a close visual match to the Portland stone cladding. The completed sculpture was assembled on the ground and the complete piece lifted into place. It was hung on the side of the building from the fi fth fl oor and tied back to the building at the second and sixth fl oors for stability.
