The sculpture makes no architectural and functional sense other than matching the cornice work and completing the corner. Its position means that it is sometimes unnoticed, overlooked. The moment of incongruity and recognition is often delayed and therefore amplifi ed. It is an intervention that both mimics and subtly subverts the existing façade of the building.

![](images/23e01e5b69d1030487f3b17577db30d72b4eb7d83364532d48ac37baf739f2bc.jpg)  
Figure 2.6 The elements of the sculpture were directly copied from the façades of the surrounding buildings.

Prada Foundation

Location Milan, Italy

Former function Gin distillery

New function Art foundation

Date 2016

Designer OMA

![](images/207e9e2b6a091fff3ed261e0a651e8ea677bd918d797e4f5c26f63ede3e98f4d.jpg)

![](images/0ae70b5345a1edd47e96ca7eb00501432b5276da7bf57ea6567e00e16a96a483.jpg)

The French anthropologist Claude Lévi-Strauss describes the bricoleur as ‘someone who works with his (or her) hands and uses devious means compared with those of a craftsman’.<sup>8</sup> Bricolage is the skill of recombining things to create something new. Thus the bricoleur puts pre-existing things together in new creative and meaningful ways.The interior architect is also profi cient at working with the existing, of using whatever is there already and making the most of it. The problem is always how to exploit and manipulate the existing building or situation in a manner that enhances the original while serving the needs of the new function.

Figure 2.8 The walls are covered with 24-carat gold leaf.

Lévi-Strauss continues:

conceived and procured for the purpose of the project. His universe of instruments is closed and the rules of his game are always to make do with “whatever is at hand,” that is to say with a set of tools and materials which is always fi nite and is also heterogeneous because what it contains bears no relation to the current project, or indeed to any particular project, but is the contingent result of all the occasions there have been to renew or enrich the stock or to maintain it with the remains of previous constructions or destructions. <sup>9</sup>

The bricoleur is adept at performing a large number of diverse tasks; but, unlike the engineer, he does not subordinate each of them to the availability of raw materials and tools

It is also an expression used to describe a collage-like approach to reconfi guring buildings. It relies on available materials, the ease of their reuse, on contingency, availability and ease of supply. This idea of collage or texture is something that Colin Rowe and Fred Koetter explored in their

seminal book, Collage City. Here they discuss how a city (and by extension the existing building or situation) is assembled from a multitude of fragments. This texture is the outcome of:

assimilation, distortion, challenge, response, imposition, superimposition, conciliation, in short a process of “bricolage” mediating and negotiating between the platonic ideal, technological progress and the pragmatic situation at hand. <sup>10</sup>

The Prada Foundation is an assemblage of different buildings, ideas, spaces, and functions. It is located in a former gin distillery from the early 20th century, in the Largo Isarco, a post-industrial area on the southern edge of Milan. The original complex included warehouses, laboratories and brewing silos, and a number of open yards. It does seem that at the beginning of the 21st century, the most common type of new art gallery is the one that is imposed upon a large abandoned industrial space. The typologies are strangely similar; art and industry both need big open spaces which allow for excellent fl exibility and a relatively neutral background. This adaptation project was indeed within a redundant industrial series of buildings, but the complex of buildings and spaces was much more complicated than is generally used; it contained variety, intricacy and difference.

OMA respected the existing approach: they carefully analysed what was there and decided that contained within the surviving structures were a number of useful conditions. They also identifi ed how the requirements of the new users could map onto these spaces. They realised that there were insuffi cient spaces for those needs and thus the collection of buildings would need remodelling and extending. The ambition of the architects was not to work

with contrast, but to create a situation where old and new worked seamlessly. This continuity was sometimes so great that the two actually merged. At times it is impossible to tell whether the area is old or new, whether a building, interior or space is part of the original structure, or a contemporary addition. The Foundation is not a preservation project and not new architecture: the two conditions which are usually kept separate, confront each other here in a state of permanent interaction, offering an ensemble of fragments that will not congeal into a single image, nor allow any part to dominate the others.

The project physically consists of seven existing buildings and three new structures. The museum contains the podium, a space for temporary exhibitions; a cinema, a multimedia auditorium; and the tower (or the Torre). This is a nine storey permanent exhibition space for displaying the Foundation’s collection and activities. The new podium building, which hosts temporary exhibitions, and the cinema were inserted into the centre of the 19,000 square metre complex. They divide the space into a series of small courtyards, some of which are level, others are sloping. The mirror-clad cinema is a standalone structure which is partially sunken underground, while the glazed podium was constructed around another building which is known as the Haunted House. This is the landmark structure in the complex. It is neither the tallest nor the most distinguished, but it is certainly the most extraordinary. To add emphasis to this older structure, OMA carefully clad the exterior of the building with 24-carat gold leaf. Only the glass of the windows was left exposed. Rem Koolhaas, the director of OMA, claimed that it was a last minute decision – an attempt to give value to this simple and mundane element.

# C-Mine Cultural Centre

Location   
Winterslag, Genk,   
Belgium

Former function Coal mine

New function Cultural centre, Theatre, Design museum, Art gallery

Date 2010

Designer 51N4E

![](images/6ddf47393b8c8fbd7a47c2215ffc6c9998cc06e1524a994066d8aeb3b9d398b4.jpg)

![](images/52613a41c23545358a9f8b8cab224ae53e2842ea27be22c2b54f89e393c8f0dc.jpg)  
Figure 2.9 The dining room is fi lled with remnants of a bygone age.

It is significant that some of the most popular tourist destinations are the reworked sites of industrial production. Our post-industrial society has a certain nostalgia for the drama of these places of production, not just as sites of scientifi c interest, but also, in this digital age, to gaze in wonder at the analogue complexity of the physical and mechanical technology.

Figure 2.10 The pit head lift tower now houses a viewing platform that provides a fantastic panorama across the surrounding fl atlands.

The C-Mine Cultural Centre is one such destination. It superimposes the light industry of heritage tourism over the obsolete industry of heavy production coal mining. This project, which was completed in 2010, reworks the substantial and demanding structures into a cultural complex containing: two differently scaled multipurpose auditoria, meeting rooms and spaces for fl exible cultural programming, and accommodation for technical support and administration. To facilitate this, the architects, 51N4E, have created a collection of quite simple but not subtle interventions which echo the language of the original complex of industrial buildings without compromising the needs of the new users.

The original buildings were fairly typical of this type of industrial structures; vast brick clad steel-framed buildings and an open steel tower set in an immense and fl at landscape of forests and slagheaps. The new additions appear to complete the buildings. Rather than retain the T-shape of the original, the architects have created extensions on either side of the leg of the T, thus creating an outwardly complete rectangular (in plan) building. This bold move which seemingly contradicts the nature of the mine buildings, is very much a contemporary approach to the remodelling of the existing building. This method does not contrast the new elements with the original structure, but attempts to complete the building, to create a new entirety, totality or whole.

This approach is also a development away from the more traditional methods of exhibiting heritage: that of placing the new adjacent to the old, and making each distinct and obvious. The new would usually contain the cultural; the old, the heritage. This contemporary approach establishes a compressed building with a substantially more spatially dynamic exchange between the original industrial buildings and the new elements of culture.

An enormous new, sturdy black steel box signals the relocated entrance, an element which seems to draw directly from the uncompromisingly sculptural qualities of the original water tanks that are still suspended on the roof. This leads to the foyer, which is situated within the former machine hall on the ground fl oor, and it is from here that the whole complex is accessed. This quite dark and gloomy lower level, which is littered with the detritus of the previous occupation, contains all the necessary equipment for the production of culture. The architects have reinforced the clutter with simple robust interventions that contain the offi ces, café, galleries, staircases and access to the new auditorium.

The fi rst fl oor, in contrast with the busyness of the ground fl oor, has little in the way of programme to create a barrier between the visitor and the building. The vast fl oor of red and white tiles has been extended to create an enormous horizontal plane that unites the old with the new within the extension of the rectangular plan. It appears to be of such capacity that the huge machinery of industrial production is somewhat stranded in this vast top-lit space. The nostalgia connected to the abandoned technology is tangible, but the sensitivity of the adaptation celebrates the beauty of the machinery, rather than revelling in its loss. Outside, the pit-head winding gear has been retained as a sculptural reminder of the previous occupation and a plate-steel maze has been installed immediately adjacent to it. These objects appear to contain the same shared robust language of the pit itself.

The cultural centre epitomises the condition that most western post-industrial societies fi nd themselves: the problem of what to do with the vast number of obsolete sites of industrial production. This occasion proved to be a very successful conversion, but it does lead to the question as to whether these sites should just be removed and forgotten about, allowed to fall into ruin – or perhaps it is important that they are always celebrated?

# Stroud Valleys Artspace

Location Gloucestershire

Former function Warehouse

Date 2007

Designer Tony Fretton Architects

![](images/cbe5242640eb97f0242dac5c9a710bd8a77e94e7bffc0c7e76c5d0eda0f4142d.jpg)  
Figure 2.11 The gated courtyard creates space that is owned by the arts centre but also accessible to the townsfolk.

The Stroud Valleys Artspace plays an important role in the artistic culture of this small town in the southwest of England, in that it promotes cooperation, artistic partnership and exploration. The arts centre encourages the local population to actively engage with the arts and culture and thus has become a focus for culture within the town. The organisation had occupied the collection of buildings for some years, and had attempted to

refurbish them themselves, however this had proved to be just too diffi cult. Tony Fretton Architects were approached to repair and reconfi gure the three existing buildings and the courtyard, to create professional quality studios and public spaces. This gives the Artspace a defi nite and signifi cant public face, while also making a strong social and urbanistic contribution to the fabric of the town. The remodelled arts centre contains a gallery, studios for sculpture, painting, and fabric printing and a project space.

Figure 2.12 Architect’s model that shows the relationship between the three buildings and the open courtyard.

Following their examination of the buildings themselves, combined with an analysis of the immediate surroundings, the architects’ strategy has been to use the courtyard as the focus around which all of the other spaces revolve. This enclosed outdoor space is surrounded by the studio and gallery spaces, this allows it to be both within and outside the tradition spaces for the production of art.

The Stroud Valleys Artspace has a certain reticence; it is defi nitely not a physical landmark within the town. This diffi dence refl ects the humble beginnings of the organisation and the need to not overwhelm the restraint and lack of pretentions of the town itself. The courtyard is relatively modest, and yet is central to the organisation and creativity of the project. It is gated, while still being accessible from the street. The work to the buildings was not extensive – repairs were made, and they were insulated and made waterproof – so the modesty of the original was continued. The architects then chose to accentuate features. These small interventions added artistic gravitas to the complex of buildings and elevated the space from the pedestrian to something that is refi ned, cultured and knowing. These discerning features included anodised aluminium doors and windows, polished concrete fl oors, and Italian porphyry external paving.

The place has always depended upon a large amount of interaction with the artist community of the town, and a signifi cant project that took place during the construction of the complex was a participatory performance event Beddin. Here members of the studio each donated a piece of the work, which was then embedded in the sub-fl oor of the excavated sculpture studio. The residue of soil, which had been removed by each artist, was catalogued and then encased in the foyer. This piece of interaction ensured that the physical and psychological ownership of the arts space was continued.The architects have approached the project with constraint and modesty, thus creating something that is fi tting of its environment, but the small highly refi ned interventions transform the project into a distinguished and urbane place for visitors and artists.

![](images/268e3f64557c99f609fd3259cb81f838bbb92129c35b8067893005b5a2f17e23.jpg)

## Insertion

## Introduction

Insertion is a very lively word: it defines that act of inserting or carefully placing something within something else. The action is deliberate, thoughtful, measured and uses the upmost care. Insertion implies a clearing away or making room, creating a space for the new element to be placed within. This is not a passive brushing or tidying up, but a much more expressive sense of unclogging, evacuating or removing unwanted detritus to make way for something important.

It is interesting that another expression for creation of clear space is making room. Making room for another activity, for another use; making room for another room. Heidegger describes this clearing away as ‘admitting and arranging’. So when the removal of the rubbish is complete, something new is allowed to enter. Thus there are two distinct processes, one subtractive, the other additive. Both are dynamic, proactive and self-conscious.

The new element, that is the insertion itself, is something of an intruder; it could perhaps be regarded as an infiltrator, invader or unwanted guest. Or possibly the insertion acts as a saviour, a facilitator, an emancipator. This additional element is self-contained, it is complete and independent, and although directly reliant upon its host it has an individual identity. It is definite and distinct, and thus separate from the original.

When an existing building is remodelled, the strategy of insertion denotes a new element that has been placed into, around or alongside an existing building. There is a discrete and defined relationship between the new and the old, because the new element is built to fit exactly within or around the original. The dimensions of insertion are based upon the parameters of the structure that it has established the relationship with. The space that has been created by removing the unwanted accumulation of living is exact. It has definite dimensions and three-dimensional articulation, which the insertion has to adhere to. The insertion is built to fit.

An architectural insertion is usually a single complex element with a specific function. This may be made of a number of parts that have been collected together, such as a circulation system, an auditorium or a meeting pod. The necessity for the insertion to be distinct means that there is generally a name for the element, which reinforces the sense of separateness or difference. It is often an indispensable element within the organisation of the building, and the users will benefit from its insertion, which will facilitate their needs. This complex object can sometimes contrast strongly with the existing building, and make quite obvious the intent of the insertion. It can of course work with the language of the host building, but invariably the new is easily distinguished from the old. It can be a dominant element within the space, or it may be subservient to the greater picture.

KHBT Architects have connected two small apartments in London with a long sculptural spine. This insertion links two tiny flats which were once divided by a communal staircase. It is an element that facilitates the needs of the residents of the flat by creating a connection that unifies the once separate and distinct rooms. The insertion is long narrow strip of dark brown, ornamentally grained, walnut wood. It is a meandering ribbon that forms a dominant and useful element within almost every area of the apartment. Thus it is the work-surface within the kitchen, the actual staircase, the covering within the circulation area and it also supports the tub in the bathroom. This strip, which is constructed to fit exactly into the interior spaces, is absolutely straight, but not flat. It rises and falls with the undulations of the original building. The insertion unifies the two different spaces, linking the once

separate flats and uniting them into a single usable entity.

Figure 2.13 A single polished walnut spine designed by KHBT Architects unites the disparate spaces within an apartment in London. The insertion is at once the staircase and the kitchen.  
![](images/76efc068d9cc84d58a7c10d974dd830ec8feb77994aa4025238df5a6de6a2d5c.jpg)

# All Souls, Bolton

Location Lancashire, UK

Former function Church New function Community centre

Original Designer Paley & Austin

Original date 1881   
Remodelled 2015

Designer OMI Architects

![](images/f38d66b18a9b2c0100504878b8401070cc92a24e3f7d48ece10ca90b77151b29.jpg)

Figure 2.14 The street pattern surrounding the building is recognised through the placement of the interior paths and routes.

The design of the elements of an insertion always generates the tricky problem of the language that the designer should adopt. The decision about whether to deliberately contrast the new with the old, that is to create distinction and difference, or to purposefully generate form and detail that has a similarity or a direct connection and continuity with the host building has to be considered. This second option does not necessarily mean resorting to pastiche, but can entail using the clues about form, rhythm, materials or scale that have been

generated by the existing building to inform the design of the new elements.

This is the problem that the architects OMI were faced with when they were asked to convert a disused church into a community centre. The adaptation of a religious building can pose delicate questions. The architect or designer has to signal the new use, while also paying respect to the original building. A church is often a very dominant building in an area, and frequently has a dramatic or symbolic

interior. The architect or designer has to fi nd a way for the conversion to tacitly connect with the new occupiers without overpowering the original intentions of the structure. This is especially true if the structure is in a very ethnically mixed area, and overt Christian imagery may be inappropriate.

The approach that OMI took to the conversion of the Grade II listed Gothic Revival church by Paley and Austin was very defi nitely one of generating variation and difference. They chose to not only contrast the language of the new elements of the community centre, but also to subtly break the ordering geometry of the interior.

Bolton, a large town in the northwest of England, was once a highly affl uent area in the heart of the cotton-weaving district. It is close to Manchester and was part of the huge industrial swath that crossed this part of the country. As with most post-industrial towns and cities, the population has changed, and thus the church has lost its congregation.

Figure 2.15 The new use for the building is signalled through the use of strongly contrasting elements.

All Souls is an immense church capable of holding a congregation of 800, and, at 16 metres wide, has the largest single span nave of any parish church in England. It was built at the junction of two terraced streets, and although the local street confi guration has changed over the church’s 130-year existence, it is still a dominant landmark in the area. OMI continued this streetscape into the building by constructing a series of pods or small structures that actively celebrate the continuation of the road through the front door and into the nave. This was achieved by placing the asymmetrical objects at either side of the great hall, thus leaving a path or visual route through the centre of the building. This echoes the organisation of the original church, but the irregular shape of

the pods and the vertical circulation breaks this order. Only the bridges interrupt the length of the church and the views of the immense timbered ceiling.

Major historic repair works were carried out to the church, including the replacement of the Westmoreland slate roofs, brickwork and stone repair and replacement of most of the leaded glass windows to the nave. The insertion has been pulled back from the carefully conserved fabric of the building. This has established a new relationship between the building and the visitor. Where once the building had to be observed only from ground level, the occupiers can now appreciate the intricate beauty from a much more elevated position from within the new pods. The architects were presented with a very diffi cult problem with the actual construction of the new structures. All of the materials and the machinery of construction had to be brought in and out through the church’s two metre wide west doorway. This limitation resulted in the use of a prefabricated structurally insulated panel

![](images/de9ad3eec39bd8c9da1a4dcb3be2b5c6671d69f95fd591f5ed7b2e5ea435805d.jpg)

![](images/2f108da0b815828704232a7e3fc6cf190168661a5a8920dcdb093319690c64be.jpg)  
Figure 2.16 The pods fl oat above the fl oor, leaving the ground level for communal activities.  
building. This insertion has given the local residents a building suited to their community needs without compromising the integrity of the original structure.

construction, which also removed the need for heavy lifting equipment during construction.

The architects have created a free-standing insertion that deliberately contrasts with the original building. The language of the new elements is uncompromisingly contemporary, and although the position of these structures is somewhat reminiscent of the previous organisation, it really refl ects the relationship with the street and therefore the population who live immediately around the building. The old order has also been interrupted by the three-dimensional nature of the structures, which creates a new, much less reverent relationship with the

# Sensing Spaces

Location   
Royal Academy,   
London

Date 2014

Designer Grafton Architects

![](images/77c05a3e193889a9e4156c769b3914d4b87ece65266b86c149cd9b59a348aac7.jpg)

Figure 2.17 Natural daylight is fi ltered through visually heavy boxes hung from the ceiling.

Sensing Spaces: Architecture Reimagined invited seven architectural practices to create site-specifi c installations that were inserted into the main galleries and front courtyard of the Royal Academy in London.

The exhibition was designed to explore the most fundamental elements of architectural space. The curator expected the architects to radically transform the apparently dominant character of the classically

planned and detailed interiors.

The Irish architectural practice Grafton Architects was given two adjoining but contrasting rooms and here they created two dramatically different, but contrasting insertions. Yvonne Farrell and Shelley McNamara, the directors of the practice, worked very much with the conditions that existed within those spaces. It was not a question of simply installing some sort of artefact, object or structure into the interior; they wanted to change or heighten the nature or character of the room itself. In order that the gallery and the insertion became the same thing, it wasn’t the case that the insertions could be moved, transported, or transferred to another gallery. The insertions could only exist in this particular position. They were completely specifi c to that site.

McNamara and Farrell identifi ed the sense of pleasure of moving from the darkness to the light. One of the rooms gave the notion of weight and moodiness, while the other was fi lled with light. Light is something that has long fascinated the architects. They deliberately emphasise the quality of light within all of their buildings, they consider it as another material with the palette.

With this in mind, the roofl ight was the site; this was the place that provided the difference in atmosphere. Large wooden framed structures were hung from this ceiling which controlled the space and, by extension, the nature and character of the light. The fl oor of both galleries was left completely clear, so the visitor could move unencumbered through the two rooms as the exhibition was created by the ceilinghung installation.

The fi rst room was bright and in here huge white fi ns were hung to refl ect the brightness onto the white walls. The length of the space was accentuated with a long bench with different degrees of hardness to help the visitor pause and contemplate the ways in which light and substance change each other.

The second room was considerably more gloomy and foreboding. Huge textured orthogonal box-like structures were hung in the space. These appeared to defy gravity; they contained great weight and felt not only melancholy, but also heavy. This created the conundrum of the appearance of heaviness in what were obviously lightweight structures hanging from the ceiling of the gallery. The huge suspended box-like frames were organised on the diagonal, in opposition to the classical and ordered organisation of the Royal Academy buildings. This build-up of the elements broke the linear axial views and movement with the space. The oblique angle refl ected the manner in which the visitor negotiated the space, and encouraged the eye to also cross the gallery in a particular manner. The architects also created a connection between the spaces through a new doorframe. Concealed lighting moved gently across the ceilings to subtly change the conditions of the rooms at different times and encourage the visitor to stop for a while and contemplate these strange calm spaces.

Grafton used the language of architecture to create insertions that discussed surface, structure, weight, lightness and light. One gallery explored the revelatory character of light and lightness. The other discussed the ominous nature of dark and heaviness: thus the two rooms complemented each other in their opposition.

Location   
Tower Hamlets,   
London

Empire Studio Balcony

Former function Church

New function Art space and recording studio

Date 2011

Designer Adam Khan Architects

![](images/36277e4b1617e36b4d009d08337e862e79a7361c8885400c7ee3edbf2790745f.jpg)  
Figure 2.18 The balcony has the qualities of a rampant lion controlling and organising the far end of the studio.

A sense of continuity within any environment can provide a link between the past and the future. A building may exist within the collective memory of a local population, and its reuse emphasises resilience and permanence in times of great change. This connection between the building, the urban pattern and the local residents can contribute towards a viable and sustainable future for an area, which has the opportunity to be both creative and cultured.

Adam Khan Architects has designed a new timber staircase and mezzanine platform

for an arts space and recording studio in Tower Hamlets, East London. The building is a former church, which dates from the beginning of the 20th century, although it is some time since it was used as such; indeed, after the Second World War it was used as a shoe factory. The studio is situated in the enormous open fi rst fl oor space, which runs through the complete length of the building.

The architects claim a number of points of inspiration, the fi rst is a direct link to a detail of the building itself: the Empire’s insignia of a rampant lion. The leonine shape

informs the balcony, which is almost animalistic in shape. Four legs support the structure, with a head wrapping around an existing column, and the dog-leg staircase forming a tail.

The second inspiration for the project was a most poetic precedent: the studio of Gustave Moreau in Paris. Khan describes this as containing the fabulous winding stair: a carefully choreographed promenade for showing off and schmoozing. The circular staircase, which links the open second fl oor with the studio above, revolves twice, thus allowing those below to appreciate the fi gures of those descending, while also ensuring that those using the staircase are fully aware of the occupants of the room. This, claims Khan, reminded him of the complaint made by Oscar Wilde about gallery openings: so much art you couldn’t see the people properly!

The balcony is carefully constructed to fi t exactly into the space at the far end of the studio space, with the four legs at the front boxed for strength as well as utility. While the rear of the lion is securely attached to the wall, avoiding the important acoustic barrier. The whole thing is constructed from 19mm larch triboard, which is cheap, easy to work and available in huge sizes. The board was imported from Austria and machined in Sheffi eld in the north of England before being transported to the site. This made it inexpensive and simple to construct; indeed the total construction budget was just £15,000.

![](images/1d3df567087c4c0ae1844e0f6a80488eb6c86bd22eb0d486b05edaf0ef7072fc.jpg)  
Figure 2.19 The deliberate turn in the staircase allows the user to observe the room before stepping into it.

The architects have created an economical yet dynamic insertion, which promotes interaction between the different areas of the building without compromising the necessary mass of the space. The balcony houses the technical equipment, but also creates a romantic promenade within the space.

# Roma Tre University

Location Rome, Italy

Former function Abattoir

Original date 1891   
Remodelled 2016

Original Designer Gioacchino Ersoch

Designer Stefano Cordeschi

![](images/19256d0182f221084a95318a929a98d3bba20606f83582bec008d6a69d97e299.jpg)

Figure 2.20 The lecture theatre from the stage towards the audience.

When it opened in Rome in 1891, the Testaccio Slaughterhouse by Gioacchino Ersoch was regarded as the most advanced in Europe. The collection of orthogonal brick and cast iron buildings were organised in a then innovative and rational manner that paid great heed to cleanliness, sanitation and hygiene. Just in case there was some doubt about the purpose of the complex, above the pediment of the main building (mattatoio), an extraordinary sculpture of a winged hero slaughtering an ox was placed. The enormous abattoir was designed to

accommodate an eightfold increase in the population of the city and also to provide meat for much of central Italy. The workers at the stockyard were provided with free meat, but of course only the bits that couldn’t be sold, so they became creative with the rejected heads, tails, hearts, lungs, and intestines. This tradition lives on and the restaurants in the area have become known for the presence of offal on the menu. The original buildings were constructed at a time of transition, and although an important example of industrial archaeology, they also demonstrate the

move from classical to rational or more modern architecture. The slaughterhouse was moved to an eastern suburb of the city in 1975, when the delivery trucks could no longer navigate the inadequate, constricted and busy roads of central Rome.

After many years of discussion about the best use for the vast complex of buildings, structures, courtyards and other structures, an arts-based function was agreed upon. The area now hosts the large multifunctional collective: the City of Alternative Economy, an outpost of the Museum of Contemporary Art, a centre for fairtrade sales, spaces for multimedia projects and exhibitions, and the university’s architecture department. The vast spaces have been renovated with sensitivity to the past function, and care has been taken to incorporate many of the original features, such as the meat hooks hanging from the elevated track that was used to transport the cattle, and the animal pens outside.

Stefano Cordeschi, in collaboration with the School of Architecture, designed the new facilities for the university. The majority of

Figure 2.21 The folded articulated ribbon is made from cherry wood with black seats and blood red painted reveals.  
![](images/5d0d0f06b89707485ca3ceb904e90118b75f46168e589bd4f0a619cc3b6b6f7a.jpg)

the spaces are sympathetically fi tted into the existing structure, which have been kept deliberately clean and minimal. The main lecture theatre is the exception to this: it stands as an astonishing statement of intent among the highly diplomatic contrasting rooms and spaces.

A vast folded timber structure has been inserted into the main hall. This dynamic and responsive construction is clearly making a contemporary statement that is in direct contrast to the carefully conserved buildings around it. The containing structure seats over 250 students and is a focal point within the department. The original building is clearly visible behind the enfolding lecture theatre, and this distancing does enhance the dynamic quality of the insertion. It is constructed from planks of warm and even cherry wood. Timber pyramids hang from the ceiling; these are designed to aid the acoustic quality of the space. They act to distribute the sound and thus reduce echo and the build-up of unwanted noise. This articulated ceiling also contains the lighting and the audio-visual equipment. The raked seating is industrial black, a nod towards the original buildings, while blood-red paint picks out particular surfaces. The lecture hall is a well-managed insertion. It is a dramatic moment of theatre within this sensitive and sympathetically renovated collection of industrial buildings.

# Porta Nuova Tower

Location Arsenale, Venice

Former function Naval building

New function Exhibition and conference centre

Date 2011

Designer MAP studio

![](images/a88579fb4c331054da5d0173facba3f941d348a85ee8f11998000dfcce7aca4e.jpg)

The concept of an echo or ghost of an original element can be the generator for the adaptation of a building, especially if the original object is invested with particular meaning. The architect can choose to engage with the concept of replication, reproduction or imitation. The copying of simple objects can reveal ambiguity, it can shift their identity and introduce an element of uncertainty. The act of duplication is invested with shared meanings, in that the memory of an object is often a distortion of the original.

Figure 2.22 The tower from the edge of the canal.

This approach of creating a copy, a simulacrum or impression of an existing object is the methodology that MAP studio took to the remodelling of the Porta Nuova Tower in the Arsenale area of Venice.

The Arsenale was the heart of the Venetian naval industry from the 12th century and is a great symbol of the ancient maritime power. It was once the supreme shipyard of the world and held the most formidable navy. Jan Morris, in her book Venice, describes the war-readiness of the Venetian Navy. “As soon as the alarm was sounded the first warships were towed out of the Arsenale and an almost Fordian succession of activities were rapidly completed to make the boat ready for sea. First supplies, then cordage, small weapons, oars, and fi nally at the entrance to the Arsenale, the crew leapt aboard, and thus the galley sailed away ready for action. The Porta Nuova Tower, which stands at the lagoon of the north side of the dock, was built at the beginning of the 19th century, and was constructed as a building to fi t the masts onto the ship.”<sup>11</sup>

The Arsenale area is no longer used by the military, and this project is part of a wholesale remodelling of the workshops and warehouses. The incredibly solid and necessarily strong stone building was divided into three, both horizontally and vertically. The interior is tripartite and contains three full height spaces, while the exterior is classically a tower, with a base, shaft and top. The project recognises and interprets this; the new elements are as visually strong and robust as the original building, although notably of the 21st century. The architects have inserted an exhibition area, conference space, laboratories and library into the building. The materials used are exposed concrete, fi bre-cement fl oor panels, waxed COR-TEN steel. These contain the same magnitude of expression, but contrast quite simply with the existing stone and timber.

Figure 2.23 Section   
through the tower   
showing the   
relationship between   
the thick robust   
structure of the   
building compared with the lightweight inserted staircase.

The waxed COR-TEN staircase is a most magnifi cent single element within the space. It sculpturally rises through the whole open interior space and is a dramatic insertion in the heart of the building. It doesn’t touch the edges of the walls, but is specifi cally designed to fi t tightly within the existing alcove. It is an extraordinary echo or impression of the still existing but structurally unsafe original timber staircase, which sits immediately next to it. The new imitates the old.

The solidity of the staircase, the sense of its completeness, and the repetition of the structure echoes the language of the Minimalist sculptures or works of the

mid-20th century. A constant play between the experience of perception and the understanding of the insertion has been created. The staircase contains the qualities of wholeness produced by the simple geometric forms, thus there is a comprehension of the complete object. The staircase is not perceived as a collection of risers, treads, and landings but as a simple geometrical undivided object. MAP studio have created a timeless insertion, something that is both sculptural and useful, dramatic and solid, and yet speaks of the future for the building and the area.

![](images/3fffb36361f8ee926730b8fa0c21e5a6440abd9676111f0ff241f31dd385cd1a.jpg)

![](images/ff1a28f3c7a009489b9de1eca1946bb2c42d9d98a7687548c3fe7ddde8512d4c.jpg)

Figure 2.24 The new steel staircase is an echo of the original timber one.  
![](images/2d2f38f9430ad740c03752bf4690114fa0751413badee3fba9030c87ceeb8343.jpg)

## Installation

## Introduction

To install something is to place it in position and for it to become established in that location. The exact position of the object is composed, in that it is deliberately situated in relation to the things around it, whether these are other objects, or the surrounding context. It is a particularly useful remodelling strategy. The architect can curate the interior; they can select, organise and present precise and specific objects or works in a particular manner. They can design structures that are self-contained and completely self-supporting. They can create an installation that is demountable, reversible, and exchangeable. Because an installation is maybe cheap, it can be investigative, clever and therefore more experimental. It can ask questions that maybe more permanent architect can’t, and it can be magical, controversial, awkward, provocative, emotional and wonderful.

The term Installation Art first came into use in the 1960s, and was used to describe the way in which an exhibition was laid out. Consideration was given to adjustments of scale, appropriateness, placement, style, materials etc. It was particularly popular with Minimalist artists who were acutely aware of space in which they were exhibiting, and the ways in which their work related to it. Their work was often sensitively arranged, or installed, to create a dialogue with its surroundings, so viewers could take in both the work and its environment as one overall, immersive display. An installation was and still is a total, unified experience.

The relationship between the installed object and its immediate context is always somewhat tenuous. The installation is not necessarily permanent and therefore it often has a transient or fleeting quality. It may be deliberately unsettling within the space, too big, too bright, the wrong

shape, and this ephemerality creates tension: tension between the objects and the context, and tension between the individual objects themselves. This anxiety can heighten awareness of the objects or elements, and also of the container or situation. The fugitive qualities accentuate the impermanent relationship.

Installation is reversible. The objects, works or elements can be removed and the host space will revert to its previous incarnation. The carefully conceived and placed elements will have no lasting impact upon the building; they will not have caused any damage. Within a remodelled building, the strategy of installation is often used when the host building is particularly precious, when it is of great conservation value, or is exceptionally fragile.

In 2007, Merkx+Girod architects created a contemporary bookshop in a former Dominican church in Maastrict, in the very south of the Netherlands.

The Gothic church, which was consecrated in 1294, had been considerably changed over the years, but still retained its huge somewhat romantic quality. In order to preserve the character of the church, while also creating the necessary commercial space, the architects installed a multistorey, black steel structure to contain and display the books. This was positioned off-centre in the length of the nave, thus creating usable shop space within the resultant larger space and a reading area within the smaller.

The café, which is situated in the apse, contains a huge cruciform table, which is lit by an enormous suspended corona, or huge circular light fitting. This is a somewhat audacious act, and had the installation been perceived as a permanent feature, then it might have been badly

Figure 2.25 The bookshop allows the users to develop a relationship with the building that would not previously have been possible.

received. But the ephemerality of the design allows for this impertinence.

The church was fully restored, and the utilities and other equipment have been housed in the extended cellar. The installation did not affect the integrity of the building; should the need ever arise, all the elements of the bookshop can be removed. The architects have created an extremely dramatic, atmospheric and completely reversible installation.

![](images/02cf26736442c9f61ba7195a914d4831a8425732af133ec8ea152f23e6afb58d.jpg)

## Kiang

Location Vienna, Austria

Function Café

Date 1985

Designer Helmut Richter

![](images/de0985fa9ad773bbb4ea5e44924b78a4e06b92aec5a870cee7c9ac41a54f3a4d.jpg)  
Figure 2.26 The spatial progression through the restaurant is controlled through the placement of a series of carefully designed objects.

In Vienna there exists a collective certainty about the spatial connection between eating and communication. There is a pronounced tradition of the coffee house. It is somewhere that is much greater than a place to consume coffee, it is part of the cultural fabric of the city. Indeed the Viennese coffee house or Wiener Kaffeehaus is so important to the culture of the place that it is listed by UNESCO as part of the Intangible Cultural Heritage of Austria. The Viennese have a name for the spatial harmony that exists between eating and communicating: Gemütlichkeit. This is one of those words that refers to sensation, and so is somewhat difficult to translate, but really means that feeling of cosiness, contentedness, comfort and relaxation that is experienced when in a comfortable chair, drinking good coffee surrounded by friends. In Vienna a system of semipublic hospitality has evolved, one in which the acts of eating and drinking, seeing and being seen are overlaid within the coffee house.

The Kiang Restaurant has been inserted into the ground fl oor of an apartment block in the city centre. A series of independent skins or linings divorce the restaurant from the building and thus these, rather than the containing masonry, form the space. This use of cladding to create space is very much in the tradition of the Viennese architects such as Otto Wagner or Adolf Loos, but Kiang lacks the formal geometry of these earlier buildings. Kiang is at once both much more relaxed and more dynamic. The linings act to create a space that is at odds with the original building. The architect Helmut Richter uses a diagonal and a curve to instil a slight sense of unease within the traditional confi nes of a Viennese café.

The fi rst skin is made of slightly obscured glass and placed in front of the façade of the building, deliberately upsetting the conventional relationship between the exterior and the interior of the building. This new plane contains full-height sliding windows with fl uorescent lighting tubes placed immediately above each opening. This semi-transparent external skin signals the new use, while also providing additional entrances and seating alcoves when the weather permits.

Figure 2.27 The surface-mounted proud window frames subvert the usual organisation of the building.

The stainless steel counter has been swivelled away from the orthogonal geometry of the interior walls. This not only provides suffi cient space for the small kitchen, but also directs the visitor towards the rear of the café and the enormous free-standing, bright red, stretched plastic screen. This curving stretched plane of lorry tarpaulin sits slightly forward of the end wall of the interior space and defi nes the room while also discretely shielding the route to the WCs. Another much smaller perforated steel screen, which is situated at the point where the interior has a single step, helps to control the extent of the eating area and acts as a coat stand. The Wiener Kaffeehaus has been dragged into the modern era. Richter has created an interior that subverts and updates the established nature of the traditional coffee house through the installation of a series of dynamic elements made of contemporary materials, deliberately breaking the conventional rules of the existing building and by extension the tradition of the coffee house.

![](images/8d6749cd8d0d69da7b43fc57213d13e863f7926c14ddfabfc1c9c51a9832e524.jpg)

## Harbour Bath

Location Copenhagen, Denmark

Designer Bjarke Ingels Group (BIG)

Date 2003

![](images/5efa369877df2f10355e3409f00dbce2cf9db6d7e99c0e192bdd5b564ead85d1.jpg)

The revitalised front is a common feature of many post-industrial port cities. These landscapes of production have become obsolete, and their adaptation and reuse has become an important element in the revitalisation of the urban environment.

Figure 2.28 The diving board and the lifeguard’s seat are sculptural objects within the horizontality of the harbour.

For example, Liverpool in the UK has transformed a once bustling port into a thriving cultural area. Museums, galleries, concert halls and shopping now fi ll the magnifi cent port buildings and the harbour frontage.

Of course, this wasn’t achieved without considerable anguish; there were decades of empty and forgotten despondency before the metamorphosis was instigated.

The harbour in Copenhagen has been transformed from an industrial port and traffi c junction into a cultural and social centre for the city; a development which is an integral strategy for regeneration of public life along the water’s edge. The harbour was active until the 1980s, and had always acted as a barrier between the residential area and the water. It was horribly polluted, not just from the waste of the industrial area, but also from the effl uent of the city, which at one time drained directly into it. The city has invested heavily in sewage treatment to improve the quality of the water, and also in the transformation of the water front area into a park: a facility for local people to use.

The idea of leisure has evolved in the 21st century: from self-improvement, through tourism to therapeutic recreation. Leisure is reinvented by succeeding cultures and by the values and preoccupations of each. The link between leisure and health is very much a contemporary preoccupation, and so it is common for people to want to exercise during their non-working hours. But this too has also progressed; the sterile environment of the gym is no longer attractive to many people who want to exercise outside. They want to take advantage of their own environment, to be able to enjoy themselves in a sympathetic landscape, conducting different activities while still being able to appreciate the benefi ts of fresh clean air.

Figure 2.29 The baths are part of the revitalisation of the whole quayside, and as such belong to both the land and the sea.

The public transport strategy for the Copenhagen Harbour Front project transforms the water into a virtual street, with continuous pedestrian and bicycle circulation along both sides of the harbour. To further add interest and experience, a series of independent interventions were strategically placed along the promenade. The most dramatic of these is the Harbour Baths designed by BIG. This is a contained timber platform or terrain on which to play and to rest. The wooden frame contains fi ve seawater pools of various types, including a wading pool for children and people with mobility challenges, a 50-metre lap pool, and a diving pool. The most dramatic and extraordinary architectural feature is the stair-like structure that serves as a diving platform and performance area. The complete baths complex is movable, so can be transported along the quayside when necessary. It is also sustainable; the timber boards were grown locally, so that replacement timbers are readily available and easily sourced.

The adaptation of the rotting and obsolete landscape into a vibrant playground makes it possible for the citizens of Copenhagen to go for a swim in the middle of the city. The Harbour Bath has created a new type of public space for the city, it offers an urban harbour landscape with dry-docks, piers, boat ramps, cliffs, playgrounds and pontoons. Copenhagen has a direct relationship with the water, it is said that no citizen is more than 15 minutes from the sea. This intervention exploits this to create a progressive contemporary playground for the 21st century population. By cleaning the water in the former harbour area and building fl oating interventions, an unused space of the city is transformed into a space of recreation.

![](images/ff2dc48be8b8961c3cfccc767149160c38e76183e0269700bb9264a9991b5199.jpg)

## World

Location BBC, London

Function Public installation

Date 2015

Designer Mark Pimlott

![](images/214b42a2ccff0f8a9fd6150c2836964f66dc774b878c99b6e97193c18dae000c.jpg)  
Figure 2.30 The names are grouped together by personal association rather than location.

The term public art refers to art that is in the public realm, regardless of whether it is situated on public or private property, or whether it has been purchased with public or private money. Generally public art is commissioned to occupy a specifi c site, and of course there are many examples of monument, memorials and civic statues that inhabit important civic locations within the urban environment. During the latter half of the 20th century, when art began to be removed from the rarefi ed atmosphere of the gallery and placed within the public arena, the nature of public art began to change. It could be transitory, in the form of performances, dance, theatre, poetry, graffi ti, posters and installations. Public art could also be subversive, antagonistic and political. The nature and form of permanent public art also changed: traditional statues and sculptures are now generally avoided while more abstract notions of art are employed. A public installation can represent the ethos of a company or organisation, in that it can translate and make solid the principles or ideas that the organisation was built upon. A public installation can communicate the character of those that commissioned the piece in a positive and creative manner.

World is a pavement artwork created by Canadian-born artist and architect Mark Pimlott. It is a permanent installation at BBC’s Broadcasting House in London, and expresses the global dimension of the

BBC, whose motto is ‘Nation shall speak peace unto nation’. The artwork has been installed in the U-shaped public space between the buildings and acts to connect them to each other, to the streets of London, and to the world beyond.

World refl ects the global dimension of the BBC and consists of 750 stone fl ags that have been inscribed with the names of places from around the globe. These are connected together with elegant steel rods that act as lines of latitude and longitude. This ‘fragment’ of a much larger globe is enhanced with inset lights that can be imagined as constellations of stars or cities, and speakers, which are set into the surface of the installation and each of which whisperingly transmits live world service broadcasts in different languages.

There is no global accuracy to the positioning of the names; rather they are grouped together in the hope that lateral associations will be made, that personal connections will occur and accidental images will emerge as visitors stroll over the pavement towards the entrance of the building. The selection of names was informed by the artist’s own experience, memory and knowledge. Thus places were selected because they represented something to him, as well as having a wider bearing within the wider public consciousness.

![](images/00ba8e3659df46a12d79bb6d7818fa3d486f5c205afeaf88897f532dc9f78e15.jpg)  
Figure 2.31 ‘World’ is an installation that fi lls the courtyard of the BBC with murmurs, obscure connections and dreams of distant lands.

So for example, ‘Coney Island’ is featured in a little cluster of other islands, some remote, others famous. The notion of the island is what connects these names together: just the word island. Pimlott revealed that “Coney Island appeared because of its evocation of ‘everyman’ relaxation, Luna Park, photographs of the great hordes on the beach made by Weegee, the hot dog eating competition, a particular conception of summer. I visited only once, many years ago, a rather melancholic affair, not uncommon to all seaside resorts”. <sup>11</sup>

World is a strangely beautiful and quite emotional installation. It expresses the importance of the organisation it represents, not by bravely shouting or exclaiming its importance, but through whispers, shadows, engraving and association.

## Phantom Restaurant

Location Paris, France

Former function Opera House

Original date 1875

Remodelled 2011

Designer Charles Garnier / Odile Decq

![](images/21e7faf0c2ae4d078110bd0511ae1037be273b95a0c6d11f3ad4df77589c15e0.jpg)

Figure 2.32 The relationship between the classical building and the curvaceous mezzanine is at its greatest in the centre of the space, where the great keystone ponderously hangs over the customers.

The Palais Garnier is perhaps the most recognisable opera house in the world, not only for the opulence of the architecture and the magnificence of the setting, but probably due to its use as the setting for Gaston Leroux’s 1910 novel The Phantom of the Opera. The building itself is highly decorated, sumptuous and classical. It is designed in what is described as the Second-Empire Beaux-Arts style, which was quite prevalent in France towards the end of the 19th century. The building is rather excessive and over exaggerated, the sculptures are slightly overscaled, and thus the building appears to be somewhat larger than life. The interior is equally lavish; it is richly Baroque with velvet, gold leaf, chandeliers, cherubs and nymphs. Leroux’s Gothic love story of the Phantom is said to have been inspired by the dramatic qualities of the building, plus the rumours of the never-ending fl ow of water that continually bubbled onto the ground that had been cleared for the building. This great lake is said to still exist, despite great efforts to drain it, beneath the building.

Garnier had originally planned to install a restaurant in the opera house, but given the already enormous fi nancial outlay, this was abandoned. The idea was resurrected as a competition in the early 2000s. Given the historical importance of the building, the design restrictions were quite extreme: the restaurant was to be reversible, that is it had to have the ability to be completely installed with the possibility of later removal, without causing any damage to the building. All of the elements of the design had to integrated into the site without touching the original structure.

The restaurant is situated in a once open pavilion that is slightly separate to the opera house. It was previously known as the ‘Subscriber’s Pavilion’ and was used to shelter the carriages of theatregoers as they arrived and departed. The restaurant is formed by the installation of a voluptuous undulating white mezzanine that fl oats under the ceiling of the pavilion. It is like a cloud formation that slips between the existing elements of the room without touching them. This curvaceous structure has a geometry of its own, which actively works against the strict axial symmetry of the original building. This accentuates both, especially at the point on the mezzanine immediately below the hanging keystone, and allows for an exaggerated relationship between the strength of the masonry

Figure 2.33 The independent undulating balcony defi nes its own order within the space.

Great holes in the balcony allow for surreptitious views of the other visitors.

![](images/ff4bc66aa3cf26970173e3aa84a0decc1ecb68bdae09a7fea2cde80b5122d30c.jpg)

building and the ephemeral moulded plaster body of the new structure. The white of this hull is lined with bright red upholstery, creating a clean, simple and dramatic installation within the space. The detailing is minimal, the junctions between the materials are not elaborated, and the fi nishes are smooth and unadorned.

The architects have installed an undulating glass wall at the edge of the building to contain this space. This allows a clear view of the dramatic new restaurant and also for natural light to fl ood into the space. The structural glass is simply held in place with a single strip of bent steel that runs along the arched curve of the ceiling. Odile Decq has created an interior that is of equal magnitude and excitement to the original building. It contains the same drama and delight of the Palais Garnier but uses the contemporary stripped down, minimal detail design of the 21st century. Charles Garnier now has a restaurant worthy of the opera house.

![](images/f3593a171a9e0786e99ced24649ac10c0549771d6351973b793fdde97b05f155.jpg)

## DESSA Architectural Gallery

Location Ljubljana, Slovenia

Former function Retail

New function Gallery

Date 1985

Designer Boris Podrecca

![](images/b7883840df773a30168a7df969f4a6d3d969df6c782030a15c5711a572d19308.jpg)

Figure 2.35 The tiny and painstakingly designed timber library is carefully fi tted into the circulation area.

Within much Modernist architecture, the interior was merely the product of the exterior walls. The position of the openings, the ingress of light and the shape of the rooms were completely dictated by the

strict requirements of the external envelope. The pursuit of transparency within much of contemporary architecture has taken this reduction in the importance placed upon the integrity of the interior

even further. It means that the division between the inside and the outside of the building all but disappeared. The interior is a continuation of the landscape, with just the structural columns acting as almost insignifi cant interruptions. The theoretician, Fred Scott, describes this as the interior having escaped from the building.

In direct opposition to this, the interior of a building can be vastly different to the exterior. It can offer distinction and contrast, and have a defi nite and particular identity. The remodelled building offers an opportunity for the new to be individually distinct, to be complete. It will always have a relationship with the container in which it is placed, but the interior can also have an identity of its own.

The DESSA Architectural Gallery is located in the old Jewish quarter of Ljubljana in Slovenia. The site had already undergone a number of transformations before its current use. It was once a synagogue, but in the 16th century, after Caesar Maximilian deported the Jews from the Carniola region, it was converted into a Catholic Chapel. This was demolished in the 19th century to make way for an apartment block with retail at ground level. Even that had several changes; a restaurant in the 1930s, a music school following the Second World War and after that a workshop.

Figure 2.36 The new cladding for the wall has been pulled slightly away from the original walls of the space to create a perfectly proportioned room.

The minute and beautifully detailed gallery is composed of just four rooms: a library, a reading room, an offi ce and a meeting room. The approach that the architect took was to clad each of the rooms with a different material. This cladding, or lining, was placed slightly in front of the original walls, leaving a small gap all of the way round. This device allowed the rooms to become geometrically pure. The new walls were positioned so that the resultant interior space was exact. Thus, the irregularly shaped gallery became an impeccable rectangle, the offi ce plan was based on six squares, and the tiny reading room was a perfect circle. Each of the rooms is devoted to a different architectural material which refl ects the activity that happens within the space. The gallery marks the moment of arrival within the building, so it is lined with stone, the library is articulated in warm timber, effi cient plastic for the offi ce and the acoustic deadening qualities of textile line the meeting room. The fl uctuations in the spaces behind the new walls allowed the architect Boris Podrecca to insert storage, such as cupboards and shelves.

This use of cladding to identify space, and the extreme level of attention to detail places Podrecca in the great tradition of Mitteleuropean Architecture. Semper, Loos, Plečnik and Wagner are all obvious precursors or forerunners of this approach. Much of Podrecca’s later work is within the urban landscape and he has created many delightful city squares which contain charming but useful outdoor furniture.

![](images/db384524a7e75db48e11e3a03dc880beecda32a680d34d13be81ec57262b68f1.jpg)

![](images/c2e4bdf26bfbc727af0e98a6e48da00e626245d95d8227009e0d8147c44bd25f.jpg)

![](images/6ef86279651e53c030217fd57f3599ffb0930179430385750c976afa5affe640.jpg)

## Chapter 3: Tactics

“What tactics are available to the designer? He can become a master of secret fixings; he can develop infinite refinements of junction detailing; he can participate in the increasing rationalisation of building services… The creative solution may lie deeper – just as the problem does.” <sup>13</sup>

Figure 3.1 The Maruhiro Flagship Store.   
Nagasaki, Japan.

## Introduction

Often described as the details of making interior space, tactics is a term we use to describe a more substantial way of creating particular identities utilising a number of spatial devices. These instruments, used in the construction of interior space, range from the everyday elements that designers are familiar with, such as floors, walls and ceilings, through to more esoteric materials such as light, surface and movement. All of these elements of space may be utilised by the designer in a variety of ways in every project. This is in order to create a very distinct and particularised set of environments for a variety of different uses, often for numerous occupants, some of which will not even be known.

Figure 3.2 Casa De   
Musica, Porto,   
Portugal. Rem   
Koolhaas/OMA. The   
brushed steel clad   
staircase was designed to create a dynamic   
elevated interior street in the space.

The six main tactics outlined in this book are: Plane (façade, walls, floor and soffit), Object, Light (artificial and natural), Surface, Openings, and Movement. It is no coincidence that these components form what could be considered one of the basic mechanisms of the interior: the room. We consider the room to be a mechanism as it can be considered as the constructed entity of a series of systems, procedures, materials, structures and devices, all of which will be both physical and cerebral. The tactics that we describe in this chapter are the basic elements that form the constructed room, an assembled enclosure that directs our behaviours in both subtle nuanced fashions as well as in instructed

Figure 3.3 Bar 10, Glasgow, UK. Ben Kelly Design. A carefully considered palette of ideas and materials were combined to construct this enduring space.

![](images/e4272114e311db2e178607edd326ddb5c4e02208ecc80010ecbaab49d69ddeb4.jpg)

![](images/0391d9cf21fd32cff77d4e0ebf9c4b73ce0d4e16a3e396dfe99c8e6cf9200218.jpg)  
and didactic ways. As Don Delillo states:

The point of rooms is that they are inside. People behave one way in rooms, another way in streets, parks and airports. To enter a room is to agree a certain kind of behaviour. <sup>14</sup>

Tactics are the complicit elements that form methods for the way that people behave inside interior space. In a particular room the way any element is arranged, deployed and detailed will invite the occupants of the space to act in a very particular manner. For instance a handrail, or the balustrade of a landing or walkway, will impact upon the occupant of the room, in a very specific way. Its shape and form, its material condition, the messages that it conveys, can invite the hand for its use in a certain way, and in turn steady its user as they move vertically between spaces, or as they stand on the edge of a void. A stair, detailed in a particular material and organised in a particular manner will not only lend a visual image to a space, it will also create an internal logic and rhythm for its user as they move between floors.

When orchestrated all together, the effect of a series of well-thought out and highly organised tactics of the interior can lead to space that is enduring. Their nuanced coordination ensures an unforgettable interior experience, where light, movement, surface, planes, objects and openings are combined to create memorable and extraordinary interior space.

## Plane

## Introduction

Derived from the Latin Planum, plane denotes a flat or level surface, usually placed either horizontally or vertically in or outside of a space. It is one of the most recognisable elements in the built environment, and is one that the occupant of any space will usually have a direct relationship with. The four planes described in tactics share some commonalities. They will often connect in some way, such as wall to floor, but it is when they are combined to create confined space in the form of a room, that they exemplify the control available to a designer.

## Façade

When it came to prominence as part of architectural discourse, the decorous façade stood as an architectural super-element, coordinating the door, windows, and walls, united into a singular and signature composition.

– Alejandro Zaera-Polo and Stephan Truby. <sup>15</sup>

The façade is often described as the plane endowed with the most signifi cant meaning in architecture. As Zaera-Polo suggests, throughout history it has provided the

opportunity to create a prominent plane, one that is replete with aesthetic, cultural and political signifi cance. This is still the case, but technologies have impacted upon its role as an exterior-facing wall. Its responsibility for controlling both interior and exterior environments, excluding inclement weather, and retaining interior temperatures, has been radically altered through the development of glass and curtain walling systems. Because of the signifi cance that the façade of a building will consist of, and combined with the complex planning laws of most cities, the façade is often one of the few remaining planes left standing after a building is demolished. Therefore the aesthetics, and subsequently the meaning of a good façade, are often highly prized. In the remodelling of the iconic Everyman Theatre in Liverpool, Haworth Tompkins utilised the signifi cance of the façade to project a meaningful image of the building and its occupancy back to the city.

The main west-facing façade of the building was adapted to portray a large-scale public work of art consisting of 105 moveable metal sunshades, each one carrying a life-sized portrait of a Liverpool resident. Typographer and artist Jake Tilson created a special font for a new version of the iconic red ‘Everyman sign. Collaborating visual artist Antoni Malinowski made a large painted ceiling piece for the foyer, to complement an internal palette of brickwork, black steel, oak, reclaimed Iroko, deeply coloured plywood and pale in situ concrete.

As well as signifying what a reused building can mean to the city, the façade of a building can provide a direct view into what the interior has become. Brinkworth were commissioned to rethink the All Saints brand’s identity. The designers created an unforgettable façade display for the retail spaces through which they wanted to refl ect the All Saints image of ‘elegant decay’. The interior of the fi rst store in Spitalfi elds, London, was partly ruined, with walls and bare structure exposed through a process of creative demolition and the intuitive stripping back of the layers of accretions in the existing interior space. The exterior façade was enclosed in rusted steel and then the shop window was fi lled with Singer sewing machines, projecting the tailoring background of the brand’s founders and the function of their clothing retail space to the street outside whilst enticing the shoppers in.

![](images/f92f1f3691d7124aaceeaf244eb61f220470d66fad07c7a92715bb65cc5115d8.jpg)  
Figure 3.4 All Saints, Spitalfi elds. Both the façade of the store and the interior were fi lled with rows of Singer sewing machines: an acknowledgement of the brand’s tailoring history.

Figure 3.5 The   
Everyman Theatre,   
Liverpool, UK. Haworth Tompkins. The façade was composed of   
numerous life-size   
portraits of the   
residents of the city.

## Wall

The wall is a plane that both divides and unites space. Whether permanent or temporary, a wall will organise and demarcate what is public, private, formal or informal in a space. It is a spatial device that will provide protection, separating various visual, acoustic and environmental situations. For instance, in the Casa De Musica in Porto, opened in 2005, both the visual and acoustic properties of the main hall were enhanced through the utilisation of corrugated glass screen walls, allowing light and view into what is normally designed to be a sealed off black-box performance space. Glass is not often used as the material for enclosure because its acoustic properties

A wall is beautiful, not only because of its plastic form, but because of the impressions it may evoke. It speaks of comfort, it speaks of refi nement; it speaks of power and of brutality; it is forbidding or it is hospitable; – it is mysterious. A wall calls forth emotions. – Le Corbusier. <sup>16</sup>

![](images/c022909318d3944948f7ee0244c82bad1e17f360755a0fbaca1dc970cb1d910f.jpg)

are negligible and therefore, along with the ceramic-tiled walls of the room surrounding the window, an echo of Delft Pottery, the contemporary glazing of the space created a new way of thinking about how glass can be used in acoustically sensitive projects.

The wall is an element that can be designed and made to work very hard in an interior, containing storage in the form of cupboards and shelves upon which items such as books and possessions can be placed. Walls can also be less matter-of-fact, and instead, when made very thick, can contain surprises in the form of secret passages, stairs or libraries. Poche, or pockets, is a wall tactic where spaces such as rooms can be ensconced in the thick walls of what are often historic buildings. Utilising this wall tactic means that whole rooms can often be found secreted in the walls of historic palaces and castles.

As well as being secretive, walls can be used to impart information through the display of objects in an exhibition. In the Palazzo della Ragione, Milan, the design of the exhibition Convoy RSHA January 30, 1944, designed by Morpurgo de Curtis Architetti Associati, utilised a series of dynamic walls that were placed amongst the historic space of the existing building. Each wall told the story of the movement and deportation of Jewish people through this time. Walls were used to not only impart information but were also placed dynamically against the rigid structure of the building, in order to symbolise movement and denote the terror and sadness of these enforced transitions across space and time. Whether thick or thin, permanent or temporary, hard or soft, static or in motion, as Corbusier stated, walls can call forth emotions.

## Floor

The fl oor is a surface upon which our daily lives are performed. It is a plane that negotiates the space between the body and the substance of the ground. In an interior context, it is sometimes found to be a highly decorated element, patterned and embellished in order to make a distinct feature of a space. In other interiors it is reduced to invisibility, abstracted to form a continuous unbroken surface upon which use can take place unguided and without distraction. It can be textured, smooth, elevated, moveable, transparent, fl oating or solid. Whatever form it has been manipulated to take, it always establishes conventions, cultural habits and the techniques for territorialising inside spaces.

In the Towada community centre in Japan, Kengo Kuma designed the floor to be an undulating surface, providing a playful surface for children to roll around on inside the building when inclement weather kept them indoors. The timber floor was constructed from different shaped sheets of timber, stacked to form a contoured interior landscape, a series of rolling timber hills, where the children can roam and play.

In essence, in all projects, the floor is an instrument of territory making, an overlooked surface that is arguably a support player to the dominance of the vertical plane to which it is often attached – the wall, the surface of endless scrutiny in architectural history. The floor is often a discrete surface, yet one that is fundamental to our daily lives.

![](images/2fab959d237e59e5e47f5ca3f6a716151116e43a54a85434e3fce0a4ab8914e7.jpg)

Figure 3.6 Towada Community Centre, Japan. Kengo Kuma & Associates. The stacked layers of the timber fl oor provide a gently undulating interior landscape, upon which the children can play.

## Sofit

If the fl oor anchors us to the earth, the ceiling allows our spirits to aspire to the sky.

– Edwin Heathcote, The Meaning of Home.<sup>17</sup>

Usually opposite the floor and atop the wall, the soffit, or ceiling, provides an enclosure for a space in the form of a lid or a cover. It is a plane that can be mundane and banal, merely a cover for hiding the services of a space. The ceiling or the soffit – a plane installed on the underside of the floor above – has become synonymous with the contemporary requirement to artificially heat, cool, light, power and also to protect interior spaces from dangerous phenomenon, such as fire.

Throughout the 20th century, the space between the ceiling and the soffit became a deepening cavity that held all manner of pipes, ductwork and cables, detectors and alarms, neatly secreted behind a plane of modular tiles suspended in a metal grid. This has not always been the case and in some projects the ceiling can be afforded a vital role in the design of the interior, making an expressive surface in which even with the suspended ceiling removed, the exposed ductwork, cables and sensors are organised and used to delineate movement or lighting in a space.

When fully considered the ceiling can become expressive to such a degree that it becomes the dominating interior element of the space. The Camper Store by Atelier Marko Brajovic in São Paolo in Brazil has been fashioned from thousands of red shoelaces, hung from the ceiling. The ceiling plane undulates through the space and is atmospherically carefully controlled with lighting inside it. In carefully chosen spaces the laces hang lower right down to the floor and connect the ceiling with the floor plane.

A soffit can be designed to perform its practical duties, such as lighting and climate control, but rather than hiding these elements away it can be configured in a way that celebrates their existence. Lazzarini and Pickering’s design for a villa in Positano, Italy, included a table whose surface continued up exuberantly to the ceiling, fulfilling the duty to light a particular piece of furniture as well as creating a sculptural element in the interior space.

Whichever way a plane is utilised in a space, and for every example where it has been deployed without thought for its expressive potential, there are a multiplicity of ways of realising dramatic walls, floors, ceilings and the façades of interior spaces.

Figure 3.7 Camper store, Atelier Marko Brajovic in São Paolo, Brazil. Vertically hung red laces create an undulating expressive soffi t for the new shoe store.
