![](images/c42f1a15bed9892e036422494fa27a761546b5b8e9d0efe09130f8b9b2e04c17.jpg)

# Hotel Barrière Le Fouquet

Location Paris, France

Remodelled 2006

Designer Maison Édouard François

![](images/ce4a3f94729ac77d9eef3b3f576ba75188687ed526b10d70b7c9f9de0d4238cf.jpg)

Figure 3.8 In order to unify the whole aesthetic of the block, concrete facsimiles of scanned existing façades were applied to the fronts of the existing buildings.

To enclose a building with a new cover or a façade ensures that the manner in which the building is presented to its surrounding environment is altered. These changes may range from the straightforward, such as a repainting, or the reordering of non-structural matter, to something much more complex and significant such as restructuring the façade and altering how the building is communicated to its existing environment. The form of the alteration is critical, because it can be utilised in order to signal a number of things. Sometimes in a sensitive location any changes to a façade will have to accommodate certain restrictions, often through specifi c regulatory conditions such as listing or

heritage concerns. But this does not mean that the resultant façade cannot still be a radical projection of the new use of an existing set of spaces.

In 1999 new owners acquired the traditional Parisian Brasserie Le Fouquet, along with the surrounding block of buildings. The block contained seven buildings that ranged in age and architectural distinction. They were in a central location and faced onto the Champs-Élysées, the Avenue George V, as well as two surrounding side streets. The brief to the designer was to transform the seven buildings into a whole building, unifying them to become a single

Figure 3.9 The new approach disrupted the relationship between the window and the detailing of the façade.

monument. Due to the dense fabric of the block, internal courtyards were to become terraces for the hotel, and a garden for the brasserie was to be retained in amongst the block of buildings.

New facilities for a modern hotel, such as a spa, other restaurants and servicing, were constructed in the shells of the seven buildings. More importantly, 107 rooms were to be created. These would have a very particular ‘service architecture’, a layer of support services that created a series of internal streets, starting in the basements of the buildings in the block. Along with new lift towers, this system could ensure that the highest levels of service to each of the rooms could be maintained, making sure that food, cleaning and all manner of deliveries to individual rooms could be received within minutes of being ordered.

The overwhelming problem was that of the façades of the existing buildings. These consisted of numerous different styles. The four buildings facing the Champs-Élysées were authentic Haussmann style and were listed. The two buildings on Avenue George V were built in the 1980s but were copies of earlier styles. The latest edifi ce to be built was a 1970s smoked-glass façade bank building. The precious, yet mixed, qualities of the architectural heritage of the site prohibited the design of a contemporary style. Instead the designer took a radical strategical approach. The most recently built contemporary façades of the existing buildings were demolished. The Haussmann style listed façades were selected and copied: scanned like sheets of wallpaper, and then applied to the exterior of the contemporary façades of the block, unifying them as one.

Rather than rendering the new façades in the same colours and materials as the

originals, extensively camoufl aging them or passing them off as old, the new exteriors were constructed and fi nished in raw concrete, ensuring a clear distinction between the new and the old. The scale, form and details of the original façades were retained, yet fl attened in the concrete. To add to the dissonance between the new and the old a seemingly irregular array of glazed openings were punctured through the façade. These were the windows of the new hotel rooms behind, on fl oor levels that did not necessarily correspond with the details and scale of the interior levels and the copies of the Haussmann façades. The traditional relationship in a historic façade between a window and the surrounding details of the opening, such as architraving, stringcourse, pilaster and balcony was irrevocably lost in this approach. Yet the dissonance projected in the relationship between new and old created a striking new façade.

![](images/c70985a0461a8d9ba649661b84f4acfc98ee26c0e03e32936926e6c8bdc129c5.jpg)

## Paul Smith Shop Front

Location London, UK

Remodelled 2013

Designer 6a Architects

![](images/b272c90d3d2ca0753e1acce683d247525a372f3a71348a4fed36c2bbb8024242.jpg)  
Figure 3.10 Glass vitrines, reminiscent of the shop windows on the nearby Burlington Arcade, enhance the views into the interior.

When considering intercourse with a building, its face, however veiled, must always be a desirable and a provocative item. – Colin Rowe, The Architectural Uncanny. <sup>18</sup>

The remodelling of an existing building will often provide the opportunity to rework the exterior as well as the interior of the space. This transformation may take the form of the application of a new external surface or screen with which to communicate or symbolise the identity of a new interior. In the case of making a new public space, one where visitors are invited to come and browse, and in turn hopefully consume what is inside, the portrayal of the ideas of the brand, the message of the company and the interior, will need to be communicated in a desirable and pleasing manner.

Figure 3.12 The cast-iron panels forming the rusticatedeffect base of the façade contrast with the upper level painted brickwork and the patterned window balustrades.

The new Mayfair shop front for the Paul Smith fl agship store, designed by 6a Architects, incorporates elements of existing London streetscapes. Objects such as balconies, railings and lamp posts, observed from the environments around the site, were combined with the textures, woven textiles and the characteristics of cloth, to make an abstract pattern for the

new screen façade surface of the store. The existing brick façade of the building was painted black, creating a stimulating but subtle backdrop for the new screen surface. The ground fl oor level of the building was then clad in an interlocking relief of cast-iron panels. This was

![](images/71c1aca9ae3c13817e560ae5fa3d9a2326364e5e3ba0f0878e7e32c8c4759aa4.jpg)

Figure 3.11 The same spherical motif of the surrounding cast-iron panels was used to disguise the wooden fi re door.  
![](images/8597de66b4b6f501066a737292579ff04031ae5945cb545fb8923ea856699a3f.jpg)

Figure 3.13   
The plan below shows how the curved-glass shop windows   
protrude from the   
façade in order to   
compete for the   
attention of the   
passer-by.

reminiscent of architectural rustication, yet with a new contemporary twist on the traditional idea of making a base or plinth for a building.

Each panel was digitally modelled, and the circular patterns of the surface CNC carved from plastic blocks to make a former. The panels were then cast in a traditional manner using a sand-casting mould. The iron elements of the screens were allowed to partially rust and then were sealed in order to form a rich dark patina, reminiscent of industrial machinery, evoking the textile manufacturing equipment of days gone by.

Each panel was then hung on the façade, incorporating a series of precise lap joints to ensure a seamless application. This type of jointing was very important as it accepted any movement or expansion, a given condition especially as the panels would adjust and compress and move during disparate ranges in temperatures. This system of fi xing also ensured adequate ventilation to the wall ensuring no damp or ‘cold-bridging’ of the existing walls.

The circular pattern created an optical complexity that added dynamism to the streetscape, especially when moving past the façade, ensuring that the eyes of passers-by would be drawn to the shop front. To enhance the window display,

a series of curved glass shop windows were positioned in the openings previously occupied by sash windows. The glass vitrines were lit, and in a nod to the nearby Burlington Arcade, as well as other adjacent Mayfair shops, the view into the carefully crafted display cases enhanced the beautiful and eclectic carefully selected products for which the Paul Smith brand is infamous.

The dynamism of the screen was reinforced vertically by placing a solid non-textured cast-iron panel at the bottom of the façade, and counterpointing the heaviness with a thin balustrade balcony emerging at the fi rst fl oor. The thin and elegant handrail protected shoppers from any danger of falling out of the upper fl oor sash windows, when opened in agreeable weather, as they loiter on the fi rst fl oor of the shop stupefi ed by the glorious goods on show.

The continuity of the pattern was maintained even in the detail of the wooden fi re door that opened onto the street. The circular motif used on the façade was carved into the timber panel, and the door was stained a dark hue in order to remain anonymous and fi rmly in the background of the new woven textile-like façade.

![](images/f3d8a339fa7a35c43d8a00c29297a8267f3614601d3da265e5f5b2e1bf5599d6.jpg)

# Taichung Infobox

Location Taichung, Taiwan

Remodelled: 2011

Designer Stan Allen Architects

![](images/7a6a5a61c778d928b1b18af4ab6ee388e2757972ac869c1764bcc01346f50375.jpg)

Planes such as walls, fl oors and soffi ts can be seen to link and unite disparate spaces. Through their construction and detailing they can communicate important aspects of a project. Qualities such as permanence or temporality can contribute to their character, and in turn they will convey distinctiveness. Unusual materials may initiate complex and unusual readings of their identities. Even the timescale that it takes to construct them, and the period of time they are expected to remain on site, can provide the impetus to convey important messages regarding how they have come to symbolise the meaning of a particular project and the site in which it is located.

Fig. 3.14 The two storey pavilion is situated as a freestanding element in the large open space inside the obsolete aircraft hangar.

The decommissioned 240-hectare city centre site of Taichung airport is being regenerated in order to become the new Gateway Park City. Stan Allen designed the masterplan, a mixed-use development,

arranged into four distinct neighbourhoods distributed around a long, runway-like, thin park. In order for the general public and other interested parties to feel a part of the development and also be able to monitor the progress on the site, the designers proposed the construction of a pavilion from which to oversee the work, a space that would also contain an exhibition on the site’s history and future use.

One of the key aspirations for the new city plan was the ecological footprint of the project and the long-term sustainability of the place. In order to exemplify these ambitions the designer decided to ensure that the new pavilion summarised and communicated an environmentally friendly message. The 1160 square metre pavilion was sited inside one of the now defunct large aircraft hangars on the edge of the airport. This location provided a huge open space, one that prompted the designers to utilise the full height of the building, creating a freestanding two storey element within the grand hall. The rest of the hangar around the Infobox was designated as temporary exhibition space, allowing visitors to circulate generously around the large open shed space before entering the Infobox.

Bamboo is in abundance in the Far East, and is an economically expedient and practical building material. It is often sustainably grown, and managed and harvested appropriately. Bamboo is stronger per centimetre than steel, and because of this it is a material often found on local building sites where it is used for scaffold. The Taichung Infobox was constructed using a steel frame structure that was then clad in bamboo. Bamboo was chosen not just for its sustainability but also for its strength, where it was utilised in order to bind the steel frame together. As well as its structural capabilities it was also used in order to exemplify identity and

make historical and responsive connections to the visitors to the site through reminding them that this development will not be alien to the city, but was conceived as an extension of the fabric of Taichung. The temporal nature of scaffolding also provided a connection in that it was envisaged that the pavilion will stand for two years before it is then demounted and recycled, with both the steel and the bamboo being reused elsewhere, echoing the changing nature of the cityscape around the visitors to the site.

The walls of the Infobox were made utilising a 75cm-deep three-dimensional grid made from 40,000 pieces of bamboo. The plants were cut and boiled and then dried for one month, giving them durability as well as a tea-coloured appearance. Bamboo construction is highly regulated in the Far East and it must only be worked upon by suitably qualifi ed installers. While delivered to site in fairly standard lengths, irregularities in shape are prevalent. This rendered conventional working drawings

Fig. 3.15 In section, the 750mm deep bamboo and steel construction of the wall of the pavilion contrasts with the portal frame of the hangar.  
![](images/ac1bce75aa823ba14ed36e1f7e4b745fdb0b477e466488698a71b398cb5aa3d9.jpg)

Figure 3.16   
In parts of the walls,   
the density of the   
bamboo is differed in order to let in light and set up views through the space. Figure 3.17   
Openings in the wall of the pavilion frame   
views of the   
surrounding building and the adjacent new developments beyond.

fairly useless, and instead a certain amount of contingency was required as the installers customised the large pieces of bamboo on site as and when they were happy with the size, shape and quality of each piece needed to construct the pavilion. Aware of the need for a large amount of leeway, all that the designers stipulated was that each corner acted like a stitch, as the materials were connected to each other. The two storey pavilion was entered on the east side of the hangar. The ground fl oor contained exhibitions and also a presentation space. Steps ascended to the upper level where the main presentation space was arranged as a set of bleacher seating. A series of openings frame the views of the gateway city park as it develops ensuring a connection between the past, present and future of the city, its occupants and its reused infrastructures.

![](images/3e882f61d792c1a3f76f2c12f400bf0d0654ce23df6820694d338c3651bd0a19.jpg)

# Maison, Floirac

Location Bordeaux, France

Remodelled 2012

Designer Petra Blaisse / Inside Outside

![](images/377cadf09ab73f10b201379f4613a2ad045dd9409931c88f3db63d17594a1671.jpg)  
Figure 3.18 The terrace curtain fi lters the light and the views to the surrounding landscape.

In the essay ‘Curtain Wars: Architects, Decorators, and the 20th century Domestic Interior’, the author Joel Sanders suggested that the curtain embodies many of the tensions and prejudices that have divided the roles of the decorator, architect and designers of interior space.<sup>19</sup> He suggested that it is here that the hard surfaces of the architecture meet the soft fabric of the decorator in a juxtaposition that reinforces the common perception that architects work conceptually, with tough and durable materials, while the decorator works intuitively with moveable and often ephemeral objects and surface coverings. Sanders concluded the essay with a call fo the invention of a new design vocabulary that overrides conventions that would collapse distinctions between ‘masculine’ or ‘feminine’, ‘decoration’ and ‘architecture’, ‘solid’ and ‘ephemeral’ and instead embody a new design language for domestic space and beyond.

## He stated:

The time is ripe for a new generation of designers to move beyond Curtain Wars and invent a hybrid design vocabulary that will allow a range of human activities to transpire in domestic spaces. <sup>20</sup>

![](images/d4854cff20c3ce1fd3bc31d018823e3db96eabc291343629e27297458e602fd4.jpg)  
Figure 3.19 Diagram showing the new curtain strategy in the three levels of the existing hillside house.

In 1998 OMA/Rem Koolhaas designed a house for a client who, during the project’s undertaking, was involved in a serious car crash that left him paralysed from the waist down. Rather than create a one storey, fully fl at and easily accessible bungalow style building, ensuring the wheelchair-bound client encountered no change of level, the resulting project was arranged with three fl oors stacking vertically into a hillside house.

The ground fl oor basement was carved into the hill and contained private spaces such as bedrooms. The ground fl oor contained a garden room and the upper fl oor housed parents’ and children’s rooms. The fl oors of the vertical building were connected via a 3 by 3.5 metre central platform ‘room’. This level plane, with no walls, was operated by a hydraulic lift that would rise through the building allowing the wheelchair user to access all spaces of the house as well as his library of books, artwork and a cellar of wine.

Fifteen years after the original design and construction of the house, Inside Outside were commissioned to revisit the spaces and reassess their qualities. They were asked to do this in order to propose some new interventions that would revitalise the domestic space for the new occupants of the home.

Inside Outside devised a series of curtains that would be utilised to accentuate or edit various aspects of the existing qualities of the house. The bottom fl oor was sunk into the landscape, with an interior courtyard facing the caretaker’s residence and guesthouse. Here Inside Outside introduced a brown and silver, double layered, silk curtain, enhancing the light quality when closed and drawing light into the lower spaces.

The middle fl oor was the most transparent space, boasting a huge glass façade. The client wanted to be able to shut out the light coming in from changing angles of the sun during the day. The designers created and installed a white cotton curtain, a surface that contained a horizontal slit, and a panel that refl ected the bright light of southern France outwards, while subtly diffusing it inwards. An exterior curtain track on the southern façade was reused to house a grey net curtain. In its early years, this part of the house was accompanied by a jute curtain. The new and large net screen fi ltered the view and the harsh sunlight, creating an extra in-between space on the terrace.

The top fl oor was semi-transparent and covered on both sides by a large façade punctured with portholes that light up the bedrooms. These round windows created an opportunity for Inside Outside to

Figure 3.20 Bedrooms were enclosed within luxurious curtains that refl ected the light onto the walls of the rooms.

![](images/8cb7510f65d7bb888514eb4a3f122c9a881241ff16a151cda96ede31f69b39fe.jpg)

Figure 3.21 The new red wool carpet   
accentuated the   
central hydraulic   
platform and   
completed the fl ow of the room when   
elevated into position in the living room   
fl oor.

fabricate curtains that responded to the specifi c architectural conditions. White lacquer patches, lined with gold faux leather fl oating on a canvas of white voile, were derived to cover the windows and softly refl ect a golden glow on the interior wall. A horizontal slit in light blue and grey lacquer provided a delicate view outwards, while the penetrating daylight was cast onto the curtain’s oily refl ective surface. Thus the three different curtains for three different bedrooms answered to a different demand for daylight entry and colour throughout the building.

The element that connected the three volumes, the impressive hydraulic lift, provided a moving ‘room’ through the house. This created a challenge: what can the designers do with such an iconic architectural feature? To accentuate the presence of this fl at plane or room, Inside Outside installed a bright red woollen

carpet, one that partially covered the platform, and one that also lay in the living room, only showing itself in ‘complete’ form when the lift is locked into the living room level and thus completing the fl oor. A second red carpet, with a deeper pile, was placed against the curved wall in the media room, as a prelude to the other circulation element in the house, the tube stairwell, linking both of the vertical elements together using the same highly coloured carpet.

The deployment of a series of considered planes such as curtains and fl oors has revitalised the iconic building and provided a space that was more suitable for the new occupants of the home.

![](images/638881798f39316f495e5d6ad8672a306a23c14027a5c275626a668ced9ab862.jpg)

Carrer Avinyo

Location Barcelona, Spain

Remodelled 2012

Designer David Kohn Architects

![](images/ebff707b1249cc0f2985da8b8e375c141492857dd9d35028bb0f6f02a9d2ec14.jpg)  
Figure 3.22

Axonometric drawing of the apartment. The ‘stack’ of bedrooms to the top right, and the kitchen to the left, are linked together by an upper level balcony.

![](images/e6642850caa4051c4e9afe84b2fecea6746601dd4423bc74ff73c22c35412b72.jpg)

It seems surprising that so little is done with the fl oors. They are, after all, the stages upon which our lives are acted out.

– Edwin Heathcote, The Meaning of Home. <sup>21</sup>

When carefully considered, the fl oor plane can provide much more than just the pragmatic requirements of covering a surface. The fl oor is a surface that can be utilised by a designer to create a variety of effects. It can be employed in an overtly functional manner, providing a covering that supplies direction, warmth and colour, as well as a distinct surface or texture, inside of a building. It can also be used to link, as well as to separate, spaces. Whichever way it is deployed, as in all tactical moves, the selection and use of a particular fl oor plane will reveal the designer’s full intentions for the realisation and ultimately the identity of the space.

Figure 3.23 The subtle shift from green to red in the colour of the fl oor tiles emphasises the change from private to public interior space.

The Carrer Avinyo apartment was designed for two Spanish brothers to share, and in

which to spend their family holidays in the city. The apartment occupies the fi rst fl oor of a building that is located at the intersection of two important roads in the city: Carrer Escudellers, and the street that gave the apartment its name, Carrer Avinyo. Through the demolition of a number of internal partition walls, a large, angular, double-height space with many small rooms, was converted into one large, well-proportioned single space. The demolition of all of the interior walls ensured that the single space was now ordered and articulated with a series of full-height windows, each with a balcony.

The preponderance of windows and balconies united and also animated the room. This enlivenment of the interior was through natural light, now illuminating the interior, and by overlooking the streets below – allowing the hustle and bustle of the busy city to become part of the interior. A ‘stack’ of bedrooms was placed at one end of the large room in the form of a large, over-scaled piece of two storey furniture. At the other end of the room, and above the kitchen, the designer placed the other bedroom. A mezzanine level, incorporating a library, was designed to afford the occupants access to the bedrooms and also the upper level bathrooms.

While the interior of any apartment is usually a resolutely private space, the Carrer Avinyo is treated as a semi-public space, drawing in the atmosphere of the immediate urban context and distilling its essence into the main family room of the interior. The formation of the large room opens up the interior to the city through the light and views of the numerous windows of the space. Akin to the adjacent George Orwell square, the room was designed to be like a public plaza, a place where the brothers and their families could congregate and enjoy each other’s company around dining and socialising. In order to accentuate this, Kohn installed a large, irregular, lozenge-shaped table at the pointed end of the triangular shaped apartment.

Figure 3.24 Triangular fl oor tiles and the lozenge-shaped table reinforce the dynamic shape of the apartment.

Unifying the interior, yet subtly shifting the emphasis from the private to the public, is an intricate and highly articulated mosaictiled fl oor. It was designed with a triangular pattern profi le, one that emphasised the geometry of the plan of the apartment. The tile pattern was graded in colour from green at one end of the apartment to red at the other. This was done in order to subtly differentiate the brothers’ private spaces. Ensuring continuity with other great designers of the Catalan capital, the encaustic tiles were manufactured by Mosaics Martí, suppliers of tiles to the projects of Antoni Gaudí. As well as symbolising the geometry of the apartment, the triangular profi le of the tile was set up to mirror the adjacent George Orwell square: an even deeper connection made with the urban environment inside the interior space.

In the smallest detail, in this case a fl oor tile and its colouring, the story of the interior, its occupants, and their relationship to each other, and the city they inhabit can be told.

![](images/4c9b9ae1da49951777e43c9b25d2e5ee695444750eb7e5e387f47fceff4abc94.jpg)

# La Maison Unique Longchamp

Location New York, USA

Remodelled 2004

Designer Thomas Heatherwick Studio

![](images/3b7e877a87e3bb1d568c5c32d3e5a81962737e2e77bd8d76952367ee4e044223.jpg)

Because the fl oor has often be regarded as an inert supporting player to the vertical, it has been, in a sense, free or wild. It is a reservoir of more primal or potent desires.

Figure 3.25 Seen from above, the sinuous curves of the waves of the ribbons of the steel fl oor appear as an undulating landscape of stair treads and risers.

– Keller Easterling, Rem Koolhaas, AMO-Floor.<sup>22</sup>

Floor planes can be used to separate as well as link interior spaces. In order to do this they may sometimes have to confound expectation and become shifting landscapes of material and movement, working hard to create tangible and visual connections inside diffi cult or unusually conceived existing interior contexts.

After the success of the Zip Bag, a bestselling luxury bag for the fashion brand Longchamp – which was made from a long run of Zip that allowed it to double or half its size by unfastening it – Thomas Heatherwick was commissioned to design the new fl agship store for the same company in New York. The challenging site offered very little street impact, with virtually no shop window presence – considered a vital and valuable component in successful retail design. Instead, all of the available retail zones were to be housed in spaces on the upper two fl oors of the building. Therefore, a radical solution was needed to ensure shoppers were intrigued enough to enter the store and journey to the upper levels of the space – a notoriously diffi cult proposition in store design. Heatherwick decided to entice customers to the upper fl oors of the building in two ways: using daylight and by creating a fl owing, fl uid fl oor that would blur distinctions between what was fl oor and wall plane in the three levels of shopping space.

The fi rst element to consider was the street presence in the form of a new shop window. But in order to fl ood the interior space with daylight, Heatherwick inverted the idea of a shop window as a street front façade. He opened up the roof and created a new rooftop ‘shop window’. The glazed opening still acted as a viewing device but, instead of contemplating the goods in a street level window, the new aperture bathed the fl oors in natural light, animating the interior and drawing the customers’ view through the space, up into the depths of the store.

The second element designed to counteract any reluctance to move upstairs and through the store was the insertion of a new horizontal and vertical fl oor stair. Drawing inspiration from the Zip Bag and its complex structural realisation, a fl uid landscape of ribbon-like sheets collapsed distinctions between the different levels. It was conceived to entice the customer inside and invite them to ascend through the three levels, culminating at the new

![](images/ac173bd8bcabc00c99fceb4891f99ec2610669fffa03f09f33f746e232614eff.jpg)  
Figure 3.26 The long ribbon links the rooftop shop window with the street level and collapses the distinctions between the three fl oors of the existing building.  
Figure 3.27 Short section showing the ribbons of steel fl ooring fl owing through the interior of the store.

vertical shop window on the top fl oor. This new fl uid and sculptural stair/fl oor plane ensured that the sinuous qualities of the element were immersed and then ultimately enhanced by the preponderance of natural light as it cascaded down.

Each ribbon, weighing over 60 tonnes, was made from a 2.5cm thick steel plate, welded into position on site and inlaid with strips of coloured rubber, ensuring a non-slip surface on each tread. In order to ensure complete compliance with building regulations, balustrades guaranteed customer safety. In order to complement the sculptural qualities of the element, specially treated glazed panels were attached to a series of upright poles on the ribbons. These were heated in a specially made ‘toaster’, making them pliant, drape like a fabric, and form unique gravitygenerated forms derived from their own weight. These were left to harden before being attached to the uprights of the rails. The effect was of a set of billowing transparent sheets, set against the fl owing waterfall of the curved steel streamers, as they poured through the space. To complete the sinuous qualities of the fl oor, a set of display stands, with thin steel legs supporting a platform, were attached to the steel edges of the ribbons with magnetic feet. The bags displayed look as if they are enjoying a rollercoaster ride down through the shop and cascading through the different fl oor levels of the interior space.

![](images/ddd286cf0fa22cbd099dc1ce10d231cc52929f0e10d4304c624e842d785f5791.jpg)

# Maruhiro Flagship Store

Location Nagasaki, Japan

Remodelled 2015

Designer Yusuke Seki

![](images/8334e27310304806a24ef1e3de37c686b917addb50b333fda5280be200af9cbf.jpg)

Figure 3.28 The fl oor was constructed of 25,000 pieces of rejected pottery, carefully stacked and then cemented together with poured concrete.

The fl oor has first of all a pragmatic meaning, much more than the walls and the especially the ceiling. One must be able to move around on it and place objects on it.

– Pierre Von Meiss, Elements of Architecture. <sup>23</sup>

As well as being pragmatic, and satisfying particular requirements such as the placement of objects, for example furniture, the thoughtful design of a fl oor plane can produce an unusual and inspiring element of the interior; one that can be startlingly provocative, even though simply executed and elegantly thought through.

Maruhiro, based in the area around Nagasaki, is the leading producer of Hasami Porcelain in Japan. It has a history, dating back to the 17th century, of producing tableware and making and distributing ceramics across the country. In order to make Maruhiro’s new fl agship store memorable, Seki designed a new fl oor inside the simple one storey building constructed from 25,000 pieces of rejected pottery from around the region. Each piece was carefully stacked and then moulded together with poured concrete to make a series of ‘bricks’ from the pottery. Seki did this in order to form an elevated plinth upon which products could be displayed and

customers could interact with them, never losing the connection between the fi nished object and the processes that it took to get them there. The 25,000 pieces of pottery were sourced from the Hasami region. Each of these pieces was known as Shinikiji in Japanese. These are objects that were found to be fl awed after the initial bisque fi ring by their respective local production facilities. Seki sourced and revived these pieces, retaining them and turning them into solid fl ooring materials, and

![](images/99b78ef23edbc9ed5fd3d012dc5cc632a9f010a8e79cc0f087945f0966b7746f.jpg)  
Figure 3.29 The distinction between the fi nished and the imperfect ceramics is reinforced by the placement of completed objects on timber display boxes.  
Figure 3.30 Doorways to the store frame the centrally placed ceramic plinth fl oor.

transforming them into a new architectural material for the store. Collected together and then formed into an elevated plinth, this ‘stage’ is similar to Monohara, the name given to the kiln-side areas existing solely in and around the Nagasaki ceramic production factories. These areas were where the broken pieces of the fi ring processes were accrued, ensuring a lasting connection to the histories and processes of the production of the ceramics displayed inside the store.

![](images/4c80ab3e2e0a4e7abd170fe5cd3c268654b14192a155ac725314f3298112f6f4.jpg)

## Sake no hana

Location London, UK

Remodelled 2008

Designer Kengo Kuma & Associates / Denton Corker Marshall

![](images/59a479be3ed4ded2ca329f5f01cdf84290229a8932a08725ac65f6fea245f760.jpg)

Figure 3.31 The Japanese Cypress timber soffi t and walls of the upper level restaurant signify the difference in quality of food and also the time expected in which to consume it.

As well as enclosing services and ordering internal spaces, a soffi t can be used to exemplify the narrative of both its context and the function of an interior space. Sake No Hana, an exclusive Japanese restaurant owned by Alan Yau, the originator of Wagamama and Hakasan, occupies the ground and fi rst fl oor of the Grade II listed Economist building, in Mayfair, central London. Designed in 1964 by Alison and Peter Smithson, the building was highly regarded for a number of innovative features, including its carefully thought out approach to not just the buildings themselves but the open public spaces in-between. The structural simplicity of the building, along with its

‘Roach’ cladding, an expressive fossil-rich Portland stone, created a landmark building, described by Reyner Banham, and the Smithsons, as an example of ‘New Brutalism’.

The restaurant was designed to occupy two fl oors of the smallest of the three towers, marking the corner of Ryder and St James Street. The ground fl oor was designed to accommodate a sushi bar, while the upper level would contain a more formal Japanese restaurant. The corner entrance to the restaurant encouraged the diners to choose the more informal sushi bar experience, arranged around a counter-kitchen and facing St James St, or to ascend the escalator to the upper-level restaurant.

Both the informal sushi bar and the upper-level main dining space were enclosed by an atmospheric tree-like framework of bamboo, on the ground level, and Japanese Cypress on the upper fl oor. On the ground fl oor the bamboo columns were 100mm in diameter, and formed into a series of columns that supported a soffi t consisting of a series of 60mm bamboo beams bolted together to form an overhead lattice. The frame of beams undulated through the interior and across the ceiling. Like an inverted ziggurat, its edges were just one beam thick as they reached the edges of the space, and the external windows, but becoming much deeper as it signalled something important, such as the entrance, where it acted as a signalling device to passers-by on the busy street. Lighting, in the form of Japanese paper lantern pendants, were suspended over the sushi counter. The interior walls were

lined with bamboo while the exterior walls were constructed from cypress veneer.

Upstairs the soffi t was constructed from a framework of Japanese Cypress. The 100 by 100mm columns supported a lattice of 100 by 30mm beams. These were fastened with wedges, as opposed to bolts, aligning the craftsmanship of this level’s interior shell with the slower pace of the consumption of the food in this part of the restaurant and hinting at the quality and time expended on not only the food’s intake but also its preparation. The interior wall was constructed from simple cedar louvres.

The main dining space was placed on a raised platform, and organised around the human scale of tatami mats and low tables. Guests are invited to sit on cushions on the mats, cross-legged in a traditional Japanese dining style. To complete the atmosphere, the double-height windows of the building were covered with full-length

Figure 3.32 Plan: Upon entrance from St James Street the diner has the choice of a quicker, more informal experience in the ground floor sushi bar or a more formal dining experience in the restaurant above.

![](images/8e1c29c11bb610a3a3ad6c2a9cfc28acfbb7b4882efd4b6cfe6c9f8f351cde97.jpg)

bamboo blinds. These were screens that shimmered in the breeze. At night they convey the intimate atmosphere of the atmospheric interior to the outside world.

As well as disguising the important servicing aspects, such as air-conditioning supply, lighting, cabling and emergency systems such as sprinklers, that a space like this requires, the carefully considered soffi t narrated the two levels of Japanese

dining. Kengo Kuma also described the importance of the relationships between the existing building that the Smithsons had carefully considered and the spaces in-between them. Kuma designed the interior to appear as though a ‘futuristic forest’, positioned within the open spaces of the plaza between the three towers of the site, as well as exemplifying something of the symbolism of a Japanese urban environment.

![](images/40d6b90ee9c86f3c9888090a63a3a303467c7b82747ce46c29f8ccabbab026cd.jpg)  
Figure 3.33 At night, subtle lighting externalises the ‘futuristic forest concept of the interior to the adjacent city.

## Clyde Frazier’s Wine and Dine

![](images/1e434953fd7ace9fd1f152fddf529ed7d621b19bf47ea300ab8d15f7ae710223.jpg)

The calculations of George Bailey on the economics of the slab skyscraper, bring together three environmental aids – acoustic tiling, air-conditioning, and fluorescent lighting, that were to add up to one of the key inventions of recent architecture: the suspended ceiling. – Reyner Banham, The Architecture of the Well Tempered Environment. <sup>24</sup>

Figure 3.34 Careful analysis of Walt Frazier’s wardrobe resulted in the development of the conceptual thinking and ultimately the deployment of the expressive fl oating soffi t.

Often considered as a workmanlike component of any interior, an expressive soffi t design can instead become the most startling and original element of an interior space. In many projects the important aspect or main role of a ceiling plane is to disguise the servicing and acoustic dimensions of a space. Beyond that the designer is free to develop the element in whichever way they see fi t. In some extreme instances, the soffi t can be used as metaphor representing the client, a

depiction of the identity of the space and a symbol of the person who has generated the impetus of the project.

With its frontage adjacent to 10th Avenue and occupying a whole block between 37th and 38th Streets in New York, Clyde Frazier’s Wine and Dine is not a sports bar in the traditional sense. Initiated by its owner, a former professional basketball player and now announcer for the New York Knicks, the restaurant houses a dining space, lounge, bar and kitchen as well as a 3 by 6 metre basketball court, situated between the bar and lounge.

Running the entire length of the space is a 52 metre long dynamic plane of colour and pattern unifying all of these elements, quite literally, under one roof. Walt Frazier (Clyde) earned his eponymous nickname due to the fi lm Bonnie and Clyde, as well as

Figure 3.36 The animated ceiling plane runs the length of the interior space and signifi cantly enhances the intensity of the vibrant bar (overleaf).

Figure 3.35 Images on the walls of New York Knicks games contrast with the bright shimmering multicoloured modular stee panelled planes.

through his tendency to dress rather fl amboyantly. When commissioned to undertake the project, Thom Mayne decided to immortalise the host’s sartorial elegance in an expressive soffi t, one that fl oated through the long room.

The soffi t was constructed with 544 aluminium panels, each formed from a modularised variation of six shapes. The inspirations for the patterns and colours of the plane were drawn from Frazier’s wardrobe, and in particular his bespoke suits, which were extensively photographed and then digitised, printed onto adhesive fi lm, and applied to the fi ns. The subsequent riot of colour, patterns and form sliding through the space is akin to a fl oating wardrobe of Frazier’s suits and ties cascading through the interior above the diner’s heads.

The scintillating colour scheme contrasted with the muted backdrop to the rest of the restaurant, a space fi nished with a polished concrete fl oor, and resin countertops for the bar and the kitchen. Large oversized columns were used to delineate where various elements of the interior, such as the bar, dining and the lounge begin and end, without enclosing them behind walls. Each column was clad with digital images of Clyde in his various outfi ts whose abstracted patterns and colours fl oat past on the fi ns of the soffi t. A 4.3 metre tall wrap of images, depicting various 1960s and infamous 1970s New York Knick’s games, surrounds the dining area and encloses the buzzing atmosphere of the busy eatery.

Like many traditional soffi t designs, this is an acoustic ceiling, and the electrics, lighting and mechanical ducts of the services for the space are all contained within it. But outside the duty-bound performing role of the ceiling plane, its fl amboyant exterior represents a dynamic and colourful identity, much like the character after whom the place has been named.

![](images/9ea3176df3d2548315f086450cb1bd94151a68a70a19e0cd8cf49cb686f0be80.jpg)

![](images/9970026a24d3ddd0931cad904b3f7e4c05d9b0732f7e83a1d1363683701a8d97.jpg)

## Object

## Introduction

The design and positioning of particular objects and elements that inhabit the existing building is a process of exploring and taking advantage of the opportunities and constraints inherent within the original structure.

– Graeme Brooker and Sally Stone, Elements and Objects. <sup>25</sup>

Objects – whether an element of furniture, a pair of shoes in a shop, or an exhibit in a museum – are focal points in an interior environment. The positioning of these signifi cant entities is an act that can exemplify the essential principles of any interior. In other words, the careful selection and thoughtful situating of the object, will exemplify a number of different ideas and narratives about a space, and indicate that this is how the space or room will be used. For instance, the placement of a piece of furniture, such as a chair, will delineate how the designer anticipates how a space will be utilised through the particular placing of its occupants. The orientation of the chair, its relationship to others, and if it is a low or high chair, formal, or informal, at a table, or low and reclining, particularises how the space will be used and in turn how it will be viewed when the user is seated. Fixed furniture elements like banquettes and tables ensure that the occupant is invited to use and to view space from one angle or place,

Figure 3.37 Maidan, Bureau A. Swiss Architecture Museum, Basel.

![](images/771665d85803ccc11ce6f858db3f2c68b0058589aca773b37a8e7abbf2ab3dfe.jpg)

compounding this sense of the particular arranging of space. In other words, the way in which any objects are located and exhibited, whether it is a person seated in a space such as a bar or restaurant, an object in a museum, or products in a shop, such as clothing, will exemplify distinct narratives about a space.

The story of the interior can be told through the medium of objects: elements that enclose and thus disclose all of the narratives of the domain of inside space. Objects are an interior entity that can be realised at a variety of scales: from the size of the city, to the dimensions of a human body. They are the defi nitive interior elements, things that can represent all inside environments through their physical formation. Utilising intellectual and psychological dimensions: all components that are material constructs, elements designed in order to portray particular spatial identities and atmospheres. This narrative also includes objects that are ‘found’, elements that are selected and used in a particular way in order so that their existing properties can ‘resonate’ with the new uses that they are subsequently prescribed. Found objects can be selected precisely because they come already imbued with meaning and loaded with resonance.

For instance, Maidan, a project by Bureau A, took a number of 100 by 60cm aluminium shields used by Ukrainian soldiers and security forces during the months of resistance in Kiev in 2014, and reappropriated them, in order to turn them into an expandable shelter, capable of protecting small or larger groups of resistance workers. The resonance of the found object lay in its appropriation from an object of attack and shielding its user from missiles, to a more passive roof, covering its occupants and keeping them from harm.

Bureau A have described it as a metaphor for architecture:

Give them a shield, a shelter or a barricade and they will create a small project out of it, the manifesto for how architecture will be in years to come. <sup>26</sup>

Objects are by no means static. Their movability will lend them a liquid dimension and one that will allow them to affect differing environments through their infi ltration of particular zones. Bao House by Dot Architects consists of an unusual cube mounted on the back of a bicycle. The cube is formed from polyurethane foam inserted into a mould. Bao means ‘bulge’ in Chinese, hence the name of the house. The house was designed as a moveable shelter that was human powered. One side of the cube slides open and there is enough space for three people to sleep in the foam-insulated water-resistant space.

The object as a receptacle of stories and an element in which each interior can be analysed using its own theoretical principles, or terms of reference, will include an examination of spatial organisation, atmospheric conditions, and will often demonstrate pioneering technological innovations. In short, each object positioned in a space, whether it be a pair of jeans or an impressionist painting, will often convey a clear vision of the spirit of the age, a paradigm of the zeitgeist in the matter of the interior.

![](images/bd63385cca5dce29125ef57ec81b78ec578755224d8507b08c7e7974401b2ab8.jpg)  
Figure 3.38 Bao House, Dot architects. Beijing, China.

# Ler Devagar Bookstore

Location Lisbon, Portugal

Remodelled 2009

Designer Aurora Arquitectos

![](images/96fe8d226f3c5643c8d71e9ce887303d81b7a67d76ae0d78cd40b81f1565ad3a.jpg)  
Figure 3.39 Immovable printing presses were incorporated into the new design of the bookstore, creating a unique set of interior spaces.

Working with existing buildings will always entail the transformation of what is already in situ. The material condition of the existing building will often provide some of the impetus for change, and its careful scrutiny will expose matter that can be considered useful in the ongoing processes of instigating modifi cations and ultimately the possibilities for transformation. Not only will the existing fabric of a building need to be altered to accommodate a new use, any residual material that is waste, or matter that is either left on site or required to be removed, will have to be dealt with in a considered fashion. Sometimes this may not be easily remedied. A budget may not stretch to radical alterations that are costly and time-consuming. And sometimes material that is left on site is just too

awkward and diffi cult to remove. If so, then the designer of the space is left with few options, one of which is just to work with what is already there by incorporating whatever is left behind into the new design. This can take the form of objects that are considered to be immovable, and therefore need to be incorporated into any factors for the design of new interior space.

Aurora Arquitectos were commissioned to make the Ler Davagar (read slowly) bookshop in the LX Factory in Lisbon. The bookstore is just one of over 150 creative companies located in and around a 23,000 square metre sprawling estate of buildings and enterprises in a 19th century Lisbon textile and weaving mill in the city district of Alcântara. Abandoned until 2008, with the injection of some funding and government support the complex has now become a ‘creative village’, a space for social enterprises and small businesses. Design studios are situated alongside bars, restaurants and music and arts venues, creating a thriving economy and a buzzing hub of activity for the outer suburb of the city.

One of the early additions to the complex was the bookshop, housed in a warehouse unit that had previously contained a printing company. The spatial legacy of the company took the form of some leftover redundant machinery and in particular some huge rotary printing presses. Recognising the potential of maintaining a connection to the past through the origins of book production with the presses, as well as aware of the pragmatic concerns of the budget and that the cost of removing the machinery would be extremely prohibitive, the designers decided to retain the massive sculptural printing press machinery in the warehouse and incorporate it into their design work. Thus the design processes became an approach that was set out to work with, in and around, as well as upon the printing presses in the large open space.

The bookstore announced its new presence to the LX Factory complex with the design and construction of a large black steel door with a complementary yet startling bright yellow entrance. The stack of presses was set back from the door, allowing visitors to adjust to the overwhelming spectacle in front of them, as the three storey wall of machinery dominated the central hall of the large open warehouse space. The bookshop, two bars, counters, and rows upon rows of shelving, groaning under the weight of all kinds of publications, were then arranged around the machinery and appliances as well as the structures supporting them. The designers conceived the space in an ad hoc

Figure 3.40 Meeting rooms in two open steel-trays are situated atop the structure and provide an overview of the mass of the bookshop components.

fashion, with niches and spaces between the machinery becoming locations for book display, some shelving, or a bar, recessed into the structure supporting the heavy weight of the presses.

This contingency-based approach allowed the designers to create an improvised feel to the interior and in particular the relationship between new and old. A series of convenient staircases, previously allowing workers access to the presses, were utilised for getting to the layers of shelving. Satisfying codes and regulations for the change of use to a public building, the bookshop visitors can clamber all over the machinery, pick up books and fi nd a space to stop and read, have refreshments, or just marvel at the spectacle of the now silent equipment. At the top of the enormous contraptions, the structures of the machinery have afforded the designers some leeway to insert two meeting room levels. Formed in steel, they are conceived as open ‘trays’ within which to meet, rest, read, or merely take in the view of the surrounding spectacle and to catch your breath after you have managed to ascend the mountain of metal and machinery.

![](images/3851d126d36715945ccf30f5346934fc2f2d7a1b57293076d0a631f147587e45.jpg)

## The Bath Room

Location London, UK

Remodelled 2010

Designer Ben Kelly Design

![](images/63e6dee4d7079695c6e97c1a0c24f3e6e8454da919c1fc08d769d061b9b52307.jpg)

It’s not a showroom, it’s not an exhibition, it’s a working environment, where people can come and look and talk and investigate and imagine… It’s like a Wunderkammer that you open up and there is a surprise to be found… – Ben Kelly Interview,The Bath Room. <sup>27</sup>

Figure 3.41. In order to enhance testing and   
selection, the   
considered   
arrangement of the   
showroom emphasises the juxtaposition of the various elements of the sanitary ware on   
display.

Details and services such as materials, fi nishes, lighting, handrails, bathrooms, can often get overlooked in the design process. Yet all are fundamentally the elements and objects that the user of any space will physically come into contact with the most. In a bathroom, objects such as toilets, sinks, baths, taps, shower units, mirrors and lighting, along with surfaces such as tiling, come in a huge range of formats,

types and styles. The choices made when specifying them have huge ramifi cations for the design of the interior and in particular the creation of specifi c identities for both public and private spaces. The thoughtful design of spaces in which to explore and imagine how these might work, to consult with experts, and to select and arrange examples, along with being given assistance on how to deploy these objects, are practically non-existent.

The Bath Room is a studio space for designers and anybody creating bathrooms or service spaces with which to view, examine and explore how they can be designed, selected and then specifi ed. As its designer Ben Kelly states, it’s not a

traditional showroom, nor an exhibition, but instead a hands-on studio where objects and artefacts from a number of ranges of sanitary ware can be taken off the walls, or off the screen, played with, or seen in actual settings. In order to realise this type of space Kelly conceived the interior as a thought-provoking cabinet of curiosities – a playful space that promotes interaction with the objects themselves and invites guests to explore and engage as well as experimenting with the composition of all of the different ranges of products. The designers do all of this through the creation of an environment that mixes off-the-peg materials, such as mobile shelving units, steel piping, and bright orange plastic sheeting, with the readymade objects of the toilets, sinks and plumbing. The effect is the creation of a vibrant interior space, one that invites investigation and the hands-on testing of sampled objects.

The bathroom occupies 280 square metres of a second fl oor space in amongst the creative industries that it seeks to inspire in Clerkenwell, East London. In order to reinforce the distancing of this space from other more traditional showrooms, the entrance lobby is treated as an ‘air lock’: a space to decompress the visitor from their own preconceptions of a toilet showroom before they enter The Bath Room. Lit with blue neon the lobby acts like a symbolic ‘plunge-pool’: a threshold that is cleansing the visitor of their presumptions, as they are bathed in blue light before they enter the well-lit second fl oor exhibition show space.

The single-fl oor space was organised into a series of zones, a strategy used not only to order the goods for display, but also designed to enact a noncompartmentalised method of exhibiting products. This was an important consideration because it not only allowed spaces to fl ow into each other, dispelling

the notion that the private space has to be enclosed and hidden away, but most importantly allowed customers to see objects against each other, making possible unusual or unforeseen juxtapositions across the open space. In order to enhance the opportunity for chance connections, the layout of the products and the environments used to contain them was unusually innovative and not seen in a traditional sanitaryware showroom. For instance, the designer range was located next to bathing and wellness, and were all contained in an off-the-peg mobile shelving system. Usually found in archives or libraries, the system is on a track that is moved by a hand-wheel in order to open each cabinet to reveal a series of bathroom appliances and increasing the chances of surprise. The slow slide of each cabinet opened up not just a different set of appliances but also a riot of different orange and grey interior fi nishes, contrasting sharply with the well-mannered brushed steel exteriors of the cabinets.

The visual language of plumbing was used to emphasise the dynamism of the small compact interior. Tracks and other connecting devices such as pipework, normally hidden away in walls or fl oors, were exposed and utilised as directional instruments for fl oor, wall and ceiling. Track systems for fl ooring and lighting also unite the mobile shelving system, and combined with the lighting and piping connected the different zones of the showroom to become an instrument of movement, signalling the fl ow of products and people through the space. The enclosed presentation room was conceived to be a transparent box wrapped in glass panels and lifted off the fl oor with bright orange-bracketed feet. Although bolted to the fl oor they look temporary, and appear to also be fl uid, promoting the sense of movement in a

space that appears to be constantly on the move.

There is nothing in this interior that has been overlooked; as every element, object, detail and fi nish has been very carefully considered, specifi ed and then creatively applied. It affords the visitor the impression of an exquisitely well-crafted room of objects and spaces, one made to engage with and promote the idea of looking and enjoying the often-overlooked details of one of the most unnoticed rooms.

![](images/b4f5958bf24b79e08832ad926b2ba10b5c3c0884389068900e60de4cf6498a66.jpg)  
Figure 3.42 The cool neon-blue entrance lobby ‘air-lock’ determines the threshold between the inside and outside of the showroom.

Figure 3.43 A mobile shelving system, usually found in archives or libraries, can be moved around in order to accentuate the connections between different collections.

![](images/489ad2e93c57dd1bf8ef7d0e6a2ce8ecc878718eaf8cd59175a401bc9388cc4d.jpg)

## The Mistral Wine Store

Location São Paolo, Brazil

Remodelled 2012

Designer Studio Arthur Casas

![](images/f656569dedd7dccac7173de3ead64f86937e82a7ba64a2a5649a172e7bc84558.jpg)

Figure 3.44 A reading room is enclosed by books on wine held in-between fl oor-toceiling timber beams at the back of the main double-height space of the store.

Figure 3.45 Plan: The organic language of the design is derived from the fl uid nature of the product it is displaying. Visitors are moved through a series of rooms, in a journey that carefully orchestrates a number of back and forth routes.

The form and also the content of an object can provide the inspiration for the design of a new interior space. Wine retailer Mistral commissioned Studio Arthur Casas to design a new store, one that would physically represent the ideas and values of a company that did most of its business on the internet. Without losing the digital dimension that the company valued so dearly, the new space needed to exemplify the virtual brand of the company.

As well as this, it needed to extol the more visceral delights of sampling fi ne wines. In short, the design of the site needed to attract new clients as well as retain existing customers, elevating and inspiring them to ensure that they felt like a clientele of knowledgeable wine tasting connoisseurs. The small, 100 square metre, store was situated in a busy upmarket shopping centre in São Paolo. The double-height space needed to house a sales area, cellar, storage, interactive gallery, a reading room and places for wine tasting, as a small group or as part of a larger party.

Studio Arthur Casas were infl uenced by the sensual and poetic qualities of the wine they were displaying and selling, and the bottles in which it was being contained. Aware of the amount of programming they needed to organise in such a small space, they utilised an organic, sinuous, curved design language for the interior. This was to ensure that both the poetic and the pragmatic dimensions of the project could be satisfi ed and ultimately resolved into a cohesive whole.

The designers organised the interior as a journey, a route or path between the fi ve spaces of the shop. At the beginning of this route was a tall, double-height curved wall. This enticed the shoppers to journey into the store and engage with each aspect of the sequence of interior spaces. From the walkway of the mall, a glass wall, enclosing the sales area, contrasted with an inviting curved corridor: a space lined with wine bottles. The designers created the gently curved entrance in order to invite customers deeper into the space and to discover the unique brand of the company, and hopefully the contents of each bottle. The gradual curve created a path to the centre of the store where the fi rst sales table was positioned. From here doorways revealed the next part of the journey into the reading room, or the interactive gallery. The display wall of bottles appeared to be fl oating, as they were suspended by their necks and thus projected bottom-end out into the organic shaped corridor, exaggerating the curve of the space.

Between the backlit opaque glass of the wine display wall, and the tall vertical timber strip upper wall, a dark stripe of black glass concealed several screens: each was activated by guests touching the wine bottles placed within each section. Once a bottle was picked up or even just touched the screen revealed general information about the wine. This clever device ensured that the wall became animated with information, images and stories of the wine. The bottles were organised by region, and were designed to provide a visual clue to shoppers outside the store in the mall, as well as provide a constant undulating wall

![](images/3201191b8a2ea0bd3d52f8d7167c0ccf3716be72ae384521dc790ecad9f2c7a8.jpg)

of texture and animated graphics at the beginning of the interior journey. All the technical equipment was hidden within the walls. Where the curved wall of the entry was separated from the structure and walls of the existing mall site, a room was formed. This created a small double-height cellar with its own air-conditioning system. Separated from the main corridor by a glass door, this was not open to the public. It was designed to be a secret place to store rare wines, which only the chosen few could access.

Inside the gallery space, wines that were displayed on the wall could be brought to an interactive table. This was designed to showcase a monthly selection of special wines. Sensors underneath each bottle allowed customers to place it on the table and get information related to the wine, data that was projected onto the fl at screen. Effectively, when positioned on the surface, the bottle became the equivalent of a keyboard mouse. When it was twisted, information such as location, interviews with producers and notation on taste and other information would appear on the fl at surface of the table.

![](images/1564d89ecc2622ff808b4c2ea7e9c6cee2691f895fb511a863e6edaf03388ca8.jpg)  
Figure 3.46 A sales table surrounded by wine bottles punctuates the initial part of the journey through the interior.

A reading room was created at the rear of the store. Books on wine were held between fl oor-to-ceiling wooden laths. A Lina Bo Bardi chair invited customers to relax and read next to the double-height wall that was covered with bottles. Secreted in the wall, and separating the entrance and the interactive gallery, was a hidden stair. This ascended to the tasting space in the mezzanine, where invited guests could sample wine away from the hustle and bustle of the shopping mall below.

In every space the bottles of wine were presented in surprising ways. Even though omnipresent, the object of the bottle was never rendered monotonous. Instead it becames a motif and a texture that was used to distinguish the ambiance of each context in which it was carefully positioned, whether as giver of information or as decoration. Wine holds sensations and stories that are extremely diverse. The designers used this multiplicity of possibilities, inviting the discovery of the content of each wine bottle, the starting point of the design project. In Mistral, the presentation of the product cannot be dissociated from the information it contains. Therefore the truly innovative aspect of this project consists in trying to bring together architecture, product, information and interaction into one single interior entity.

# State Textile and Industry Museum

Location Augsburg, Germany

Remodelled 2012

Designer Atelier Brückner

![](images/55768ab16f9d5b271e5849eae15fcb96a86234977f8089e4a912504f4d82ab69.jpg)

Figure 3.47 Digital projections onto the ‘three graces’ animate the exhibition space and bring to life the extensive pattern book archive.

For Semper, building originated with the use of woven fabrics to defi ne social space, specifi cally the space of domesticity…weaving was used as a means to make the home, the inner life separated from the outer life, and as the formal creation of the idea of space. – Mark Wigley, White Walls Designer Dresses. The fashioning of modern architecture. <sup>28</sup>

There are many parallels and similarities to be drawn on the relationships between textile design, fashion and the construction of interior architectural space. Gottfried Semper, in the Four Elements of Architecture, considered the close relationship between fabric and weaving and the construction and making of

enclosures with textiles. His writing has ensured an enduring relationship and interest for designers in exploring connections between body, space and dress. The design of a textile museum offers a unique opportunity to examine once again the relationships between making spaces for the display of garments, textiles and pattern, and the construction of architectural elements.

The Augsburg State Textile and Industry Museum (TIM) is situated in the Augsburger Kammgarnspinnerei, a Worsted-spinning mill. Worsted is a particular woollen yarn, which this mill specialised in making. Friedrich Merz founded it in 1836. It was the fi rst major industrial company in the city, and it

employed up to 2,000 workers. It was the largest Worsted-spinning mill in the Federal Republic of Germany until it closed in 2004. The Austrian architect Klaus Kada restored the museum in order to present the history of the Bavarian textile industry. Atelier Brückner were commissioned to design a 2500 square metre exhibition dedicated to the textile world of fabrics, colours and patterns.

This exhibition space was organised into three distinct zones: pattern books, histories and processes. The core of the museum’s inventory was the huge collection of pattern books of the New Augsburg Cotton Factory. This rich and unique archive, which comprised over one million patterns from three centuries, was presented as the core spatial link between all zones of the display.

The designers achieved this by displaying the pattern books in cases, but they brought them to life by utilising interactive digital technologies. In the central zone of the exhibition, staged like a catwalk, were three large mannequins. The over-scaled sculpture-like qualities of the fi gures adopt the classic silhouette shape of the pattern-making and sampling objects. In this zone the archive of pattern books was brought to life by a series of projections onto the three larger-than-life fi gurines. The so-called ‘Three Graces’, named after the mythical classical statues, were connected to digital screens that allowed the visitors to select patterns from the books and then have them projected onto the mannequins. The infi nite richness of the patterns became interactively accessible and animated the central area of the display. The light show was reminiscent of a catwalk of giant models,

Figure 3.48 The rolls of fabric drifting through the machine park exemplifi ed the narrative of the processes of constructing and manufacturing textiles.  
![](images/be99e84e9514db2b3bda126d7c9a4b27130a39fbc8f1a380da5f338f763cf894.jpg)

![](images/ecdad97f61219d64a9aa5d2743f39253d287b915ae91347419f02b8f6f386882.jpg)  
Figure 3.49 Model: The distinct zones of the museum, designed to surround the catwalk.

each spinning round with the patterns and fabric styles projected upon them.

The narratives of the production processes and the history of the development of the textiles were exemplifi ed with two parallel routes of exhibition, both surrounding the core of the central zone. The production process examined the journey of the material as it begins to be manufactured from the raw fi bres, to the yarn, and then to the fi nalised woven or knitted fabric. This journey was articulated through the exhibiting of the historic machine park.

This incorporated a collection of machinery used to produce textiles. Guided tours through the park showed people through the space and exemplifi ed how the material was refi ned and then made into textiles. Finishing processes, and the handmade area of the exhibition, brought to the textile-making process through the incredible sound of the working looms and machines. This made it a comprehensible as well as an exciting and visceral experience.

The second storyline runs parallel to the production processes, and consists of a series of rooms displaying the history of textile production. The visitors experience the development of the beginnings of the weaving trade in this room, with the last room showing future textiles and smart fabrics. The visitors can explore and test the latest ideas in textiles for themselves in a hands-on exhibition environment. When combined together all three aspects of the museum bring to life the designed textile object.

## Light

## Introduction

When articulated and deployed thoughtfully, both natural and artifi cial sources of light have the potential to enrich and transform interior space.

The power of light lies partly in its abilities to sculpt, shade and foreground space, poetically realising the abstract properties of an interior whilst communicating its numinous qualities and dimensions to its users. The symbolism of light, and its magical dimensions, can be manipulated to instil supernatural sensations in onlookers. Light also embodies authority, and through the sciences of the understanding and application of lumens and lux values, a detailed and structured framework of hard data and facts will decisively relay how a space for particular uses will need to be illuminated. Whichever way light may be tactically used, and quite often it may well be in both of the ways that have been outlined – atmospherically and scientifically – it is fair to say that without light, space cannot be visualised. It is a design material that has the ability to significantly enhance and suppress mood and emotion.

As well as a mood enhancer, light will be deployed in order to illuminate and animate space. The main stair of the Casa Batllo, by Antonio Gaudi, was designed to be an expression of organic sinuous curves, fashioned from wood, steel and mosaic tile. Linking the living spaces, the stair gently curved and slithered between the floors, as though a snake. As it rose between the levels of the building, the wooden balustrade was expressed as a twirling ribbon, exploding into a vertical twist of thin steel, crowned with a glowing glass crystal ball neatly encapsulated in the winding taper.

Figure 3.50 Casa Batllo. Barcelona, Spain. Antoni Gaudi.

The dynamic energy of the stair was animated with a wash of natural light, delivered from above via two large sculptural lozenge-shaped lead-glassed skylights. Natural light was used to enhance and bring to life the dynamism of the stair, as well as illuminating the way through the space, as the occupants and their guests moved vertically through the building.

The effects of light, its articulation and deployment, will give form and meaning to a space. Its careful material expression, through a light fitting of some kind, or through a very focused attenuated source of illumination, can provide a highlight and a focus for any kind of interior. From the outside of a building an illuminated interior can appear inviting and atmospheric. In the startlingly inserted addition to the Elsa Morante Public Library in Varese, Italy, by DAP studio, both existing openings and the new windows in the addition, were illuminated so that at night they would provide a welcoming view to the people of the village. The powerful dialectic between old and new is echoed in the uncompromisingly contemporary addition to the building. At night subtle lighting softens this stark distinction and whilst still ensuring that the new element is different from the old, the interior lighting unites the inside spaces of the new library.

![](images/3e11daa60ffab676f2220ea343c80c09edece40199ad9f48d3db07042b79502f.jpg)

The dialectic between old and new can be expressed in light through an effect akin to chiaroscuro, or the treatment of light in painting and drawing, often utilised to dramatic effect. This can be exemplified through the manipulation of the contrast between shade and light:

The beauty of the Japanese room depends on a variation of shadows, heavy shadows against light shadows. – Junichiro Tanizaki, In Praise of Shadows. <sup>29</sup>

The 17th century Katsura Imperial Villa, outside Kyoto in Japan, is often considered to be a precursor of Modernist spatial tendencies. Its harmonious balance of landscape and buildings, site, view, composition, proportion and order, offer an epic spatial and time-based experience. The Shokin-tei Pavilion, the fi rst of four shelters on the path through the garden, was designed for a tea ceremony. Access was via a stone bridge where guests could wash their hands in the water of the pond before the ceremony commenced. Typical Japanese trees inspired each of the tea pavilion names, and ‘Shokin-tei’ evoked the pine that surrounded it. The interior of the pavilion was arranged around three tatami, with a combination of screens and walls that could be slid back and forth, both to modify the views of the garden and to accentuate the light and shadows of the interior spaces during the tea ceremonies which took place throughout the day and also at night.

![](images/0653e5e03a790b9550f485378f19050694b80187c4261b92997ad73abea817da.jpg)  
Figure 3.51 Morante Public library, Varese, Italy. DAP.

# Atocha Station Memorial

Location Madrid, Spain

Remodelled 2007

BUJ+COLÓN Arquitectos with: Esaú Acosta, Mauro Gil-Fournier and Miguel Jaenicke

![](images/aa47a8a286b69c5472853e24d9f05d44a771537e5f4f27ce713fe00fa1885ab2.jpg)  
Figure 3.52 A shaft of light in the dark room draws visitors to the underside of the tower and offers some relief in the gloom of the interior.

The deployment of light and shade in any interior project will bring focus to a space as well as the ability to enhance its dynamic tendencies. The manipulation of natural light can be undertaken in order to impress very particular feelings upon the occupant of a space. Its poetic and emotion-altering qualities can be used to articulate both light and shadow, and thus create affecting responses to both interior and exterior spaces. Sometimes the handling of light in a very particular manner can be a device used to reflect upon tragic circumstances and direct what can be diffi cult emotions. Light can be the raw material that gives both comfort affects poignant responses.

On the morning of 11th March 2004, ten terrorist bombs were detonated on four commuter trains between Madrid’s Alcalá de Henares and Atocha stations: 191 people were killed, and 1,800 injured, in what was one of the worst terrorist attacks in Spanish history. Built to commemorate the bombing, the Atocha Memorial is located at the front of the main station, in a peaceful side room, removed from the hustle and bustle of the comings and goings of the trains and commuters of the busy central station. The monument consists of two connected environments: an empty underground chamber and an oval shaped glass tower. The former is quiet and

Figure 3.53 The 11 metre high tower is an illuminated beacon in the city and a constant reminder of the legacy of terrorism.

introspective, a space used to fi lter out the busy station and gather thoughts. The latter is externally orientated, and rises up into the outside environment to connect to the city: incorporating the noise of the urban surroundings back into the interior space.

The empty underground chamber affords the visitor some respite from the noise of the busy station and was devised to fi lter the transition from the outside to the seclusion of the monument. At its entrance the names of the victims of the atrocity have been etched onto the glass doors that separate the chamber from the station. Its dark blue walls were designed to be calming and subdued, in sharp contrast to the harsh and vibrant lighting of the station in which it was embedded.

The second part of the memorial consisted of an 11 metre high glass cylinder. The oval tower was placed on top of the second chamber. Its verticality provided a stark contrast, as well as a partial relief, to the

sombre, low-ceilinged, relentlessly deep blue despair of the entrance chamber. The tower was composed of 15,000 curved glass blocks, each one glued together with liquid-acrylic and hardened by ultraviolet lamps. During the day the tower is illuminated by sunlight, ensuring that natural light pours deep into the space. This shaft of illumination draws the visitor through the fi rst chamber to its centre and offers some respite from the darkness of the room. The interior of the tower was lined with an Ethylene Tetrafl uoroethylene (ETFE) plastic membrane etched with thousands of messages, left at the station by the mourners of the attack.

Throughout the day the change in light conditions as the sun moves around the tower animates the turret of words. The combination of the tower and the words brings to life the powerful expressions of dismay, anguish, shock and anger, as well as illuminating the defi ance of people responding to this senseless act.

![](images/b54f74b3738e93b5cb63fff90b362734c1e2f3b98afb3aa6ae8791f66430ee40.jpg)

From the otherwise dark chamber, people can look up into the light, and see words of hope and remembrance.

At night the mood of the chamber is reversed, as artifi cial lighting at the base of the tower projects the words outwards and back to the city. In this project the range of the properties of light, such as direct, indirect, bright, soft, subtle, shadow etc, are used to elicit powerful emotions.
