In this project, natural light is intrinsically connected with the memories and responses of a particular place and its unfortunate role in a moment of history.

![](images/0d10a44bab1f59682573321aa05260693a115bb9d6f570ad5600fbcb19ea4945.jpg)  
Figure 3.54 The tower of words.

## Hempel Hotel

Location London, UK

Remodelled 1996

Designer Anouska Hempel

![](images/91949de36d3fa38c0f328d354819a6b374fd238fef114b37944c926a173adf1d.jpg)

Figure 3.55 As the traveller enters the lobby they arrive directly opposite the monolithic block of the reception desk.

The most wonderful aspects of the indoors are the moods that light gives to space.

A deliberately   
positioned shaft of   
natural light punctuates the lobby. The sunken seats are recessed into the ground in front of the fi replaces,   
emphasising   
relaxation.

## Figure 3.56

– Louis Kahn. Writings, Lectures, Interviews. <sup>30</sup>

Natural light can be considered as a site-specifi c raw material when considering the design of interior spaces. This encourages its consideration as a principle spatial element, and one that will always be regarded as a fundamental component of the design of any interior space. The understanding of the range and movement of natural light, throughout the day and the seasons of the year, will provide the potential for a designer to organise and

![](images/7d1ddb29ba15864eb49750a74ad698573d928363132225a6fdae2f3910123be1.jpg)

arrange a variety of spaces and objects that will offer the articulation of a range of particular atmospheres. In some designs, lighting can provide the key material, a substance that, whilst immaterial, can shape and articulate all manner of spatial ideas, forms and identities.

Natural light is the fundamental material in the design of the lobby of the Hempel Hotel, an exclusive fi ve star residence that was carved out of a row of fi ve Georgian townhouses in West London. Anouska Hempel designed the hotel in the mid-1990s. It was created as a space that would transform the experience of the weary traveller as they arrived from the street and stepped into the hotel. Reviews of the space were written in response to the transcendent quality to the interior:

Crossing the main entrance is almost a mystical experience. Beyond it, the visitor fi nds a wide empty space, in which the only outstanding element is the monolithic block of the reception desk, which recalls the calm and contemplative atmosphere of a temple. – Arian Mostedi. Design Hotels and Architectural Design. <sup>31</sup>

The exclusivity of the hotel, and the sequential narrative of the journey from outside to inside, was exemplifi ed by the fi rst entrance space: a small white room for the concierge to relieve you of your luggage. A sliding glass screen, playfully fashioned to look like a Georgian six-panel door, was triggered to open by a sensor revealing the main lobby of the hotel. The lobby was arranged across the entire width of the ground fl oor of three of the fi ve townhouses. The space was bookended by a bar and restaurant in the fi rst and fi fth townhouse. Both were accessed behind monolithic screens placed at either ends of the lobby. The reduced, ordered, minimalist, pale, white aesthetic of the interior offered a striking antidote to the noisy street outside.

The visitor is greeted by a reception that has been articulated as a long solid block of stone, perched above an unyielding stand. The screens separating the hall from the bar and restaurant, at either end of the lobby, were expressed as solid walls, containing deep recesses for fi replaces. In front of both of these walls were seats. These were recessed into the ground, with elegant woven bamboo and wicker low tables offering the only colourful alternative to the unrelenting pale stone interior.

The lobby was punctuated by a shaft of natural light, a sharp-edged beam of illumination that pours in from the roof, six storeys up, through a precise aperture carved into the soffi t of the space. The light has been used to defi ne and embrace the monastic qualities of the room.

The meticulous and crafted elegance of the space was personifi ed in the careful detailing of all of the junctions and materials. Shadow gaps were employed where the wall meets the soffi t, to express the clarity of the junction and articulate their particular and different qualities. The soft edge of the grid of stone tiles was used to articulate the proportions and organisation of the desk, and the recessed seats and fi replace walls. Ultimately the grid had been used to order the whole room.

In the lobby of the hotel, the disjunction between the existing building and the new interior was presented as an initially startling and yet ultimately soothing quality to the interior space. Natural and artifi cial light was used to create a distinct ambience and atmosphere. Light becomes a solid material, and it is used to express junctions and volumes of space. Elegant proportions and robust construction ensured that the light animated the space.

It is these light and material conditions, their composition and their proportions, that infl uenced the form, organisation and the aesthetic of the new hotel lobby.

## Dressing The Screen

Location Beijing, China

Remodelled 2012

Designer Serie Architects

![](images/c35f413c7477c056a346ea404044d52d64a5a029a55b69f93357b52c7e785fd2.jpg)

Figure 3.57 Whilst   
implying the   
separation of the   
spaces, the   
diaphanous quality of the curtains ensured   
that sound, light,   
images and the visitors all merged into one.

Artifi cial light can be deployed through a series of mediums and mechanisms, instruments that can be used to fully animate an interior space. When carefully considered, sources of artifi cial light can galvanise a space with an added dimension of theatrical drama. When deployed in conjunction with other spatial manoeuvres and entities, such as sequence and materiality, artifi cial lighting can create magical and atmospheric spaces, brought to life by the people moving through them. This can be especially pronounced in the design of performance and display or exhibition spaces, places that will often require an element of ‘drama’ in their realisation.

commissioned by the British Council to design an exhibition that combined theatre, performance and display. Entitled Dressing the Screen, the exhibition showcased the connections between fashion and fi lm, using over 100 movies that included the work of famous designers and companies such as Alexander McQueen, Vivienne Westwood, Henry Holland and Burberry. The exhibition highlighted pivotal moments in fashion, fi lm and technology – from pre-internet experiments such as the early fi lms of Ossie Clark, Rifat Ozbek, BodyMap and Bella Freud, to pioneering technological advancements such as POST magazine, Burberry’s holographic

catwalk show and Cassette Playa’s Augmented Reality.

The temporary exhibition was located at the Ullens Centre for Contemporary Art in Beijing. Serie divided the large space into smaller rooms in order to create a more intimate setting, one that allowed the viewer to focus on each section of design work whilst not feeling too confi ned to the interior space. Section one contained excerpts from early cinematic experiments in the 1960s, through to the 1990s, and included works by Biba, David Bailey and Jaeger. Section 2 explored catwalk theatrics and spectacular shows such as Alexander McQueen. Other rooms featured work based around the pre-internet age, Heritage, fashions fi lms for the internet and then Icons and Muses.

In order to express the fl uid boundaries between each area of the exhibition, and to ensure that the visitor was not restricted by the rooms within the host space, Serie used fi ne white draping fabric to give a level of translucency, dividing the space into simple strips. In order to move from one room to the next the visitor needed to move through each drape, connecting the

Figure 3.58 Each hanging screen emphasised the fl uid boundaries between each section of the exhibition.

![](images/c6e341120ff00a00c0a31c6dce563a0a3f7e67a3e1f057c9ca824be96ce5380e.jpg)

subject of the exhibition with the tactile qualities of the space. The diaphanous, dream-like curtains became whiter as they were illuminated. These helped to separate each part of the space. Smaller screens at the entrance displayed early explorations of fashion through fi lm from the golden age of cinema. Stepping forward chronologically, and in space, another room featured the fi lms of runway shows from the 1980s. The main fi lms were displayed on large screens on the walls either side of the curtains. These culminated in the central room, showing the fashion fi lms of the internet age.

Dividing each space created an intimacy that allowed the viewer to focus on each section, whilst retaining a loose visual connection to each adjacent zone. Sound was localised, using special speakers. This focused viewers’ minds and views, and allowed them to concentrate on the video they were watching.

The atmospheric mixture of the projected fi lms in the room, seen through the curtains, ensured that the exhibition prioritised the sensuous relationship between light, sound, fashion and the body. As visitors drifted through the layers of fabric, and mixed with the moving images projected onto the walls around them, the combination of images and people, light and dark all coalesced and merged into one entity of bodies, shadows and fi lm.

## Gallery TOTO

![](images/242a13bb12c713092bc20a1ea727e15446f98c52bac494f175c3c9afce8bb067.jpg)  
Figure 3.59

Suggestions of what was going on inside the cubicles were implied through the use of fi lms on the outside. In reality the occupant had total privacy.

We were intrigued by the notion of the most private place simultaneously being in a very public place. We wanted to get away from the perception of toilets as closed and dark environments, where one rarely can see beyond the entrance area, and where one is usually confronted with a series of terraced stalls. - KDa co-founder Mark Dytham.³² – KDa co-founder Mark Dytham.

Traditionally spaces such as public bathrooms require a very particular approach to their illumination. Subtlety and discretion, as well as a functional approach to clinical and hygienic sources of light, are usually the driving factors behind how these public yet also intensely private spaces are lit.

In a recent project in Japan by Klein Dytham, an approach to how bathrooms can become less discrete and more fun places to visit, has used light and fi lm in order to make visiting the toilet an enthralling experience.

In a partnership with TOTO, Japan’s leading toilet manufacturer, Klein Dytham designed a working showroom, displaying their latest products, in the Sky Lounge, in Terminal 2 of Tokyo’s Narita Airport. The TOTO gallery acted as a showroom as well as a fully functioning public toilet. The project was a collaboration between the Narita Airport Authority, which was upgrading the airport’s facilities in preparation for the 2020 Olympic Games, and TOTO, which celebrated its 100th anniversary in 2017.

Figure 3.60 In order to elicit interest, enticing silhouettes of a series of activities such as cleaning and dancing are projected onto the bathroom exterior facing the main area of the lounge.

The Gallery TOTO is located at one end of the Sky Lounge. It is alongside cafés, relaxation areas and children’s play spaces. With over 14,000 travellers passing through the lounge, and on to Terminal 2 each day, it was important that the gallery was eye-catching.

Instead of fading discretely into the background, like most bathrooms do, the designers wanted the toilets to stand out and spark the imagination of the passer-by with something memorable and funny. They wanted to create an unexpected experience, one that they hoped viewers might share on social media and spread the word of the TOTO showroom. The gallery was arranged as a 20 metre wide clear glass box, 7.5 metres deep, containing ten individual elements. Each element was a bathroom, four each for male and female, one accessible universal toilet and a nursing room. Each space was designed to provide an open and bright unique bathroom experience.

Along with long-time KDa collaborators black\*bath creative agency, and the Japanese contemporary dance company Strange Kinoko, the designers choreographed and fi lmed a 20-minute performance of silhouetted happenings. These included and ranged from dance sequences to cleaning scenes, all at a life-sized scale. They also fi lmed water droplets and coloured waves, to add to the water-based sensual experiences. They applied fabric walls, each one containing low-resolution LED screens, to the external walls of the fi ve bathrooms, facing out into the lounge. Suggesting that the passers-by could see activities being undertaken within the toilets, the fi ve walls relayed a series of moving silhouettes to the lounge. Akin to

the traditional semi-transparent Shoji screens of a Japanese house, the panels suggested a semi-transparent view into the interior of each bathroom. In reality the user had complete privacy.

Each toilet space was fi tted with individual digital indicators, changing colour like a thermometer, and showing time elapsed in each space. So those waiting could see at a glance which toilet was likely to be free fi rst.

Inside the bathroom extra-large-sized panels of Hydrocera ceramic sheets were printed with large-scale landscape images of iconic Japanese features such as Mount Fuji. Ironically, in the often-described ‘littlest room’, the occupant was shown inspirational images of great landscapes as well as inspirational images of other journeys and travel. Of course at the same time, each toilet showcased the very latest in TOTO toilet technology.

Using innovative artifi cial lighting technologies, and with a keen eye for making the most unassuming spaces become magical, the gallery provided an innovative and entertaining backdrop to the large waiting passenger area, directly in front of the gallery, and in front of the planes as they drew up to the nearby gates.

![](images/1c79f4a8aca52602497b71f4bc32283bf57309e403eee9a561b2955dc3eab156.jpg)

## Surface

## Introduction

Materials are selected and deployed in the design of an interior in order to create specific spatial identities. Surfaces are critical tactics for the designer to consider, as they are the cover or overlay of the space with which the inhabitant or occupant of the environment will interact. The ‘palette’ or ‘sample’ is the language used to describe the assemblage of materials that designers and architects will collect in order to portray the proposed new identity of a space.

The importance of selection can also be extended to the ‘found’ or existing surfaces of the building that is to be reused. These may embody useful narratives from the history of the building. These stories may be symbolic, as they may be materials and surfaces that illustrate the previous life and uses of a space, or they will physically embody its history through the scratches and indentations of their previous lives. However found surfaces are considered, if they are to be reused and retained, their incorporation, along with new applied surfaces, can create a rich weave of a spatial identity that is a complex representation of the past, present and future of the interior space.

A diffi culty facing the architect is how to negotiate a past that concerns the detective and the historian and a future that is of interest to a client and the occupants.

– Irénée Scalbert. <sup>33</sup>

In the design of the Raven Row gallery, in Spitalfields, London, a detailed examination of the history of the building enabled 6a architects to understand how two 18th century silk mercers’ houses, and a 1970s concrete-frame block, could be meaningfully combined to make a space for art. The history of the buildings included the devastation of a fire in 1972. Some of the ornate details and mouldings of the houses survived, and inspired by the surface qualities of the conflagration, the architects reused the fashioned and charred timbers as moulds for the design

![](images/ce9ee4b5a4742b1ffb0c1e21278ea8b867c8fe34d7a59087c4f38022d94f9fb3.jpg)  
Figure 3.61 Raven Row. London. 6a Architects.

Figure 3.62 The Ethics of Dust, Westminster, London. Jorge Otero-Pailos.

of new cast-elements of the building. These consisted of the doorknobs and a new set of railings for outside. The surface qualities of the disaster of the fire were retained and traduced into a way of remaking new elements for the building.

## As Scalbert described this process:

New parts had been shaped after old parts, for instance, the charred timber panels and the cast iron handles and railings. All of a sudden old parts seemed to depend on new parts...Construction became overwhelmed with fi nishes. <sup>34</sup>

Some found surfaces are so unique, and their endurance is so vital, that they must be conserved and retained as found. This approach to existing surfaces can be exposed when the designer of a new space least expects it, and the stripping back of a space that is to be prepared for new use, reveals some long-hidden important elements. If retained, as well as being incorporated into any new design, these surfaces will also need to be cleaned and maintained. Westminster Hall is the oldest building on the Parliamentary Estate in the centre of London. A chamber has been on the site, in some form or another, for over 900 years and the Hall has been the venue for many of the important events in British history ranging from the trial of Guy

Fawkes and Charles I, and the lying in state of such figures as Winston Churchill. Jorge Otero-Pailos is the Director of Preservation at Columbia University and over a period of five years designed a project for the hall entitled The Ethics of Dust: a work that cleaned the 919-year-old east wall of the hall. The title of the work was taken from John Ruskin’s book about cleaning, and housekeeping. Otero-Pailos covered the entire wall with liquid latex, reinforced it with fabric, and then peeled it all off, extracting not just the dirt, grime and markings from the wall, but in essence 900 years of history of the city not only through its use as the centre of British political drama and life but also just as a building in a city that had endured a tumultuous history of change and endurance.

The resultant latex ‘skin’ was then hung a suitable distance away from the original wall from which it was extracted, so that viewers could walk through history, observing the new clean wall, and its resultant exposed markings, and its suspended doppelgänger: replete with the detritus of years of dust, breath, smog, fire, human hair and skin, accumulated throughout the ages. Both applied and found surfaces can be loaded with all kinds of meaning. It is up to the designer of interior space how they utilise these codes and symbols.

![](images/b3afb02d035478caa95f2a1108d4f0ed0ff8d96af607c4190aab2bc5543b091e.jpg)

# Armstrong Fair Stand, BAU

Location Munich, Germany

Date 2013

Designer Ippolito Fleitz Group – Identity Architects

![](images/7add87590f5874a4ab680d13e4d99d3b94503989150c0a1e71bfcda21a4745e9.jpg)

Figure 3.63 A vibrant and dynamic colourful geometric style was utilised in order to increase the stand's visibility in the show.

Designers will often specify surfaces in order to create a particular identity for an interior space. An ‘off the peg’ approach to this process ensures that each design project can be considered through tried and tested procedures. Some of these procedures include ‘sampling’ and also prototyping. Sampling is where fragments of the materials to be used are obtained and experimented with, arranged and presented to a client via a board placed together to give a sense of the projected identity of the new space. In some instances samples will be more rigorously tested for performance in particular conditions, for instance a fl ooring material will need to be tough, and provide resistance to heavy footfall, and so on. In

some instances mock-ups or full-scale prototyping will take place to ensure a full testing of surface materials. Whatever methods the testing of materials takes the form of, the recognition and then the sampling of materials, their exhibition and availability to potential designers, is a very important part of the design process.

How do designers get information about new materials that they can add to these processes? Fairs and exhibitions on construction materials are one method of getting information about new products, their capabilities and also their qualities, as well as sometimes seeing the actual materials deployed in a life-like situation. The BAU exhibition in Munich is one of the largest trade fairs in Europe concentrating exclusively on materials. Armstrong DLW is a producer of linoleum and vinyl fl ooring. In order to make an impact with a series of new products, and in order to ensure that more experimental designers and architects may consider their materials as a new and exciting product, they commissioned Ippolito Fleitz Group – Identity Architects, to make an exhibition that would stand out in the large hall of the trade fair.

![](images/e3c00d1693a5cbbc965f7e2e65e0ce42d9ba0444b8a4229a1f4d95f4fdf3860d.jpg)

The stand not only needed to communicate the company’s new materials, it was also required to encapsulate and express the expertise and experience of the business. The stand was also required to facilitate discussion, both formal and informal, seated and standing, and allow prospective customers a variety of experiences ranging from picking up information to being able to sit and discuss the products in much more depth. It also

needed some storage. Whether a short or long stay on the stand, all of these functions needed to be condensed into a spatial installation that impacted signifi cantly upon the visitors’ design sensibilities.

Using two particular surfaces, a woodgrain effect surface and a range of coloured vinyl, the designers constructed an expressionist, geometrically complex form of a stand that was wrapped in a bold and vibrant complex pattern of surfaces. A reception desk and three long tables were placed upon a fl oor that folded up to become a large wall that ran the length of the platform. Both were fi nished in a colourful geometrically distorted blur of asymmetrical dynamic shapes and colours.

To increase the stands’ visibility in the hall, a suspended soffi t carrying the brand’s name and also lighting was hung above the main meeting table. Amongst the dazzling interplay of colours and shapes, the main

![](images/8042ed04e6411aaed2fa4dc406b6d4b464eea68f4202ee3ed95b479a132aa826.jpg)  
Figure 3.64 As a counterpoint to the storage wall, the reception desk, meeting tables and seating area were all organised in an asymmetric, dynamic fashion.

table provided the principal zone for discussion, requiring a simplifi cation of the colours and a lessening of the impact of the dynamism of the stand in order to facilitate serious discussions. This area was demarcated by the soffi t and an abundance of pendant lights, ensuring that the stand stood out in the large hall of other exhibition spaces.

form of a raised plinth for sitting and discussing the products. As the prospective clients discussed the products, they could sit and feel the attributes of the materials that they were perched upon – an opportunity to fully experience the distinct properties of the new surfaces.

Amongst the riot of colours and shapes the wood-grain effect fl ooring was used to create a moment of calmer refl ection in the

Figure 3.65 A raised plinth for seating, fashioned from vinyl, ensures that visitors to the stand get to appreciate fully the attributes of the materials.

![](images/df722ff190848eb3c06bae14c78da08cbeaaa2085e54d43aa72f6cd637b611a1.jpg)

## Mercato

Location Shanghai, China

Remodelled 2012

Designer Neri & Hu Design and Research offi ce

![](images/304934b647374acb3684e3eccbc7f70b50239c53d1331a3ea91155dbcd224393.jpg)

The design is really a play on materials. It’s not so much a stylistic thing. – Neri, Architectural Record, September 2013. <sup>35</sup>

Figure 3.66 The ‘originator’ status of the steel-frame host building has infl uenced the development of the design language, a mixture of both new and old, salvaged and reused materials.

As well as importing new and carefully chosen materials into an existing site, designers remodelling existing buildings will also need to respond to the surfaces of what is already there. Sometimes there is no relationship between the existing and the new materials, but in other circumstances the old can have a direct infl uence on the new surfaces chosen. If a building that is to be reused has a particular history, or past, then the extant surfaces may become an expression of the narratives of these times. In other

approaches to reuse, the surfaces of an existing building may be treated in order to act as scenography or a backdrop for the new, as well as illustrating the activities that are to be undertaken within the space. Whichever methods or processes the designers choose, the response to what is already extant is of paramount importance.

Situated on the sixth fl oor of the seven storey 1916 Union Assurance Building, on the Bund in central Shanghai, the Italian restaurant Mercato serves high-end rustic Italian food to a discerning clientele. For one of the fi rst steel frame buildings in Shanghai, the designers were able to open up the 1,000 square metre interior space to house the reception, cocktail bar, pizza

bar, lounge and main dining area by demolishing a series of non-structural internal partition walls. Without enclosing this new large open space, they carefully organised a series of distinct dining experiences. At the centre of the space they designed a central ‘market’ consisting of a vibrant cocktail and pizza bar, both surrounded by a number of stools, inviting guests to sit and enjoy an informal snack or dinner. Considered as the dynamic heart of the interior, and in order to represent the street market aesthetic of the space, both islands were enclosed by a steel frame, with meshed glass and recycled wood canopies. Around the bars, a network of reclaimed steel was intertwined with the services and ductwork of the interior and provided a system for hanging shelving and lighting, completing the ‘rustic’ and informal ‘street market’ aesthetic appearance of the interior space.

Situated outside of these two ‘market’ spaces were the more formal and sedate lounge, dining area and the private dining rooms. The surfaces of the interior space have been exposed in order to contrast with other restaurants in Shanghai, where luxurious and extravagant materials are often considered to be very important and demonstrate the prosperity of the host. In this project the affl uence of the restaurant is considered to be partly found in the treasures of the existing building.

Figure 3.67 The central ‘market’ space with a bar clad in recycled timber and a meshglass and steel shelving and lighting canopy.

Stripping back the strata of fi nishes that had built up after years of renovations, the design of Mercato celebrated the raw beauty of the bare structural elements of the building. This included the decision to commemorate the originator status of the steel frame by revealing the original beams and columns. Against the textured backdrop of the existing brickwork, concrete, plaster and mouldings, new insertions were clearly demarcated and

then placed. In order to extract the maximum impact between the new and the old elements, second-hand and recycled materials, such as replacing the former spaces terrazzo fl ooring with reclaimed wood from the nearby Anhui province, were prized and maintained. Other salvaged materials were chosen for elements such as the banquettes along the edge of the dining area. The private dining rooms were infi lled with panels of varying materials: reclaimed wood, natural steel, antique mirror, metal mesh and chalk board. All were used in order to engender a relaxed and eclectic aesthetic. A band of textured glass along the top edge of each personal dining room afforded some transparency, whilst sliding doors between each room provided maximum fl exibility. This language continued into the corridor between the kitchen and dining area, where a backlit wall of textured glass panels – inspired by old warehouse windows – encouraged interaction between the chef and their patrons.

In contrast to those seated at the counters, diners seated along the edges of the room experienced a very different sort of ambience. Clad in white travertine, the walls act as a temporary departure from the other rich textures and palettes of the space. The focus was simple, while the breathtaking views of the Bund beyond the building were utilised to draw the far reaches of the city, into the dining space itself.

![](images/c27e7125f719fe706b5d68402b9ef3d487f68a7ece1513f7b5481ee1061529cd.jpg)

Sushi-teria

Location New York, USA

Remodelled 2012

Designer Form-ula

![](images/c6553a90534a82b734e9c87366265d2ec1c219b168ebbfb84eb87e404d3b403d.jpg)

There are a thousand different possibilities in one material alone. – Peter Zumthor, Atmospheres.<sup>36</sup>

Figure 3.68 The pickled, painted pine wall was fashioned to be redolent of the scales of a fi sh.

Unusual applied textured surfaces can be deployed in order to give identity to a particular place and location. Normally, through touch, it is a surface of an element that receives the most human contact. But image is also a signifi cant aspect to be considered when a distinctive material is to be used in the design of an interior space. The same material can be used in myriad ways, yet depending on how it is used, fi nished, and then deployed, it can send very particularised messages about an interior space.

Sushi-teria is a 93 square metre sushi restaurant located in the atrium space of the Citigroup Center in mid-town Manhattan, New York. In order to process the large amount of hungry customers it serves daily in this busy commercial district of the city, the takeaway restaurant utilises an innovative new ordering technology that combines tablet and smart phone apps with bespoke made-to-order sushi. Diners can order either remotely or in-store and then pick up the food, ensuring that they don’t miss a minute of their hectic workday schedules.

The innovative ordering technology ensures that the interior can accommodate a large group of staff, able to deal with the busy times of the day such as the lunchtime rush, when customers require a particularly quick service. This has impacted signifi cantly upon the design of the interior. Over half of the allocated space for the project was designed to accommodate a 14-person workforce in the state-of-the-art kitchens. With the mechanics of the food preparations, and the staffi ng operation solved, the designers could focus on a surface-led strategy with which to make the interior space

memorable for the small amount of time customers may be in it. The designers derived a spatial strategy and organisation that maximised the remaining space for ordering terminals, allowing customers to make their choices of food in-store, but also, and possibly more importantly, communicated the food, and the brand via a set of surface textures. It was considered important that the space was fi nished and detailed in a way that was seen to function as a cultural signifi er or as a trigger for understanding the freshness of the products and the places they came from.

The designers did this in a number of ways. They created a simple and neutral surface for the fl oor of the restaurant by wrapping the ground level in a hard wearing, resistant, hand tooled black Kadappa stone. They contrasted the subdued and muted elegance of this surface by making the surrounding walls from mirrored polished stainless steel. An illuminated shadow gap, between wall and soffi t, reinforced the change in surface from highly refl ective mirrored wall to a pre-painted aluminium-backed suspended ceiling.

These steel and aluminium panels were perforated with a custom pattern that wrapped both wall and ceiling. The pattern was both an abstract rendition of an atmospheric fog, in its expanse of diminishing refl ection of mirror, as well as being reminiscent of a fi sherman’s woven net, hanging from above the customers’ heads. Sitting atop the charcoal grey fl oor, and framed by the refl ective shimmering walls and ceiling, was the serving counter. Clad in reclaimed poplar timber planks, it was designed to be reminiscent of a small boat, moored amongst the iridescent symbolic steel seas of the interior space.

The nautical sea-faring theme was reemphasised by the wall behind the

counter, a screen that framed the view towards the sushi chef area. Set within white subway tiles, 800 individually cut pieces of clear pine were whitewashed, pickled, and then fi nished with a gloss white and pink paint.

The blocks of timber were cut diagonally and then fi xed to the wall to produce a rippling effect, an impression that, at a glance, could be considered to be analogous to the play of light on a morning sea. As customers waited for their takeaway, a longer study of the surface could reveal that it subtly communicated the sensation of looking at the side of a colourful fi sh, as its scales ripple in the water. The subtlety of this symbolic imagery prompts ironic readings from the customers in front of it, as behind this surface the chefs are busy neatly dicing and slicing and preparing fi sh, making sushi for the hungry workers collecting their takeaway lunch.

![](images/27fb6c94a2796e24ad0c64f7911e2c895e7e88d8b0eeeefaa5905226c914e53f.jpg)  
Figure 3.69 Perforated steel and aluminium panels were used to signify a sea mist as well as more prosaically expand the small space in its refl ection.  
Figure 3.70 the simple clean surfaces of the interior indicate the freshness of the products being bought and consumed.

![](images/cfc24894bab3cc88fc92d209087299df55a144f7ac440a08441b69c0873befa8.jpg)

# Fashion Center Building

Location New York, USA

Remodelled 1990

Designer George Ranalli

![](images/987c3cc76b8b3312241b554d8b8f9780a333319da1f56faa5c6a7c7aa6150c69.jpg)

Figure 3.71 The original portico entrance was exposed in the   
renovations. Existing elements were repaired and cleaned.

The transition from an exterior to the interior will often be marked by a signifi cant threshold, one that in some cases will announce the particular character and atmosphere of the inside space the visitor is stepping into. Once the threshold is

traversed, there will often be a holding space, an entrance such as a porch, hall, foyer or lobby. This is usually a space where the visitor can take stock of their surroundings, maybe visit a reception desk if they need to, or take off their coat and leave an umbrella in a stand. Whilst of all these activities are taking place, this sequence of spaces will be introducing the visitor to the interior. Through the particular treatment of space and surface the next sequence of interiors will be signalled to the occupant of the building.

The Fashion Center, a 23 storey Romanesque Revival structure in New York’s historic Garment District, was originally designed by the American Architect, Henry Ives Cobb. Designed in 1924, the structure had undergone a number of changes throughout its existence, and had subsequently had many of its features and details removed – a fate that had befallen many of the buildings in the Garment District throughout the 20th century. In particular the lobby space’s terracotta details and plaster ornamentation had been removed or damaged beyond recognition in their covering up with false suspended ceilings and fake screen walls.

Figure 3.72 New   
lighting was carefully considered and   
discretely hidden in the cornice in order to   
illuminate the   
impressive existing   
ceiling.

Figure 3.73 The edge of gold leaf on the new plaster columns was subtly picked out with the new lighting.

![](images/de3ddbddf7099d640de0cd49e7442103f23d3c9a635a907f43f7643018828ee7.jpg)

Instead of selling the building in the early 1990s, the owners decided that, along with reconfi guring some of the fl oors for a change of use to apartments, they would restore the former glory of this enigmatic entrance space. George Ranalli was commissioned and, rather than start from scratch, he began the project by undertaking a forensic review of the lobby. Much like a detective would examine a case, he set about locating the details of the lost fragments of the interior. Ranalli also undertook a detailed examination of what had survived, piecing together details of reliefs and surfaces and cataloguing their condition through a series of highly detailed drawings. Once the analysis of the space and its fragments was determined, then the evidence could be submitted and used to inform the new restoration.

One of the fi rst interventions that Ranalli undertook on the building was the removal of the front doors. He did this in order to reinstate the original portico, in the past

![](images/92638e478e7a95a97a50a2af65d8f1be3c81976eda9cf6a24279f4dd7a95a837.jpg)

sealed off and hidden behind earlier works to the façade. Drawing on infl uences derived from the previous surfaces of the space, new terrazzo fl ooring was laid. Existing and previously hidden terracotta details, including a frieze of animals, were exposed, cleaned, repaired, and where appropriate copied for new pieces to be made and inserted to replace elements of the existing portico that had been lost or removed. Existing columns and capitals were also repaired or restored. A wrought iron grill, in the arch of the portico, was cleaned and repainted. Subtle lighting, set into the new fl oor, guided the visitor towards a new set of bronze entry doors, and into the main hall.

In the lobby, modern lighting was hidden inside original fi xtures that illuminated the dramatic beauty of the recreated fl oral plaster cast ceiling. New ornamental paired plaster columns were made, and set upon a subtly lit existing frieze. Each column was topped with an architrave edged in gold leaf. The pale ceiling surfaces were contrasted with restored cream and purple marble walls, accented with delicate gold and black edges. A new bronze reception desk, positioned alongside a set of restored bronze window frames, heating grilles, and elevator doors, introduced a striking contemporary element to the old lobby. These elements clearly articulated how the past had infl uenced the new contemporary intervention.

In this project, the reuse of the building has led to the designer researching its past and seeking out inspiration from the lost surfaces of its change through various renovations. Surface has provided an inspiration for the revitalisation of an ordinary entrance space returning some grandeur to the act of journeying from outside in a busy city to the relative calm of the interior lobby.

## Openings

## Introduction

The English word window derives from wind and eye; this etymology helps defi ne the window aperture as a complex instrument that both admits light and ventilation to an interior and enables the eye – that is, one’s vision. – Georges Teyysot, A Topology of Everyday Constellations. <sup>37</sup>

Openings, both imposed and found, impact signifi cantly on the design of the interior. A carefully crafted view through a space, via a series of openings such as doorways or windows, will imply connectivity and promote movement. The delineation of these apertures will often entice the user of the space to move and circulate in an interior, in order to interact with what is being offered through the opening. Therefore it is important to understand that a critical element of the tactic of creating or reusing an opening in an interior is that it is a device that is closely associated with movement.

Once this is understood, openings can be used as rhythmic devices, punctuations in space that can organise circulation through focusing fl ow in an interior space. For instance, in the Canaletto apartment by Morpurgo de Curtis Architetti Associati, three apertures were cut into a screen wall that divided two rooms. One aperture is opaque, and it is a source of light for both spaces; one is a mirror, refl ecting the view of the room back into itself, and one opening allows a very particular framed view of the next room. Movement between the rooms is then facilitated by a doorway, placed under a mezzanine level, a spatial organisation that requires the occupant of the space to advance to the corner of the room. Even in a small space such as this, a series of strategically placed openings ensured that view; fl ow and circulation were tightly controlled, highly complex spatial manoeuvres.

![](images/e1077d6364eeeb2d813709972977ef426fc33306c4444fdf78856598ec2e8a69.jpg)  
Figure 3.74 Canaletto Apartment, Milan, Italy by Morpurgo de Curtis Architetti.

The considered use of devices such as openings can enable the designer to heighten the senses of the user of the space. This approach can sometimes increase the tension of the visitor in a space through subtle means. This can be done in a number of ways but will often revolve around the development of the threshold between different environments. In the Cathedral Museum in Milan, Guido Canali utilised the rooms and corridors of the existing building in which to create an incredible journey that unfolded as a

Figure 3.75 Cathedral Museum, Milan, Italy. Guido Canali.

![](images/78fa67f68ed8b9d544d8e17481842bf25ee3526835ed31ce341aa4fe1898c067.jpg)

sequence of movements through a series of spaces.

Various constraints, not least the deep and complex plan of the building, meant that the route would often need to double back on itself, through the same rooms that the visitor had traversed earlier, in order to make the museum work. Canali deployed a very sophisticated composition of objects and views, chronologically arranged, and facilitated by a series of openings, such as doorways and apertures. These were positioned to offer very specifi c views, through the interior space. The visitor would often journey through a room at least twice. Sometimes they would view a space, yet wouldn’t immediately access it, as they were circulated elsewhere via guiding balustrades or display vitrines. This method of movement through the space heightened the tension of this journey, especially as Canali used these views in a variety of ways, and combined with more straightforward access points into the journey through the museum.

Objects such as long friezes, transported from the cathedral outside, would be placed in a room that consisted of a series of thin tall openings. Other fragments of buildings would be placed into infi lled arched openings. All of these tactics considerably increased the sense of intrigue, as elements such as the frieze appeared to travel through the wall. This approach allowed the visitor to view into the next room, yet required them to move in a counter-intuitive circulation pattern, outside the room, in order to physically access it. The sensation of moving away from the space that the visitor wanted to access heightened the tension and intensifi ed the experience of moving through interior space. When this pattern of movement was undertaken numerous times, as it was in this museum, the openings were used to create very focused and atmospheric interiors.

In reuse there is always this coincidence of things that seem incompatible. – Superuse. <sup>38</sup>

As objects in their own right, the reuse of windows and doors provides a very different view on the tactic of openings. Bureau A took this idea to its extreme when they created a garden pavilion, entitled la fabrique, from salvaged doors and windows. The garden folly was inspired by Buster Keaton’s film One Week in which a newly married couple attempts to build a kit-house on a plot of land. The arguments and disorganisation that ensue became a metaphor for the project, mostly in the emphasis placed upon the inconsistencies arising from salvaged elements, and the difficulties faced when trying to reconcile these differences into a coherent whole in one built object. As Bureau A designer Daniel Zamarbide stated:

La Fabrique is built with a similar mixture of seriousness and lightness. The pavilion made out of recycled windows found on demolition sites is designed and built by BUREAU A and the inhabitants in a couple days. The lightness comes from the direct relation between thinking and doing.

– Daniel Zamarbide, quoted in Dezeen. <sup>39</sup>

Whichever way openings, apertures, doors and windows are deployed, their strategic and tactical use will ensure the designer creates intense sequence and movement in an interior space.

Figure 3.76 La Fabrique, Bureau A.

![](images/e87c811a235c6a48ec0caad2f5dc220739d5eda12c333b420ba22d3932c3dbb0.jpg)

# Natural History Museum

Location Berlin, Germany

Remodelled 2012

Designer Diener & Diener Architects

![](images/e0a85fba50141c99a3b8d76032abfccc69fd23ebe3680a2079fdc5db7de91f41.jpg)

Like any other instrument (including language), the window is an apparatus that separates and unites.

– Georges Teyssot. The Topology of Everyday Constellations. <sup>40</sup>

Figure 3.77 The ruined East wing.

Openings in a building’s façade, such as a window, are usually made in order to facilitate light entering a space, and to increase the possibility of fresh air being invited into the interior. Windows are often considered vital when organising the design of a building, and a particular view enclosing a specifi c point or vista can make the interior space unique, as well as connected to the outside world. When the specifi c characteristics of a window are removed, and light and air are considered harmful to the contents of the interior, then the tactics of making openings within what is extant takes on a new and unusual signifi cance in the remodelling of existing buildings.

The Berlin Natural History museum has existed since 1889, but in the early

20th century its East wing was shattered by a direct hit from a bomb. The resultant destruction of the building left just a shell of the wing extant, with few sections of the outer walls remaining. In the latter part of the century a competition to renew the ruined wing was arranged, but due to fi nancial constraints it took many years for the winners Diener & Diener’s ideas to be fully realised.

The original East wing was the host of a large collection consisting of 276,000 zoological specimens. These were housed in differently sized glass containers, all of which were fi lled with 70% proof alcohol to preserve the samples. The collection was deemed highly sensitive, and had to be stored in a dark, carefully controlled climatic condition.

The specimens are very important and are still studied as signifi cant exemplars of natural history by the scientists of the museum. Therefore the samples needed to be accessible to the scientists and at the same time viewable by museum visitors,

and the design brief stipulated that their conservation was of utmost importance.

The contradiction of displaying a collection of sensitive specimens in a visible yet environmentally controlled environment was not lost on the designers. In response to these constraints, the winning entry achieved these conditions by rebuilding the East wing on three fl oors, each with ceilings of up to six metres high, of which only the fi rst fl oor was open to visitors. The wood and glass shelves of the display cabinets extend from fl oor-to-ceiling but can only be viewed from the outside by the public. The design allowed the scientists to carry on their work amongst the samples inside the display room, separated by walls of glass vitrines fi lled with the specimens preserved in alcohol. Lighting was installed in order to emphasise the dramatic effect of the spectacle of this relationship between viewer, object and worker.

The rooms housing the collection were conceived and constructed as closed cement containers, as though precious jewel boxes were inserted inside the building protecting the contents. In order to heighten this sense of an interior treasure box, the interior walls were painted dark red, similar in colour to the velvet used to line caskets.

Fig. 3.78 In-fi lled windows imitated the existing windows of the wing. Subtle variation in colouring illustrated the distinction between what was new and what was existing.  
![](images/977622bd4faecc779c0247dfb02b03b76f38f69171770935487efb95b3b3f039.jpg)

In order to protect the sensitive contents of the interior, the designers constructed an exterior that was directly infl uenced by the dimensions and details of the ruined walls of the East wing, but which would effi ciently control the climate and atmospheric conditions of the space. They did this by rebuilding the demolished walls of the wing, carefully mending and contrasting the new walls with the old, and copying the scale and proportions of the existing with new-build.

In this reconstructed wing the designers chose to imitate the openings of the demolished walls, and removed the normal light- and air-affording properties of the windows. They did this by infi lling the existing openings with brick and then, where necessary, created new windows from cement that replicated the exact details of the old. An extremely subtle variation in the colour of the brick, for the new façades of the wing, was used to signal the intervention of the new-building into the old. This ensured that a reading of the history of the building had been inscribed into the construction of its façades. The new window design resisted this reading; instead the bricked-in sections look more recent whilst the preserved windows appear older. The reconstructed sections, replete with windows and frames (albeit in cement) appear somewhat older. The colouring of the new and old sections compounded the confusion that arose from this reading, with the grey-brown bricks which were used to repair the old sections standing out, whilst the grey cement for the new was deliberately subdued. This confused situation was highlighted by the

Figure 3.79 The glass-jar specimens, dramatically lit in the windowless, climatically controlled exhibition space.

designers who state:

In spite of the rather archaic appearance of the façade cast in concrete, this is not an approach based on an early version of the building and left incomplete. Rather the new building is about the old one on the same site, recalling it by retracing it in concrete down to the profi led wooden slats. It does not reject but preclude it, that’s why its effect is quite different. <sup>41</sup>

The silicone moulded brick wall elevations weighed up to 45 tonnes. The distinction between what is new and what is old has been reduced to the bare minimum of material and its colour. The openings or apertures for the windows symbolised the confl ation between both new and old conditions of the historical building, as well as satisfying the production of an interior condition that carefully controlled the protection and viewing of the sensitive and valuable collection of specimens.

![](images/f3ce20ff2bf2c03d13cf00b76abe48d6e14517bbb94ebaa88ec2765c5a0ca17b.jpg)

24 Issey Miyake

Location Sapporo, Japan

Remodelled 2011

Designer MOMENT

![](images/5f52701e58962452d7bb50d103988d38d990f28763bd2c6c1e4e64570fde0bfd.jpg)

Retail architecture is the spatial expression of the envisioned relationship between customers and shop staff.

– Sophie Dubuisson-Quellier, Boutiques and Other Retail Spaces: The Architecture of Seduction. <sup>42</sup>

Figure 3.80 Openings, in the form of cabinet drawers, are utilised to refl ect the everchanging needs of a small fashion retail store.

Openings, or elements that can open, can provide fl exibility in an interior space. This variability in how interiors can be confi gured lends itself to the ability to change the appearance of an interior, sometimes quite frequently. The ability to refl ect change, along with the capability to express the brand and the relationship between customers and the objects they are consuming, is considered central to the design of any retail environment. The space needs to be fl exible in order to refl ect the variations in style and fashion, and can also be updated quickly to display seasonal garments and accessories, objects that will alter daily as new stock arrives.

24 Issey Miyake is a small multi-brand store, selling a range of goods such as garments and bags. The display of the merchandise is highly controlled and it is carefully deployed based on the season of the garments and the location of the store. Therefore quick change and adaptability becomes a very important consideration for the design and the subsequent success of each 24 space. The complexity of the requirements of organising, distributing and merchandising stock was contrasted by the simplicity of the layout of the store.

This was echoed in the design of a straightforward display system, one that consisted of a series of display walls that echoed a number of modular panels, or cabinet draw fronts. Depending on what objects were to be displayed in the store, the panels could be confi gured in three ways. They could be opened to provide a shelf for the display of a folded garment or bag; they could be adapted to house a hanger or to suspend a garment; they could be closed to fold seamlessly back into the wall, ultimately suggesting that a garment or bag no longer existed and was out of season or stock.

The numbered panels echoed the range of stock. They were symbolic of the timebased qualities of the 24 brand, itself an implicit reminder of the chronological dimension of the passing seasons of fashion design and production.

Figure 3.81 The store when closed.  
![](images/46446cb2edf9bbb54a2034a0951e36f0c63afd6488e745eb0f30b0174f423570.jpg)

## FNS Apartment

Location Berlin, Germany

Remodelled 2010

Designer Reinhardt Jung

![](images/49bfceb0d457309dc57f98c0301ae19dc6b5157ca12cb0026db9871c20562fb6.jpg)

Openings create relationships between people and things. They do this by both physically and visually connecting people, spaces and objects, forming and sometimes reinforcing interactions between all types of human activity in a number of ways and in differing environments. In domestic spaces, the delineation of openings becomes hugely signifi cant. They will reinforce or obscure distinctions between the public and private aspects of the home. Openings such as windows and doorways, along with walls that have openings within them, can be organised and constructed in such a way as to either separate or connect the many forms of occupation that are being undertaken within them. This can be done in a permanent or a temporal fashion. Historically, domesticity was undertaken in one open space, with little or no recourse to

Figure 3.82 Plan: Accessed from the communal stair, both apartments were confi gured in a way that combined both existing and new openings.

private or separate spaces. Subsequently walls have been constructed to divide the home into specialised areas. In more recent times it may be argued that developments in technology and our changing social relationships, as well as a change in attitudes towards privacy and societal structures, have encouraged a return to living in one large space. However we consider our domestic arrangements, openings, technology and furniture service and facilitate our public and private needs in our living spaces.

In order to accommodate maximum fl exibility in a domestic space, and explore new ways of inhabiting a home, Reinhardt Jung carefully analysed the requirements of the clients of two neighbouring apartments they were commissioned to design in central Berlin. They surmised that the

clients needed maximum fl exibility in their spaces and a signifi cant level of adaptability in the way that they could confi gure the apartment. They needed this in order to accommodate the particular way in which they wanted to live.

The apartments were located in a central Berlin Grü nderzeithouse, a style of late 19th century apartment block, characterised by historicist façades and built for the workers of the newly industrialised city. Both apartments were accessed from a central communal stair. The smaller of the two apartments offered more scope for innovation in the way that the clients would inhabit the space. Within what were essentially two well-proportioned rooms of the block, the designers created a fl uid interior that contained spaces for living, sleeping, and the preparation and consumption of food, as well as the normal provision for services such as a bathroom and a shower.

All of these activities within the interior were facilitated through the insertion of a freestanding furniture scaled element containing the services of the space such as a bathroom, shower, and on its other side storage in the form of a wardrobe. The centralisation of all of these services allowed the interior to be free of clutter and mess. Separating and at the same time uniting the front and back spaces of the apartment, and in turn allowing a degree of fl exibility between private and public space, the designers constructed a large rotating wall. This was a screen that when in operation created a series of openings between the private sleeping spaces of the apartment and the social sides of the rooms. In this interior a screen wall based opening has been utilised in order to create a fl exible aperture that can be fi lled or opened and which blurs the boundaries between living, eating, sleeping and abluting.

Figure 3.83 Private and public areas in the smaller apartment were separated by a large movable screen wall.  
![](images/981b1884677011bbea904e82035af64ed41eab9257218b3f9ecda592518c6fdc.jpg)

## Black Pearl

Location Rotterdam, the Netherlands

Remodelled 2012

Designer   
Studio Rolf.fr in partnership with   
Zecc Architecten

![](images/51c7b32d50db6e7c6e45ffe39977f50cde6c4245541d7128c471da7cc5d9aa9f.jpg)

![](images/ef434282a948ee79b72001b9044a52c94718f906f36f1db425e7a1d4a173496f.jpg)

Figure 3.84 New, sharply delineated, deep openings were juxtaposed in a provocative fashion against the existing black ‘shadow’ of the façade.

Figure 3.85 The walk-in closet and bathroom enclosed in an enigmatic fl oating box.

Light enters through the window. The interior maintains its intimacy. The exterior landscape is a painting on the wall whose frame is that of the window. – Elias Torres, Zenithal Light. <sup>43</sup>

Openings can demarcate the thresholds between inside and outside. A door or a window will separate as well as connect both interior and exterior environments. A window will admit light as well as allow a view from both within and outside. This view will change throughout the day, and at night will offer a very particular aspect of the life of the inside of the space. Through providing a boundary between interior and exterior environments, openings can

initiate movement, and establish rhythm in a space via the framing of a view that can invite the beginning of a journey, from outside to inside and vice versa. Whichever way openings are utilised, their realisation and focus is often accompanied by an ambiguity, a spatial opacity that confers importance upon what can be seen beyond their delineating qualities.

Black Pearl was a project initiated and designed by Rolf Bruggink to create a studio and a home for himself in a suburb of Rotterdam. Based in an abandoned ‘Metier’ dwelling, a run-down, turn-of-thecentury, ex-municipality house located in an ungentrifi ed suburb of the city, Studio

Rolf was invited by the council to purchase and convert the three storey building into one large house. Acquiring the 180 square metre building for the derisory sum of €35,000, Bruggink eviscerated the interior of the former three-apartment building, creating a large fi ve metre wide, ten metre long and 11 metre high volume within the existing walls of the building: making a large open space behind the enigmatic façade.

Acting as both client and designer, Bruggink could fashion the space to his own exacting requirements. The lower fl oors contained a studio and workshop for Bruggink. Large doors opened onto the street. At the back of the house another set of doors opened into a small courtyard garden, fi lled with bamboo and slate tiles from the roof of the building before it was renovated, scattered across the fl oor. A long stair, placed against a whitewashed brick wall ascended to the fi rst fl oor living and dining spaces. The study and bedroom was placed on the second level, and was accessed by a stair placed against the opposite wall; one that was left completely uncovered, exposing the wear and tear of the building and the scars of its history of uses and subsequent transformation. The upper level was arranged around a walk-in closet and bathroom, formed into a sculptural cube that appeared to be suspended in the space. A private stair from this level, positioned back against the whitewashed wall, lead to a rooftop terrace and a bathroom, provocatively positioned in a glass house. This allowed views across the surrounding garden and the rooftops of the adjacent houses from the centrally placed jacuzzi.

Figure 3.86 In stark contrast to the serious black façade, the gable end of the building was clad playfully in artifi cial grass.

In order to signal the new use to the street, Studio Rolf changed the external appearance of the building by reworking both the gable end and façade of the structure. The gable was covered in artifi cial grass and new glazed openings were set within it. The façade was rendered in black shiny oil, covering the windows and any details of the building. This suppressing of the architectural features created what Bruggink referred to as a ‘shadow’ of the original façade. All of the brickwork, fenestration, and window details, including the frame, transoms, mullions and ledges, were silhouetted in black. With little regard for the existing fenestration, new apertures were brutally cut into the façade. Overlapping with the existing windows, the new interior spaces were externalised through the new openings. The new windows announced a very different way of living, and created a dynamic relationship between the original façade and the new interpretation of the building.

In this project the old and the new were juxtaposed in a straightforward manner, with a series of powerful and confi dent new openings incised upon the fabric of the building.

![](images/ff4c252dd8e18ce8042c0ae16fd608a4f76c610af218439f6f5637791fb13c03.jpg)

## Movement

## Introduction

Throughout the 20th century, the stair as an element of architecture has maintained the importance which has been attributed to it since the Renaissance. Such a status still derives from a combination of utility, or functional considerations, with an inherent aesthetic potential in terms of both the articulation of the structural form, and its further enhancement. – Michael Spens, Staircases. <sup>44</sup>

Stairs, corridors, bridges, lifts, escalators, walkways, ramps; there are numerous ways in which movement will take place in the built environment and in the interior. Whether public or private space, all of these elements facilitating movement, circulation, route and journey, will have been carefully considered and designed to facilitate their particular function in an effi cient, safe and economical manner.

Like all of the six tactics explained in this chapter, movement will often be considered in conjunction with other tactical ideas. Objects placed in specifi c spaces will be organised in relation to how people may circulate around them. Planes such as fl oors, walls and ceilings will be designed to accommodate fl ow, as will surfaces, openings and the articulation of light. Therefore it is important to note that movement and the elements that facilitate it are one part of the tactical jigsaw that the designer of inside space is required to assemble.

Figure 3.87 BUDA Arts Centre, Kortrijk, Belgium. 51N4E.

The design of a bespoke stair is one of the most invigorating challenges for a designer of the interior. Whilst appearing simplistic, its key elements, combined with the relevant code and regulations prescribing the correct measurements for tread, riser and balustrade, provide a multitude of ways of realisation of what can be a mundane journey between fl oors or an epic fl ight of fancy between spaces. Whichever way stairs are utilised, their design and their placement in the space will provide the designer with not just the movement they require but also an opportunity to articulate space through the rhythmic journey that an ascent or decent of a staircase can provide. This can be seen in both the BUDA art Centre by 51N4E and in the Caixa Forum in Madrid by Herzog & de Meuron, where the quite differently constructed stairs were each utilised in order not only to celebrate the spaces they were in but to offer very particular and distinct views through the interior environments in which they were moving.

The design of lifts and escalators are the overlooked elements of interior movement. Often, off-the-peg or readymade solutions are the workhorses of mostly large public interiors such as airports, shopping centres and train stations. These are environments where large numbers of people need to be processed effi ciently and safely. Occasionally they will be deployed with some thought in their design and positioning.

![](images/b69e02a5fc83dcd4ce68c96a8bfb54183546476186074815142f58b7b8a9f32a.jpg)

The unforgettable escalators of the Lloyds building in London, by the Richard Rogers Partnership, provided a metaphorical focus for the atrium of the banking building. The exposed cogs and wheels of the workings and mechanics of the robotic stairs signalled to the traders using them how the fi nancial markets they were manipulating constantly fl uctuated like the stairs moving up and down between fl oors.

As soon as [architecture] is built it becomes the scene and sometimes the scenario of comings and goings, of gestures even, even of a succession of sensations.

– Pierre Von Meiss. Elements of Architecture. <sup>45</sup>

Walkways, bridges, ramps and corridors facilitate circulation and movement between spaces in a way to articulate sequence and in some cases heighten sensation. In the Kunsthal in Rotterdam by Rem Koolhaas, the floor of the side gallery was articulated in a steel mesh, creating a journey across the space that was not for the faint-hearted (or for the wearer of a kilt or a skirt). The large open squares of the mesh dematerialised the floor, collapsing the distinctions between solidity and floating, as viewers in the gallery below would have a view up through the extremely tall space of the quadruple height gallery.

Whichever movement tactic is used, it does not need to be banal or mundane. Movement through a space, when articulated with thought and creativity, can make space exhilarating and dramatic.

![](images/dbcf8420848226045109cc920131322c47efaeea57be778571d5144735505809.jpg)  
Figure 3.88 Lloyds Building, London, UK. Richard Rogers Partnership.

## Casa C Apartment

Location Milan, Italy

Remodelled 2010

Designer Francesco Librizzi

![](images/a369bae94b516ec22ab79dc8f5b590d39ddcb8522f89a89ca672387f2573cca1.jpg)

One of the key components of movement in interior space is the staircase. Its carefully considered balance of proportion and geometry, combined with stringent regulatory requirements and codes, is often the result of design and the combination of four key elements: risers and treads, stringers, and a balustrade. Their combination will lend the space, as well as the user, a particular interior rhythm. Whether off-the-peg or bespoke, lightweight or monumentally heavy, it is an element of interior space that has fascinated designers for a long time. Hence its appearance can undertake myriad forms even when constructed from a few simple elements.

Experiments with this type of vertical movement through a space have often revolved around the complex mix of imagination with the harsh realities of the relevant building regulations. This combination of poetry and pragmatics can vary, depending on the user, and whether in a public or a private space. For instance, the design of stairs in a domestic space can take on a different trajectory as their public duties are relinquished, leaving them freer of some of the more burdensome regulatory requirements of particular measurements of riser, tread and balustrade. Therefore domestic space stairs can sometimes prove to be the elements of movement in the interior where experimentation and imagination can really take fl ight.

Figure 3.89 As the user descends from the mezzanine to the lower level they are neatly framed within the slender lightweight steel stair sculpture.

In a small, 45 square metre apartment in Milan, Francesco Librizzi has experimented with a stair that gives its user access to an upper level mezzanine sleeping space. The intimate proportions of the apartment were contrasted with the high ceilings of the space. A full height slot in the wall, separating the two main rooms of the space, suggested an opportunity to utilise the upper heights to gain more space for the occupant. Therefore a mezzanine, providing a sleeping room, was placed above the kitchen. Access to it provided the next challenge.

The designer developed a slender lightweight vertical steel stair, linking the lower fl oor and the new sleeping level. The proportions and geometry of the stair refl ected the simple confi guration of the space of the apartment. Librizzi pared back the basic elements of the stair; (tread, riser, balustrade, and stringer) to become as economical as was possible in their barest essentials. He then reordered them into a tall and slender steel frame, constructed with a 14mm solid section steel bar. The thin treads were welded to a stringer, one that also diagonally braced the box, connecting to a landing and affording the slender construction some stability as it connected back to the wall. Each tread connected to a side bar that reinforced the other side of the frame. The side bracing carried onto the top of the frame, reinforcing the solidity of the construction and lending some stability, as well as something more reassuring to grab onto, as the user ascends the stairs to the mezzanine level and to the bed.

The stair was fi xed to the fl oor and to the ceiling. This afforded the object some structural stability as it transfered its user from the landing to the mezzanine. When in use, the dynamism of the occupant of the apartment is palpable as they carefully and deliberately ascend or descend the stair. The slender, deliberate and economically rational stair neatly frames the space and the user in a static, thin frame of this adroitly considered sculptural construction.

## Tate Britain

Location London, UK

Remodelled 2013

Designer Caruso St John

![](images/703be9499e5a28666697dc75efe20490d2b0fc24b6c3134b88e7362163445c4f.jpg)  
Figure 3.90 The circular stair sweeps gracefully down into the basement.

The practice’s brilliance is to analyse a particular sense of place, sift these traditions, then synthesise a solution that draws on diversity but hangs together naturally. This is possible only with a thorough knowledge of the basic grammar that allows complete architectural sentences to be structured and goes beyond the decorative to the vital circulation decisions that restore coherence to the experience of the building.

– Robert Bevan, Building Design.<sup>46</sup>

Over time, buildings can become illegible. Additions and changes to a place’s working life will often include adjustments with regards to how it is used. These amendments will alter its patterns of occupation – particularly when circulation systems such as stairs, ramps and lifts are added to increase accessibility and the use of an interior space. Other alterations will facilitate updates on the existing, pragmatic concerns such as addressing out of date services, or making buildings more accessible and bringing them into line with changes to building regulations. However alterations change a building, their impact is always upon the overall comprehension of a building. Over a long period of time these changes will impact on the building to such a degree that a series of robust interventions will need to be carried out in order to re-clarify the space, and ensure the building is fi t for purpose. The design and construction of new circulation, including elements that facilitate movement, will often form an intrinsic part of this type of new remodelling.

Caruso St John were appointed as architects for Tate Britain in 2007. The long-term commission involved the development of a masterplan for the whole of the Tate’s Millbank site. The masterplan was conceived as a two-fold approach: it envisaged a major new extension on the adjacent Queen Alexandra’s Military Hospital site, to accommodate a larger proportion of the Tate’s historic, modern and contemporary collection of British art, and it advocated a continuing and robust refurbishment and improvement plan for the existing building.

Completed in November 2013, the fi rst stage of the plan involved work to the

Figure 3.91   
The advent of 21st   
century digital   
practices means that it was possible to create a complex decorative pattern within the threedimensional structure of the staircase.

![](images/8b91cec27932d4a594e9348be598e8422ee9b9f3fa3ebe68db096be3243a2218.jpg)

existing galleries, and to the public amenities around the rotunda at the Thameside Millbank building entrance. Galleries within the south quadrant, the oldest galleries of the building, have been repaired and restored, whilst integrating new sustainable environmental control systems. Circulation spaces around the rotunda at the ground, main fl oor and fi rst fl oor have been opened up, with the insertion of a new stair as well as lifts connecting all levels of the interior. New members’ facilities at the upper level, and a new café at the lower level, complemented the facilities at the Manton entrance.

In the rotunda a new staircase was conceived and designed that connected the principal fl oor with the lower levels of the building. A surprisingly contemporary intervention amongst the grandeur of the historic dome, the stair facilitates circulation between the main entrance, the Duveen Galleries, and the café, restaurant and archive gallery in the lower fl oors. The circular stair was forcefully imposed, centrally in the space. It is edged at the upper level by a pierced reinforced concrete balustrade, fi nished with white Carrara and black marble, refl ecting the scalloped monochromatic fl oor from which it emerges. As the stair swoops down into the basement, a translucent glass balustrade, held in place by a polished stainless steel frame, reinforces the sinuous curves of the sculptural staircase. As well as other changes to the building, the reinstatement of a key moment in the circulation of the building has returned a clarity and coherence to an old building from which logic and lucidity had departed.

## Hankai House

Location Japan

Remodelled 2010

Designer Katsuhiro Miyamoto & Associates

![](images/359c84caa6f16de58031fe3df631cba5660350ccb29bcf746bd1ea2df1ca3763.jpg)

The ramp is an instant creator of scenography. It strives towards architectural infi nity. It generates mythical proportion…it directs the view skywards, leaving unknown what is at the end of the road.

– Koolhaas, Elements of Architecture.<sup>47</sup>

Figure 3.92 The junction between the new addition and the old building was left brutally exposed, enhancing the juxtaposition between them.

The Japanese word Hankai can be translated into English in a number of ways. It can be used to describe a partial understanding of something or it is used to depict something that is ‘semi-civilised’. In this project Katsuhiro Miyamoto used the word to illustrate the ‘partial destruction’ of a building, utilising it in order to give the semi-demolished building on the site a title that encapsulated its own demise, whilst at the same time signifying its survival and a possible future new role after the disaster that had befallen it.

The existing building was a traditional 300-year-old wooden gatehouse that was partially destroyed in the Great Hanshin earthquake of 1995. Its ruined status ensured that any changes to the building needed to be robust and at the same time needed to restructure the building in order to halt its collapse and demise. As well as saving the building from collapse the new additions needed to ensure its future survival by protecting it against any further earthquakes. To achieve this, Miyamoto fi rst of all stripped away the extraneous elements that had grown up around the original square building. He did this in order to reinstate the original orthogonal and traditional four-room home.

Figure 3.93 Views from the ramp frame the old building.  
![](images/f163dbb50a9d6df4526c78e6915803085c9762c4fe0d73bd22e94710acd7ede9.jpg)

In order to stabilise the house, and then more importantly to ensure it would survive any future seismic activities, Miyamoto wrapped the old building with a new volume. The construction acted like a ‘collar’, or a beam that rings the old building and reinforces and protects it from any lateral or vertical movement. Whilst acting as a structural device, the new insertion also solved some of the access and servicing requirements that the old building lacked. The new element contained new circulation and services such as bathrooms and storage. On the top level, where it was separated from the original house, it provided a terrace and a private room. Both overlooked the street below. The new wing followed the exterior boundary of the old property and created a new gatehouse, reinstating the original function of the ancient building by creating a new boundary threshold.

Whilst the memories of the Hanshin earthquake may fade, the house contained the effects of the disaster in its reconstructed self. In this project the utilisation of a ramp that wrapped the building, as well as stabilising it, helped to restore a sense of domestic protection, whilst at the same time ensuring that the occupant was aware of the volatile nature of the environment in which they were housed. The new home was once again made habitable by a new structural device containing circulation and services that were nonetheless haunting reminders that a rapid destruction of the spaces in which they feature could take place at any time.

Figure 3.94 The new ramp was utilised as a ‘collar’ around the existing building, adding space for services whilst stabilising the building against any future seismic activities.

![](images/3036ea78871fdf432e1fd42a1f9176db9ee376ec393eefa28a3918317d486d68.jpg)

## BAYSIXTY6

Location London, UK

Remodelled 2012

Designer Brinkworth

![](images/4c00905623d6e3cdf76e76780bcb946e6f7242a3eca04775bc93f550dcc67fd7.jpg)

The interior is often designed from the point of view of the occupant, as they traverse its spaces. But movement in the interior does not always have to be considered from the perspective of an occupant being on foot.
