Figure 3.95 The cavernous skatepark underneath the huge expanse of the Westway above.

The legendary BAYSIXTY6 Skate Park has occupied a unique location under the Westway fl yover in London since the late 1990s. But whilst the world of skating and the design of parks has since moved on and become more complex and sophisticated in their demands, BAYSIXTY6 did not. In order to ensure that the much-loved park continued to inspire future riders, it needed to change. For Adam Brinkworth, a keen skateboarder, it was a dream job.

Appointed by brand/event agency Urb-Orbis on behalf of Nike, Brinkworth were presented with a unique opportunity to help resurrect one of London’s most iconic skate parks: updating it for a new generation of skaters. Challenged to develop a scheme that encouraged a spirit of inclusion and community, Brinkworth reworked the complete layout of the site to offer the best possible skateable area, whilst improving the amenities offered to enhance the experience for both skaters and nonskaters alike.

Consolidating the new ramps and obstacles to a single area of the park ensured that the expanse of the site could be celebrated, championing the park’s core activity – skating. Importantly, lines of sight were designed to be uninterrupted across the entire park, save for the impressive structure of the Westway supports, maximising the visibility for spectators during competitions and demos and at the same time also ensuring safety for the participants.

Brinkworth worked closely with one of the world’s best timber skate park designers and builders, Skatepark Amsterdam (NDSM), who were tasked by Urb-Orbis to design, develop and build a set of ramps and obstacles. To achieve this, a number of unique features were specifi cally produced, catering for a broad range of skaters across all levels of skill and ability. A hydraulic box allowed the height of one to be adjusted at the push of a button – meaning it could be set to suit any level. Another was a new kicker ramp, directly in front of a viewing window, offering spectators the chance to catch a glimpse of boarders as they fl ew past. And an impressive bowl featuring innovative hexagonal corner panels offered the smoothest and most fl uid ride possible.

To complement the vast improvements in the skateable areas, Brinkworth developed the facilities to include a shop and a canteen that doubled up as an educational space, housed within brightly painted green shipping containers. These were positioned to back onto the ramps with a window framing sightlines down the spine of the skate park making it an ideal shelter for spectators in any weather conditions.

These new facilities were strategically positioned around a central courtyard – a communal zone clad in recycled decking and containing bench seating, for visitors to congregate to socialise or unite as part of organised events. The clean and utilitarian space was intended to be a fl exible venue, and a blank canvas to host events such as premier skate fi lms or exhibitions.

The shop, developed by Brinkworth specifi cally for BAYSIXTY6, mixed concrete wall panels and walnut shelves to offer a sophisticated pallette, not regularly associated with skate shops, further refl ecting Nike’s commitment to elevating the overall perception of the park.

One of the most iconic parts of Brinkworth’s scheme was a graphic approach to lighting throughout the site. The creative yet functional application of fl uorescent tubes followed the contours of the Westway’s dramatic concrete underbelly overhead.

BAYSIXTY6 was transformed into an inclusive and stimulating skate park, animated by the numerous riders who use it daily – ensuring its legacy will continue to inspire generations and attract more and more skaters to the ramps and facilities.

# National Archaeological Museum

Location Siena, Italy

Remodelled 2003

Designer Guido Canali

![](images/1be339f03af5e814437d36513de0dd5aa006c6cbdf07dd03ac3b1afa2ae5b381.jpg)

Movement through interior space can be arranged in order to articulate a particular narrative of a series of objects and spaces. This can often be the case in the design of exhibitions, where storytelling regarding the objects, and sometimes the space they are in, needs to be communicated to the visitor in a very particular manner.

Figure 3.96 Roughhewn, unfi nished scaffold boards were used to articulate the new route through the subterranean spaces.

In the centre of the city of Siena, directly opposite the cathedral, is the Santa Maria Della Scala. It was one of the fi rst hospitals in Europe and until recently one of the three original hospital buildings still caring for the sick and poor. According to legend it was founded in 898 AD, however the fi rst recorded documents mentioning it were in 1090. Since medieval times the building has been a hospital for pilgrims. Over time the complex of buildings has grown, and over the many centuries it has existed it has absorbed adjacent buildings, overfl owing into neighbouring courtyards and interiors. In the past, as it colonised this part of the city, it was even extended underground, carving out tunnels deep into the tufa rock on which the city was built, forming an underground citadel with its own internal street.

In 1992 Canali won the competition to create a new museum of archaeology .The daunting task of raising the fi nances to undertake such a demanding project, and to unpick the labyrinth of the extant building, meant that work did not commence on the project until 1998. The work also involved moving the hospital,

Figure 3.97 Plan of the museum with its internal street and the subterranean galleries.

Figure 3.98 The sectional perspective demonstrates the complex hillside location of the building and the underground rooms.

which was still in operation, and developing a particular conservation strategy in line with the installation of the new archaeological museum, art gallery, temporary exhibition spaces, archive, and services such as catering and offi ces that the new museum would require.

Fittingly, the new archaeological museum was arranged amongst the labyrinthine spaces of the underground chambers, tunnels and rooms of the hospital. Canali relied on the heavy, atmospheric qualities of the rooms and tunnels, leaving them more or less untouched. To form the backdrop for the placement of the museum objects he installed subtle yet distinctive lighting. This was in order to ensure that the walls physically conveyed the weight and density of the rock and earth excavated to forge the new subterranean spaces. Canali left them as found, and more importantly, as a testament to the endeavours over the years that the hospital had existed. In amongst the subterranean spaces Canali inserted a fl oating fl oor.

Constructed from simple scaffold boards, rough-hewn and unfi nished, the boards matched the intensity of the surrounding walls, yet they clearly delineate a new sense of purpose for the visitor to proceed through the interior. This clever tactic also acted as a device to house wiring and services, ensuring that the walls were as free as possible from services and able to direct the journeys of the museum-goer as the stories of the objects and the spaces unfolded before them.

![](images/5510c24650beb8763931c91ee6b988a4a29870c0b322faf4f5202316164c4d39.jpg)

![](images/ea651d14c72fc77d00ad2d0f91aa2ba30ac354d69ae29b610ba024346482fabb.jpg)

![](images/977e2cea35ce001576e55fdee31b5bd19d99f6e12941b223c2901893a889e80d.jpg)

![](images/db8136affe665efd868ae4df241bf8506de485bd53922c3a38c3a5187813e371.jpg)

## Chapter 4: Case Studies

“The use and reuse of an architectural site creates a direct connection not just with the present, but also with the past. It is a strategy that establishes an explicit relationship with context, not just of the building and its immediate surroundings, but also with the society that constructed it. The reading of a building or site can uncover a layered and stratified narrative.”

## FRAC

Location Dunkirk, France

Former function Prefabrication workshop

New function FRAC (Fonds régionaux d’art contemporain)

Original date 1945   
Remodelled 2013

Designer Lacaton & Vassa

![](images/2be8938621cc407eed1926f87aa8b37de4ac6ef4653f251f6db5af87a30f7fa4.jpg)

Figure 4.2 The ‘twins’’ role as the   
accommodator of the support services of the institution meant that the original AP2 could become a huge fl exible and reprogrammable art space.

FRAC is a network of regional cultural institutions that were formed to collect and display contemporary art from across France. Between them, the 23 regions have acquired over 25,000 works of art that they loan to museums and schools. In recent years an ambitious scheme to develop purpose built facilities across France has resulted in numerous exemplary buildings. The Nord-Pas-de-Calais FRAC in Dunkirk, by Lacaton & Vassal, is the latest project to be realised in this series.

In the UK, Dunkirk, in northern France, is lastingly associated with the evacuation of hundreds of thousands of troops in the early stages of the Second World War. In France it has always been synonymous with shipbuilding, and the port of the town was the centre of the production of, initially, liners and warships, and then oil tankers and car ferries throughout most of the 20th century. This was until 1988 when many of the shipyards were closed. Like so many industrial landscapes, made obsolete by economic change and industrial decline, the port was slowly abandoned, creating a huge 150-hectare derelict site, fi lled with many empty and abandoned industrial buildings. One, the AP2 (the Atelier De Prefabrication No 2), a ship and boat repair and making workshop lovingly referred to by locals as ‘the cathedral’, was an epically proportioned concrete frame shed. As with numerous industrial landscapes made redundant by changes in industrial production throughout the last century, including shipbuilding, the buildings’ locations often proved to be their saviour. The attractive port location ensured two successive regeneration masterplans were developed in order to regenerate the area. The second, a proposal that is currently being realised, ensured that the local council allocated a €15 million budget to an architectural competition with which to develop a space into a new FRAC. The competition, won by Lacaton & Vassal, proposed to reuse the AP2 to accommodate a new contemporary art centre, one that would become the centre of the regeneration and initiate the slow return to prosperity for the area, a situation not seen since the industrious shipbuilding days.

## Analysis

We’re always very curious about what we are going to fi nd. We think that there is a lot of potential in what already exists. Every existing situation has its own special quality, and you have to take your time and be curious in order to understand it…I think its very important today to take the existing as a starting point – this includes existing buildings and the existing atmosphere.

– Interview with Lacaton & Vassal by Mathieu Wellner. <sup>48</sup>

The designers analysed the physical elements of the building as well as understanding and giving equal importance to the atmospheric conditions of the extant building spaces. The existing AP2 that the designers found on the site was a huge open volume, with an inside space with dimensions of 75 by 25 metres and over 30 metres high, a volume in all totalling approximately 11,200 square metres. The hall itself was almost exactly half of the volume of the Tate Modern Turbine Hall: the impressive reuse of an existing industrial building across the channel in London that this project may draw comparison with (see rereadings volume 1). Rather than the heavy brick carapace of the old London power station, here the designers were gifted a large concrete frame to work with: a fl exible form and structure, one that provided a space that allowed large sections of boats to be constructed, as well as a space made more fl exible by the absence of any internal columns in the large unbroken volume of the hall.

Figure 4.3 The new masterplan of the port places the prominent FRAC at the centre of its redevelopment.  
![](images/816f220dbbcdc1a8f81445c31732641bb53ee72ecd30e0d0cbd536b510e6d900.jpg)

The light and acoustic properties of the huge hall were also considered impressive and tangible elements to be reused in the design processes of reworking the existing building. The impression of the qualities of the existing spaces upon the designers was exceptional, and it shaped their proposal, which, amongst the fi ve competition entries submitted, was the only one that did not place a new structure within the existing hall. Instead, through an analysis of the qualities of the existing space, the designers decided to leave the shed untouched, and use it as a space that could be interpreted in a number of ways: primarily through its use as an epically proportioned arts space. Instead of potentially diminishing the space through fi lling it in, the designers proposed a new building, created in adjacency to the shed that would contain the support spaces that FRAC required, such as offi ces, cafés and storage, as well as new gallery spaces. This strategy would then allow the main shed to remain relatively untouched and maintain its open and fl exible volumes to display art. This proposal was costed at a price of €1 million, signifi cantly cheaper than the budget that was set for the competition. It was their analysis of the existing form and structure of the building, derived from its form use as a ship-fabrication space that ensured the designers not only wanted to retain the form of the host, but to replicate it. Through the development of an adjacent doppelgänger shed, they created a large, open, fl exible arts space, and one that could become a signifi cant cultural destination and provide an opportunity to regenerate the area.

PARCOURS VERTICAL DE VISITE  
Figure 4.4 Axonometric diagram showing the stack of support spaces and smaller exhibition areas in the new addition.  
![](images/d42daa15cb0f5aa2287225a84df5287ffe4880fda13dbba921bc7d9452a007c8.jpg)

## Strategy Insertion

To achieve this concept, the project creates a double of AP2 (Atelier de Prefabrication), of the same dimensions, attached to the existing building, on the side which faces the sea, which contains the program of FRAC. The new building juxtaposes delicately without competing or fading. The duplication is an attentive response to the identity of the old building.

– Lacaton & Vassal <sup>49</sup>

To copy something is both a type of compliment as well as a tacit form of approval for the qualities of something already existing. An approach based on any form of imitation will of course involve the translation of the material of the existing, or original, to be replicated. But fi rst, it requires a thorough analysis of the qualities and matter of the exemplar from which the new is to be formed. From their analysis of the existing building, and through a thorough understanding of the new programme that was to be developed in the space, the designers inserted a new building directly adjacent to the existing shed. This new-build, placed alongside AP2, adapted the exact form and measurements of the existing creating a clone of the original space. This doppelgänger approach to constructing the new museum allowed the designers to utilise effi cient and economic construction technologies, to create a new perfectly formed copy of the concrete frame exemplar, whilst leaving the host relatively untouched. This approach created a huge monumental un-programmed space, within which a diverse range of exhibitions and events could take place. For the new AP2, a concrete frame structure was erected, clad with a lightweight polycarbonate sheet, akin to a greenhouse structure. This structure

Figure 4.5 The new insertion was realised as an architectural ‘doppelgänger’ of the existing building. Its realisation allowed the existing AP2 to remain as a large open art space.

![](images/1b0c38289fee59e8832f3f4c4c65f1724e5cec7f50df32421d715133ca80f2a2.jpg)

replicated the exact proportions of its immediate neighbour, and housed smaller gallery spaces, café, administration and archives, as well as all of the servicing needs of the new building such as plant rooms and so on. Essentially this created a twin space, containing all of the support spaces, allowing its other half to stay unfettered, open and fl exible, able to encompass numerous methods of displaying a wide range of both large and small exhibits.

## Tactics

The robust nature of the existing AP2 ensured that the designers needed to choose a particular set of tactics to create a powerful visual language for the new building. These were required in order to ensure that it did not disappear against its ‘tougher’, more established and resilient neighbour. Also the designers – who had to work hard to convince the clients that the insertion of a new-build project alongside the old was, as proposed, economically achievable – needed to fi nd construction as well as material solutions that were costeffective and feasible. This led to a consistent ‘light’ touch approach, combined with an off-the-peg aesthetic, where already existing technologies could be utilised for the rehabilitation of the old and the fabrication of the new.

## Surface

The monumental scale of the original AP2 was complemented by a straightforward approach to the existing surfaces of the space. The drama of the raw space was maintained by leaving the surfaces of the room untouched. The concrete frame of the shed was left untouched, the fl oor cleaned and repaired in places but also left, and any scratches, marks and abrasions were retained in order to communicate the

previous life of the building. Whereas the muscular language of the AP2 was all about the interior and exterior frame, in order to counterpoint this raw aesthetic the ‘twin’ building was sheathed in a lightweight plastic skin: a move that created a ghostly counterpart to its more solid looking neighbour. The rough and ready workmanlike language provided the perfect backdrop to the exhibition of contemporary art as well as a challenge to curators and artists to compete with the tough, robust host space.

## Light

The north-facing location of the building ensured that an energy and budgeteffi cient cladding system could be deployed across the new building. The polycarbonate skin was complemented by a roof constructed of ETFE (Ethylene tetrafl uoroethylene) sheeting, ensuring a higher level of insulation and also creating a large open space in the new-build. This space was enthusiastically described as the ‘Belvedere’ space, a hanging space for exhibitions and meetings. On this level the agricultural-standard construction materials enclosed the view through the translucent skin, to the harbour beyond. Canvas shades were added that could be deployed across the roof if the occupants of the space decided they needed shielding from the hot rays of the summer sun.

## Movement

The traditional mechanism of the architectural promenade, a device often deployed in galleries in order to ensure a logical tour through the space, was rendered vertically in FRAC, and undertaken via lifts and stairs placed in a strip of circulation located at the entrance of the building. The space between the AP2 and its counterpart was left as a fl oor-toroof void, a prelude to the epic grandiosity

of the adjacent hall access on the ground fl oor of the corridor.

## Opening

The epic scale of the front door of the AP2 was retained and where once large over-scaled fragments of boats and raw materials would have passed through them, now artworks are trucked into the large hall and distributed around the building.

The richness of this insertion lies in the resonance of the new building when set directly against its existing adjoining ‘twin’. This is a design process and subsequently a construction technique that relies on reproducing the epic and monumental qualities of its adjacent neighbour. Insertions can derive their scale and form from the host building that they are placed into, around, alongside or upon. When the existing building or space is as resonant and as powerful as something like the AP2, its echo provides an oscillation, one that the new insertion can match, and ultimately become equal to.

Figure 4.6 The upper belvedere fl oor of the new addition is shaded from the direct sunlight by blinds drawn across the roof of the space.

![](images/0bb1a1998d8e8394a4aa8998d66b9490d8dd4da955d130b6f8521abba57b0d1d.jpg)

## Neues Museum

Location Berlin, Germany

Function Museum

Original date 1859   
Remodelled 2009

Original Designer Friedrich August Stüler

Designer   
David Chipperfi eld   
Architects /   
Julian Harrap

![](images/29836a9ca00d110b706610d289b5aa239a5bf19e130b746e68f86a234ccaf16c.jpg)

Figure 4.7 Completing the logical sequence of movement through the building, the central stair was reinstated in dramatic fashion, ensuring an instantly recognisable contemporary element in contrast to the original central hall.

Reconstruction, conservation, restoration, renovation and repair are all at our disposal; no technique has a moral certitude of its own, only the purpose of its application can invest it with one.

– David Chipperfi eld.<sup>50</sup>

On the River Spree in the central Mitte district of Berlin, Museum Island consists of fi ve signifi cant institutions. Originally a residential area, the fi rst exhibition hall was erected in 1797. Then Karl Friedrich Schinkel completed the Altes Museum in 1830, Friedrich August Stüler erected the Neues Museum in 1859, and the Alte

Nationalgalerie followed in 1876. Ernst von Ihne, Alfred Messel and Ludwig Hoffmann completed the Bode and Pergamon Museums in 1904 and 1930 respectively. The latter was designed to house monumental antiquities such as the Pergamon Altar, and the Market Gate of Miletus, appropriated from archaeological excavations in Turkey and brought back to be housed in large and open spaces of the museum in which to communicate their grandeur to an awe-inspired public. The Altes Museum was designed to house ancient Greek, Roman and Etruscan antiquities. The Alte Nationalgalerie was designed to house paintings and

sculptures, whilst the Neues contained Egyptian and pre-history collections, including the iconic bust of the Egyptian Queen Nefertiti. Together this unique collection of museums provided a focal point for the cultural and intellectual life of the city of Berlin. Occupying a peninsula surrounded by the River Spree, the group of cultural attractions came to be known as Museum Island.

Unfortunately, the island provided a signifi cant target in the Second World War, and the buildings sustained considerable damage. The Neues Museum received the most sustained destruction, all from continued aerial bombardment. At the end of hostilities, the island was severely damaged, and the Neues Museum was left in ruins, with entire sections of the building missing and other areas severely fi redamaged. The destruction was at its greatest in the staircase hall, lost during a bombing raid in November 1943. Also damaged was the southeast cupola, the northwest wing, and the Egyptian courtyard.

All were signifi cantly damaged in air raids in November 1943 and February 1945. After the war, few attempts were made to repair the damage and consolidate the ruins. But, with more destruction occurring as the building was left roofl ess and open to the elements, it fell further into disrepair and was ultimately in danger of complete

Figure 4.8 The bomb-damaged ruined central stair hall.

![](images/c4d09d95429495613adb7a86cee66203744d8b6d4344da365c024d09932e98ed.jpg)

collapse. It wasn’t until the reunifi cation of Germany in 1990 that the saving and the reconstruction of the building could be considered. David Chipperfi eld Architects, along with Julian Harrap, won the 1997 competition to embark on a project to save the building, and to reopen the project to the general public as the Egyptian Museum and the Museum of Pre-History. Their proposal was to not only repair and restore the building, but to return it to full use as one of the key cultural institutions on the island.

## Analysis

By strange or lucky accident almost every conceivable level of survival of the interior structure, decoration and surface fi nish occurs somewhere in the Warren of the Neues Museum.

– Robert Harbison. Ruins and Fragments – Tales of Loss and Rediscovery. <sup>51</sup>

Upon winning the project, the offi ces of Chipperfi eld and Harrap undertook exhaustive research on the existing building, analysing its history and evaluating its current condition. This was done in order to establish a rigorous strategy for reconstructing the monument. The designation of the island as a World Heritage Site in 2008 focused the development of a unique approach to the reconstruction of the building. Along with this designation, the previous function and history of the building ensured that whichever approach and methods the designers undertook, their processes would be closely scrutinised and, due to the importance of the project, would always prove to be reliably controversial. This was because total reconstruction, based on the historic principles of what existed originally, or copying what was once in place and

making the building appear untouched, was one potential strategy. Another was just the clearing away of the ruins of what was essentially a collapsing building and starting again. These approaches provided the two outer parameters, or bandwidth of the debate. It was one that ranged between conservation, restoration, selective demolition and occasional reinstatement. Within this range of options, the designers developed an intellectually consistent approach.

This was based on, where possible, the retention of any material left extant that was of historic value and signifi cance to what was left surviving. This meant that a thorough analysis of the ruins and the ravages of history was required. A cataloguing of all of the bombardment damage, of the resultant dereliction from its prolonged exposure to nature, and all of the minor alterations and repairs, needed to be undertaken and subsequently assessed and calibrated as to its worth or value to the overall narrative of the site’s history.

Each aspect of the ruin was analysed and carefully documented through drawings and models. Each room was examined and the remains of frescoes, fabric and surfaces were detailed and recorded. Some spaces were only partially damaged, and these offered a road map for a possible language of resolution, whilst others were unrecognisable and damaged beyond repair. The guides were original drawings and pictures, but, as the designers noted, in these spaces, the most important thing to preserve was the memory of their destruction.

The form and structure of the original building ensured that a strong and logical plan of movement through the spaces was instilled into the design. The enfi lade of rooms, arranged around a central stair, meant that whilst the central stair and a wing, a courtyard and the corner cupola had been lost, the replacement of these elements would reinstate the main routes through the building and restore the rational functioning of the building.

Structurally, because of the unstable marshland of the river and its banks upon which the museum was built, Stüler and his engineers had to construct the building on a bed of timber piles, driven into the soft swampy ground. Stüler also incorporated new technologies such as a cast-iron frame within the building, secreted behind the masonry walls and façades. In order to ensure that the building complied with

Figure 4.9 Long section showing the central stair hall fl anked by the two courtyards and wings.  
![](images/3701f8a8f628f5ac83e2a7b03f1aab1be447052c61894441a1193193ba20ee12.jpg)

21st century standards the design team had to carefully analyse these existing structural systems in order to establish whether they could take the new loadings about to be placed upon them. This approach also facilitated an understanding that new services, such as lighting, heating, and security could be carefully inserted into the existing building in such a way as to not compromise the historic fabric of the space and be discretely hidden from view.

## Strategy

The meticulous process of repair and restoration…makes explicit the process as a series of informed and careful judgements, balancing the status of the piece with the vision of the whole. This approach pervades the whole project, ensuring that the new and the old, the original and the repair, the restoration

![](images/7cc973a5c9f320f812208f1b6b6869a3ceed53b9e563677c1d4bd380c1f46814.jpg)  
Figure 4.10 Sketch showing the adaptation of the existing building's demolished wings in order to reinstate the logic of its circulation.

![](images/c8dd2edfc7fff6e3064f4b0d8c792e22a545ded8968bbce2e68cddd99d77f522.jpg)  
ne-KSTABIsn meno ok Form+ Figry

and the intervention, are fused together into a singular whole.   
– David Chipperfi eld.<sup>52</sup>

Intervention is a strategy where the analysis of an existing building will provide a response that proposes that both new and old will co-exist, ensuring a close relationship between the past, the present and with an eye on the future. It is a process where some clarifi cation will be imposed upon the space through the interrogation and subsequent interpretation of what is already extant. This may include demolition as well as the reinstatement of components with which to complete the story of the building.

Utilising an interventionist approach, the designers set out to recomplete the ruin through reinstating original elements lost during its destruction. This included the northwest wing and courtyard, the southeast cupola, and to facilitate the completion of the logical sequence of movement through the rooms of the museum, the grand central stair. Unlike the reconstruction of the bomb-damaged Altes

Figure 4.11   
A meticulous   
calibration of the   
existing surfaces of the museum ensured that, where possible, they   
were retained to   
provide maximum   
effect against the new display cases.

![](images/340fb69d98d6a21f7f45b6cf5f8c2e75f43dcdab2a86f15e464a4f4d16312812.jpg)

Pinakothek in Munich (see rereadings volume 1), the tragedy of destruction was not to be memorialised, nor were the designers trying to make a historical reproduction. Instead they wanted to unite the fragments of the ruin to become a building that neither celebrated nor hid its history, but instead included it in a continuous whole: constructing a narrative where both new and old were integrated. Therefore intervention was used in order to integrate both new and old in an amalgam or composite construct of past, present and future.

## Tactics

## Surface

Through a careful and detailed analysis of each room the surface condition was established and appropriate measures applied. This operation ensured that the value of each space and its condition was detailed and then a corresponding approach to its reformulation was devised. Corridors such as the Romischer Saal (Roman Hall) were cleaned and repaired, with the fl oors reinstated. Upper level frescoes were cleaned and stabilised yet where damaged were left intact, with no attempt to ‘complete’ or fi nish them. The surfaces in the Treppenhalle, or staircase hall, required extensive work. The 1943 bomb that tore through the roof of the hall, completely destroyed the stair, along with the huge stereochrome paintings by Wilhelm von Kaulbach; completed in 1866 and depicting The History of Mankind in Six Images, these images had fi lled the entire upper zone of the hall.

The empty and damaged monumental brickwork shell of the staircase hall had left a strong impression on the designers. Through analysis of the space they

discovered 1988–89 brickwork repairs made with basic bricks that did not match the original masonry. This included some of the brick courses being haphazardly grouted with concrete. These were either suppressed, or abraded in order to make them not stand out so much, or replaced with historic bricks.

The stair hall was cleaned, but the blackened discolouration left by the fi re from the bomb was retained wherever it was found. This approach added to the narrative of the life of the building. The few remaining cornices under the mural were stabilised and conserved. It was found that there was a band of airbricks, positioned just below the upper mural zone, that were designed to act as ventilation for the hall. This was especially useful as the application of the mural to the surface would have made a vapour-proof barrier on the brickwork, leading to the potential problem of damp, initiated through a lack of air movement. These bricks were cleaned and retained in situ, adding to the air movement within the hall.

## Movement

The loss of the main staircase at the centre of the building created the need for a new element that would complete the effi cient movement around the museum. Formed from white-cement prefabricated concrete components, mixed with Saxonian marble chips, the new central stair was designed to replicate the shape and form of the original, with a single stair from ground to fi rst and twin stairs from fi rst to second. Yet it was constructed in such a way as to contrast with the elegant ruins of the space around it, ensuring an instantly recognisable contemporary element within the existing original staircase hall. The sparkle of the chips in the aggregate has ensured that the wide grand sweep of the stair as it services the two upper fl oors of the museum, is unmistakably modern and in stark contrast to the rough brick surroundings.

## Object

A new fl oor in the space of the Egyptian courtyard, free-standing to assert its independence, as well as letting light into the lower fl oor, was inserted in order to provide more display space for the museum. Finished in the same quartzaggregate concrete, the supporting column-structure of the platform was carried on to become support elements for the new glass roof covering the space. Refi ned frameless-glass cases contain busts and sculptures set against a tall sand-blasted glass balustrade: a high screen that stops the gaze of the visitor being distracted by the detailed patterns of the brick shell of the courtyard and instead focuses the view on each of the objects in the space.

# Zenkai House / House Surgery

Location Takarazuka, near Osaka, Japan

Former function Home New function Design studio

Original date 1900s

Remodelled 1997

Designer Katsuhiro Miyamoto & Associates

![](images/ec8d7c3f03ab376279eb476fcbf770b1d250c5b6b55679a1fdf219ce448b8463.jpg)

Figure 4.12 The restructuring of the house, and the subsequent dynamic relationship between the past and the present of the space, is emphasised in the main ground fl oor room of the house/offi ce.

Every intervention results in some   
destruction. Destroy then at least with understanding.   
– Luigi Snozzi. <sup>53</sup>

The Zenkai house project was reconstructed in 1997 in the Japanese prefecture of Hyogo, two years after a severe and traumatic natural disaster. The house was severely damaged in the Great Hanshin-Awaji or Kobe earthquake which claimed 6,300 lives. One of the hardest-hit areas was the cities and suburbs of the Hyogo Prefecture, including Takarazuka. One in fi ve homes was destroyed or

rendered uninhabitable by the shock, and a quarter of public buildings were damaged beyond repair. Any buildings constructed after 1981 mostly survived the earthquake as they were built to a new set of codes that ensured they were shock resistant. However, those that were not constructed to those standards suffered serious structural damage or complete collapse. Many of the older traditional houses in the district had heavy tiled roofs that weighed around two tonnes, structured in order to resist the frequent typhoons that plagued Kobe and the surrounding areas. These tended to be structurally overloaded, and

usually only supported by a light timber structural frame. In the earthquake, when the wood supports gave way in the tremors, the roofs collapsed the unreinforced walls and fl oors and fl attened the building, resulting in the loss of many lives and the devastation of the area.

## Analysis

Zenkai house is a document of my journey as an architect. I didn’t change the existing building at all; I just put new elements on and in it. We can see two generations of buildings now.

– Interview with architect in Zenkai House,18th August 2016. <sup>54</sup>

Constructed in the 1900s, the traditional house that Katsuhiro Miyamoto was born in was so severely damaged in the disaster that the local authorities deemed the house uninhabitable and decreed that it should be demolished. The sentimental values that Miyamoto felt for the building, the family memories, and the histories of the spaces imbued within it prompted a different response to the authorities’ desire to demolish and start again. Mixing the words for Zen (totally), with Kai (collapse), the designer did not want to forget the disaster by removing all signs of the tragedy. Instead he wanted the urban environment to remember and in some way memorialise and refl ect this tumultuous period in its recent history. Rather than lose the character of the site and demolish the row of houses in which his home sat, Miyamoto proposed to intervene upon the ruin with a new structure, one that would impact upon the existing building in such a way as to render it a hybrid of new and old structures and stand as a monument to the recent past. The new building would also serve the pragmatic function of bringing the house not only up to current earthquake resistant

codes, but also going beyond what was needed as structural reinforcement. Analysis of the existing building was deepened by Miyamoto’s knowledge of the house that he grew up in. Site is important in all of his work:

I study the site in each project for a long time. Always I can fi nd something that sits behind the ‘scenery’ of the surfaces that I am looking at. I fi nd vestiges of history, from the site or from a picture of it, anything. I analyse what has accumulated over time, looking for meaning and a way forward as a guide for the site and the new project.

– Interview with the architect, 18th August 2016. <sup>55</sup>

In Zenkai House, form and structure, context, environment and new use have all been analysed and reused, especially in relation to the designer’s close connection to the place. Most of all memory and the history of the building have played an important role in acting as a guide for the reuse of the family home and for it to be made a design offi ce for Miyamoto’s architecture studio. The familial ties were so strong that Miyamoto planned the new uses of the building to be positioned where the existing ones were housed. For instance, the main meeting room was located in the old living room. The kitchen, accessed through a small double-height enclosed courtyard, would now become the printing room and archive. Miyamoto’s offi ce was located in what was his old bedroom, complete, by his desk, with a set of notches on the surviving timber frame, documenting his growth as a child.

It is the analysis of the building, combined with the sentimentality of the memories and histories of the family that the building embodies, that has ensured that the designer has reused the building in such a

Figure 4.13 a and b Model: A signifi cant intervention, in the form of a new steel frame, was imposed unceremoniously upon the existing timber structure.

![](images/b2033c6a51e5877d7179893d792cd6fecd535e0f93a18b20b15ee810695d9788.jpg)

way as to meld numerous generations of inhabitation into one amalgam of new and old, past, present and future.

## Strategy

## Intervention

I suppose that the loads will be gradually transferred to the steel framework. The wooden framework will retire from the structure, and then it will exist just as a fi xture which retains the memory of the habitant’s lives and the earthquake disaster.

– Katsuhiro Miyamoto. <sup>56</sup>

Miyamoto chose to reuse and stabilise the domestic building by intervening with the existing wooden structure with a new steel frame. The frame was designed to act as a support and brace for the old timber beams and columns, ultimately, in time, taking over the role of the new structure from the old timber beams and columns. The designer likened this process to a patient in a hospital, wrapped in a plaster cast after an accident where they have broken their limbs. The frame was designed to be a support not just for the existing building but it was also a metaphor for the healing of the house and the city after the disaster that befell it.

The new steel frame was unceremoniously placed back and in amongst the structure of the house. Then the wooden beams and columns were lashed to the new frame. The effect upon the interior ensured that the new intervention was clearly distinguished from the old, and in some places leaving the junctions between new and old brutally exposed, enhanced its dynamic quality. The new structure was evident in each room, a constant reminder of the history of the city and the unfortunate disaster that befell it. As Miyamoto stated:

![](images/ea8d3e20594dd37361d710384a1cc19306f61c666b629753fca19c00f342ca84.jpg)

Usually architecture is constructed from the bottom to top. In this building it is done the other way around and we have grafted the new into and amongst the old. <sup>57</sup>

In this instance intervention was used as a commentary on the existing building. Intervention is a robust and sometimes brutal way of reusing existing space but, in this building, this vigorous intervention also expresses a sentimental dimension. Whilst the building was forcefully imposed upon, with steel work unceremoniously grafted onto and through the timber frame, columns and beams attached brusquely to the existing structures and diagonal bracings pierced through screens and walls, the new structure acted as a scaffold or a frame, supporting and nurturing the relationship between the new and the old. Instead of trying to replicate what was once there, a strategy that could have suffocated the memories of the place and instead museumifi ed the interior, the radical new intervention offered a composite construct of meaning, clearly delineating a new generation of use for the old family home.

## Tactics

The earthquake disaster exposed hidden elements of the building. When it removed the shattered walls, the frames were revealed and exposed. I left as much as I could. The plaster, the wires, the usually hidden away innards of a building I exposed to the outside world. – Katsuhiro Miyamoto. <sup>58</sup>

## Surface

Traditionally, designers will fabricate new spatial identity by imposing new surfaces, often in the form of new materials and

coverings. In a building so replete with existing memories and narratives, each surface would have exemplifi ed specifi c memories through particular materials. Miyamoto kept as much of the shattered materiality of the existing building as he could: the extant walls, surfaces and materials were retained in order to provoke a deep and profound connection to the past of the building.

## Plane

Where possible, existing screen walls, fl oors and ceilings were retained and kept. The new structure was placed in, around and through them. On the façade of the building the narrative of the story of the building was exemplifi ed by the new steel structure. A cross-brace, formed in steel section, not only bound the existing and

![](images/794211d57558bcf2d9e11e4b40f66aee1f9da1c8ea524d1f9f9003bdbda58870.jpg)  
Figure 4.14 The uncompromising quality of the new structure provides a noticeable façade treatment for the front of the building.

new framework together, it offered a stark reminder of the journey of the building and the city, symbolising the radical and robust intervention of new into old inside the building.

## Light

A small central double-height covered internal courtyard was retained in order to allow natural light deep into the plan of the building. This also afforded a space for some of the unusual steel structural members to be positioned in the building. The courtyard offered some respite from the intensity of the structural gymnastics of the interior, in the top-lit interior space.

Sometimes an event takes place that changes a city and its urban infrastructure forever. In this context the process of rebuilding the house through the retention and reuse of its existing buildings and spaces was reliant on a series of selective and also dramatic choices. The retention of any existing buildings that are salvageable allows them to become instruments enabling both new and old to coexist in order to narrate history and chart progress, especially in a diffi cult or distressing context.

![](images/61f2244968def08b72bf4dce1a69a0e4c63238957c462cce70a24ef408e8c007.jpg)  
Figure 4.15 A central double-height courtyard allows natural light deep into the building between the front and the back of the house.

# The Cement Factory / La Fabrica

Location Sant Just Desvern, Barcelona, Spain

Former function Cement works

New function Family home and studio

Remodelled 1975

Designer Ricardo Bofi ll

![](images/dd70c89e962dfd8bc8ad2211951d0f5ccfa306eb3c6f08ea1bd9f624430d1cc8.jpg)

Figure 4.16 Rusting industrial relics, from the history of the cement production, were left in situ in order to provide dramatic backdrops, as well as a reminder of the past, in the main rooms of the domestic spaces.

I found enormous silos, a tall smokestack, four kilometres of underground tunnels, and machine rooms in good shape. This was twentyfi ve years ago and it was my fi rst encounter with the Cement Factory. I already imagined future spaces and noticed that the different aesthetic and plastic tendencies that had developed since World War I were present in this factory – Surrealism: Paradoxical stairs that lead to nowhere. The absurdity of certain elements that hung over voids,

potent but useless spaces of strange proportion but magical because of their tension and disproportion – Abstraction: Pure volumes, which reveal themselves at times broken and impure – Brutalism: Abrupt treatment and sculptural   
qualities of the materials.   
– Ricardo Bofi ll. <sup>59</sup>

The existing Cement Factory works on the edge of Barcelona were noticeable for many years, not least because it had one of the largest chimneys in the surrounding

areas, but also because it was dramatically polluting the local environment with its belching smoke. It came to Bofi ll’s attention as he was driving out of the city, and his casual enquiry about the availability of the land around it was fortuitous, as the works were closing and about to leave the area. Bofi ll wanted to purchase the land, and ultimately he fell in love with the ruinous qualities of the cement works, purchasing the factory and the surrounding land. He then set about selectively retaining and demolishing the buildings of the works, landscaping the brutal buildings and the surrounding yards and spaces, in order to make his own home and studio in the building: creating spaces for numerous studio workers and the projects he was undertaking as well as creating a place for cultural activities, exhibitions, and a hub for the intellectual life of his studio, colleagues, family and friends.

## Analysis

Bofi ll considered the building as a completed ruin that was then to be remade in order to accommodate the new series of functions. These included his home as well as an offi ce and studio, workshops and exhibition spaces. An analysis of the history and previous function of the building instigated the development of a series of ideas for the new uses for the building. Through the analysis of what was extant, Bofi ll realised that some of the existing spaces would not only accommodate new future uses but also would symbolise the utopian idea he had for the site: a space of creativity and workshops where he could assemble not just architects, but also painters, philosophers and mathematicians to work on design projects. In particular the 30 enormous silos of the works were of interest and he discovered that the dimensions and proportions of the tall cylindrical cement storage towers could be reused to house studio and offi ce workspaces. He decided to retain 12 of

Figure 4.17 The completed ruin of the home and offi ces.

![](images/f467c426162f2d5cac621d4da7799a056d7ea7edc56e374bbd7ab80008cd53ec.jpg)

Figure 4.18 The cement works when they were in operation (top).

Figure 4.19 The strategic demolition of various elements of the existing cement factory (bottom).

them, using a group of six and another of four as offi ces, with two on the main house accommodating new functions and services.

This analysis afforded the designer a strategy with which to selectively demolish some of the unrequired aspects of the cement works. Many of the buildings that had been added onto the main elements of the factory had been constructed in order to accommodate new aspects of cement production, as the methods of making it had changed through time. Based on this, the initial construction work, which began with the partial destruction of the robust building with dynamite and jackhammers, lasted for more than a year and a half. It was a precision job, which consisted in revealing the hidden forms and the recovering of certain important spaces. Bofi ll compared this work to the work of the sculptor whose fi rst task is to confront the material. In some instances the silos were still full of cement and they had to be emptied, cleaned and then made fi t for purpose for their new uses as offi ces and domestic spaces.

## Strategy

Various spaces appeared, with different characteristics: The Cathedral, The Garden, and the silos. Later I had to mark the new constructions with a specifi c vocabulary, which integrates various languages from the history of Architecture, a cultured language in opposition to vernacular architecture. I imagined windows, doors, stairs, and false perspectives and applied them to the exterior walls and some of the interiors. Slowly, with the valuable help of Catalan craftsmen, the Cement Factory was transformed, but it will always remain as an unfi nished work. – Press release. <sup>60</sup>

![](images/f888633dfd49afa04c559b16037a4209c335cae30f653cd877b23b702ee323fb.jpg)

![](images/52b0ef1ee6eda7300eb426b3e53eff4925bfff8360c4930b4e1f9adeeb9acb21.jpg)

The Cement Factory was reused and rehabilitated utilising an insertion strategy. Spaces such as the offi ces, workshops and elements of the domestic spaces, were built to fi t the existing. The dramatic qualities of the powerful exposed brutal concrete structure have ensured that the designer could only work with the monolithic qualities of the extant spaces. In other words, the unusual forms of the existing factory buildings, such as the silos, have provided a unique set of host spaces against which the designer can express the desire to create bespoke spaces that are purposely built to fi t and host uses that they were never intended to contain.

## Tactics

It is for me the only place where I can concentrate, associate ideas in the most abstract manner, and fi nally,

create projects, images and new spaces, and constitute a specifi c vocabulary for my architecture. I have the impression of living in a closed universe, which shields me from the outside and everyday life. The Cement Factory is a place of work par excellence. Life goes on here with very little difference between work and leisure.

– Press release. <sup>61</sup>

## Surface

The monumental surfaces of the concrete structure were exposed, cleaned and left as found. This was done so that they provided an evocative backdrop for the new elements of the home and offi ce, as well as providing an overpowering reminder of the buildings previous function. The concrete was left unrepaired and unfi nished – a constant reminder that the project itself is also a constant work in progress.

## Openings

In such a radical change of use, the selective ruination of the works, exposed areas of connections that were never previously part of the original building’s function. These new connections became openings in the frame of the building, apertures that were exposed and needed to be infi lled with new glazing and windows. The previous use of the building had ensured that the admittance of natural light or movement through the spaces was not required: the storage of cement or the facilitation of its processing was all that was needed; it required no natural light. Therefore new openings to admit light into living and working spaces were punched through the concrete structure: selectively opening up and exposing the interior of the buildings in a way that had never been envisaged before.

## Movement

The large spans of the existing robust structure ensured that the interior could be as fl exible as required. In both the domestic and workspaces, new circulation was required to facilitate the new uses of the spaces. For instance, in the silos containing the offi ces a new circular stair was inserted into the cylindrical space, accommodating movement between the fl oors of the buildings.

## Light

New windows provided new ways of naturally lighting the space, affording new ways of seeing and using the interiors of the building more.

## Object

Found objects such as huge rusted steel hoppers and funnels for the concrete making processes were left in situ in order to provide a stark reminder of the previous life of the building. In amongst the domestic-scale elements of the house, and in some areas of the workspace, these enormous sculptural elements, monuments to the buildings past, resonate in their role as backdrops to the elements of everyday contemporary living.

The atmospheric industrial relic of a space has provided a powerful and characterful backdrop for the offi ces and domestic spaces of the studio and family of Bofi ll. The new elements, inserted into the existing spaces are built to fi t the host space, creating what can only be described as a dynamic ‘amalgam’ of both new and old interior spaces, elements and atmospheres – an environment that is impossible to replicate in a new-build project.

## Flatland

Location SculptureCentre, New York, USA

Context The Happiness of Objects Exhibition

Function Living space for three weeks

Designers Date Alex Schweder and 2007 Ward Shelley

![](images/25d5d778461549b929d50da3ba5edbdadf7924cc06dd1162f37e1d85d3078225.jpg)  
Figure 4.20 The thin, concentrated slither of a space focused the living arrangements of the six occupants for the three weeks that it was occupied.

Figure 4.21 Model: The installation was specifi cally designed to be structurally secure and yet leave no long-lasting impact upon the fabric of the gallery once it was removed.

Based on the Edwin Abbott novella, we made a building four storeys tall, twenty-four feet wide, and two-feet deep. Six of us committed to occupying the structure for three weeks with the only rule being “you can leave at any time, but thereafter you cannot reenter”. My fantasy at the beginning of this work was that we would quickly change the building in reaction to a space that constrained us. – Alex Schweder. <sup>62</sup>

The book Flatland, A Romance in Many Directions by Edwin Abbott describes a two-dimensional world occupied by geometric fi gures. The narrator is ‘A Square’, who leads the reader through an elaborate narrative on the implications of living in a world that is completely fl at. The book, a satire on the limits of perspective, inspired Alex Schweder and Ward Shelley to develop a living space, for six people, inside a gallery. It was an installation that was designed to test, to the very limits of some of the occupants, how a certain number of people could cohabit, when their environment was a severely constraining, narrow and fl at space.

## Analysis

This work can be thought of as an architectural caricature, a building that exaggerates something already occurring in and through architecture – namely, the construction of relationships between inhabitants through/by/as affected by the spaces they occupy. All too often unnoticed, these changes to our subjectivity have been habituated… If the walls allow sounds to permeate from the adjoining fl at, we fi nd ourselves changing how we use the space.<sup>63</sup>

Flatland was initiated and produced with a view to exploring the analysis of an intense, time-based function of a very particular space. The idea that six people were to co-exist in this project, in a public gallery, non-stop, and on full show for three weeks (apart from an enclosed bathroom unit, housing a toilet and shower), with food ordered online and delivered to the installation, ensured that the designers needed to carefully consider what would be needed to survive the duration of the installation. The constraints of the four storey, 18 square metre space, 7 metres wide and just 0.6 metres deep, were carefully detailed in order to allow the space to function for its occupants. As well as the analysis of the proposed function of the space, it needed to stand up and also be safe. So its form and its structure were carefully considered and detailed in order to ensure that it remained upright and structurally sound in the gallery space. As members of the public walked around it, and watched the everyday lives of the occupants unfold throughout the exhibition period, their safety needed to be carefully considered as well as ensuring that the spectacle before their eyes was permanently available for close scrutiny.

![](images/324b4226b0dbca80dc21eabf3815f6153f45fcf3de0340bedcfa0d9f88997f74.jpg)

During the duration of the project, three people left Flatland. Over the fi rst week, the group had split into two groups, those that favoured disordered environments, and were comfortable with some clutter and disruption, and those that preferred a much more orderly and tidy environment. Those that generated the disordered environment soon found the space too constraining, and because of their disorganised tendencies were chastised by the group favouring the ordered space, particularly as the disorder encroached on both groups’ use of the space and had a signifi cant impact on the environment. In this type of situation, a small space with signifi cant restrictions, even the smallest disruption, such as some discarded rubbish or a misplaced object such as a book, had an enormous impact upon each of the participants. Every centimetre of space became contested as it needed to be utilised and so every scrap of space was scrutinised for its value in the everyday life of the participants. After ten

Figure 4.22 The occupants carried on their daily business inside the installation whilst on show to the gallery visitors.

![](images/4a3925a8e71ff5e98130ba7e24cd2cd9406a34942154c19407a2721c854285ea.jpg)

days, the group of disordered occupants abandoned the structure. The three remaining participants stayed in the installation to the end of the show, peacefully going about their daily business and remaining aware of the impact upon their fellow co-habitants of the structure.

As Alex Schweder’s previous comment shows, Flatland set out to caricature spatial relationships between inhabitants. In the end it made a very important point, one that could be considered as fundamental to the design of interior space. When the space and the relationships between the people in it become coterminous, and both become attuned to certain sensibilities of how environments can be utilised and adapted for shared use, there is a requirement for a tacit agreement between participants as to how each other’s behaviours and actions impacts upon every inhabitant of the space. How reciprocity had become a critical strategy for the successful occupation on the environment.

## Strategy

Installation is a strategy where the placement of an object within a certain building will heighten awareness of the existing without compromising or overtly affecting the container it is positioned within. In other words, the installation will not necessarily be without any relationship to the host building, but it is an unwritten rule that the existing space becomes the backdrop to the installed element within it. Often the installation will be arranged or positioned in the space in order to give maximum impact to the elements, but it is generally assumed that the host space will need few, if any, minor modifi cations or changes to the space or building in order to successfully host the installation. This

approach ensures that it is the main spectacle within the environment in which it is contained: the host plays a subsidiary role in the importance of the project.

Flatland was designed and conceived to host the six occupants for three weeks in such a way as to make them safe, and for the installation to be structurally secure, yet once the exhibition was over, it could be removed with little or no effect on the existing building. For the duration of the project, the Sculpture Centre environment became little more than a backdrop or stage for the performance of the installation. Flatland was a performance piece. It was a piece of interior architecture that was very constraining for the occupants, and a space where every move had to be carefully considered because of the impact it would have upon each of the other inhabitants. Because of these tight constraints, the four storey element became diffi cult to modify or adapt, as each change had to be agreed with the other occupants of the space. These negotiations became diffi cult to achieve for two reasons. Because of the narrowness of the installation, it was impossible to arrange a meeting because nobody could physically do anything other than stand in a line within the half a metre wide structure.

Figure 4.23 The constraining framework of the installation ultimately reinforced the requirement to negotiate occupant relations during co-habitation.  
Figure 4.24 The narrow space confi ned its inhabitants within the steel structure.  
![](images/376ef82201bafe78603bbd6df2931ef0fc454c0c46a47e0279596c6db090ca13.jpg)

Secondly the breakdown in communication between the different groups ensured diffi culties in reciprocating or negotiating between the occupants and the building. Ironically, what started as an experiment in providing a framework for inhabitation, in which both behaviours and the building would be adapted and modifi ed to their needs and desires, became a constraining framework that restricted co-habitation and ultimately caused friction between differing forms of living.

![](images/352a42144d77fbfce7ce29efa09e938cd7fc613f7a9408a354cb1fa4f68cb88a.jpg)

## Tactics

## Movement

In this project movement and its negotiation became of utmost importance. The occupants moved vertically between the four levels of the building on ladders placed internally in the structure of the space. This required subtle negotiation, not just between the participants moving between fl oors and agreeing how each can use the ladders (for instance, who could come down and who could move up) but also between all of the inhabitants of the structure as it physically moved as people traversed the object and moved around it. For instance, if one of the occupants was undertaking something that required focus, then a moving building was not conducive to an activity such as reading.

## Openings

The open structure of the space allowed all of the occupants, as well as the elements of the installation, to be viewed from all sides of the gallery. The only enclosed space was the bathroom, itself plumbed in with hot and cold running water. Whilst the bathroom was enclosed, if anybody was using it their absence would be known, through their disappearance from view.

The lack of signifi cant acoustic separation in the bathroom space would probably also reveal who was in the bathroom. Therefore the performance aspect of Flatland was permanently ongoing. As visitors entered and moved through the gallery spaces, they could view everything occurring in the structure through the open framework: a spectacle of an exhibition of everyday life.

## Object

Carefully placed objects in space can provide new and innovative ways with which to view space. The four storey structure was a dominating and powerful element placed centrally within the gallery. The only relationship it had to the existing building was that it could fi t within the tall existing space. The open structure of Flatland, exposed all of the different uses of the space, and how each occupant had ordered their own private, yet very public, spaces.

## Inujima Seirensho Art Museum

Location   
Inujima Island,   
Japan

Former function Copper refi nery

New function Museum

Original date 1909   
Remodelled 2008

Designer Hiroshi Sambuichi

![](images/ce1620ce5dae9c0fa1fca363be4c04c0ed76994f2bb660229afee65990582323.jpg)

Figure 4.25 The sun gallery with Yukinori Yanagi's ‘Hero Dry Cell / Solar Note’ (2008).

When I saw Inujima for the fi rst time, I was shocked. I grew up nearby, in a place that was covered in green landscape, an environment full of nature and what I would call natural ‘heritage’. When I visited Inujima it felt despoiled. I was shocked at how the island had been radically altered and changed by quarrying and refi ning. I felt I had to expose and show both these good and bad elements of the island in the new project.

– Interview with architect, 25th August 2016, Hiroshima, Japan. <sup>64</sup>

Located in the Seto Inland Sea, amongst a number of large and small islands, Inujima is currently home to a very small population of around 50 residents. This wasn’t always the case for the coastal area. Although designated as a national park in 1934, for over a century the inland sea and the natural beauty of the islands was the location for the industrialisation of its natural resources. This gathered pace in the late 20th century with large petro-chemical plants being developed along the coast, oil refi neries located in places such as Mizushima and quarries and copper

refi neries in Naoshima and Inujima. Along with all of the associated infrastructural changes such as transport networks, roads and the requirements for energy, the population of the area substantially increased as workers came to operate the industries. Unfortunately all of these changes rapidly escalated the contamination of the area and in particular the pollution of the inland sea.

As well as oil and chemicals, industries such as shipbuilding, cotton spinning and cement production were dotted along the shores. The area was rich in copper, and was mined successfully for many years. In the 19th century, several inland copper refi neries were designated ‘problem facilities’, due to the pollution and environmental damage they were causing. In response to this they were moved and relocated to the islands in the sea.

In ‘The Seto Inland Sea: An Asian Archipelago’, Shunya Yoshimi describes how the relocation of Mitsubishi’s copper refi nery to Naoshima affected the island:

Not long after, nearly all of the trees on the Northern half of the island fell victim to the smoke pollution, and the mountains became denuded. – Insular insight. <sup>65</sup>

Figure 4.26 The remodelled copper refi nery with the main chimney around which the ‘moving materials of the gallery were organised.

![](images/e7632a4a49cde91ccc71c312cd985f08d65e59ed06a0bc081355f4c655781f1c.jpg)

In its entirety, the industrialisation of the area was impacting in a detrimental manner upon the sea, the islands, its coast and residents.

Inujima island had already been signifi cantly disfi gured by the quarrying of its own natural stone. Its granite was of good quality and highly prized. As a result of this, its western side had been completely altered through its deep extraction of stone (sometimes up to depths of 40 metres below sea level). This industry declined in the early 20th century, leaving the island irrevocably altered.

In 1909, a ‘problem facility’ copper refi nery, located in the suburbs of Okayama, was relocated to Inujima. Once built, the island refi nery was very successful, initially producing 300 tonnes of copper a month. At fi rst, it had over 2,000 workers, many of them living on the island; this led to a substantial increase in the requirement for housing and also entertainment. The copper plant was sold in 1913, and due to a complete collapse in copper prices it was closed down in 1919. The refi nery was abandoned, and with no work to sustain the rapid rise in the population, many of the workers left the island. At the peak of the refi nery operations in 1910, there were 6,000 residents on the island in both the quarrying and refi nery industries. In almost 100 years since its closure the refi nery was left to ruin, slowly becoming overrun with nature. Currently there are now just 57 registered residents, with an average age of 75.

We must shift from this civilisation of ‘scrap and build’ to a civilisation of sustainable growth. If not the inheritance and development of culture will be impossible, and the things that we create will inevitably be obliterated for future generations. I became convinced that for the revitalisation of this region and Japan, our ideal should be to ‘use what exists to create what is to be.

– Soichiro Fukutake. <sup>66</sup>

Soichiro Fukutake initiated the development of the regeneration of the island as well as the Seto inland sea. He had known the island since the early 1990s, and acquired the land and the refi nery in 2001. As he took over the initiating of a project by his father’s business, he started to redevelop the area through the initiation of various art projects and buildings. This started with the Benesse Art Site Naoshima, with Benesse House, by Tadao Ando, the fi rst building on that island, followed by numerous others. Hiroshi Sambuichi was entrusted with the Inujima project in 2004, aptly named Seirensho (refi nery) Art Museum, designed to inhabit the ruined copper refi nery on Inujima. Since Inujima, other projects have included Teshima Art Museum by Ryue Nishizawa, leading to a transformation in the perception of the islands.

## Analysis

In Inujima, I researched this place for two years, observing wind, water, light and the sun, in all of the seasons, in the cold and in the warmth, in order to understand how to capture them and make them components of the design, and making them what I consider to be the ‘moving materials’ of the site, elements to be used to design the building. – Interview. <sup>67</sup>

Figure 4.27 Energy Hall, with Yukinori Yanagi's ‘Hero Dry Cell / Solar Rock (2008), a mise-en-scène of the elements of the house of the author Yukio Mishima.

Through a long and detailed analysis of the existing context and environment, Sambuichi came to the conclusion that the natural materials of the island could be harnessed in order to provide a very unique museum. After careful study of the island and the location and context of the existing building, Sambuichi realised that the

refi nery was located in its current position due to a very particular environmental phenomenon. The refi ning process needed lots of air in order to power its smelters and also to remove their smoke. They also need to be protected from excessive winds from the monsoons. Through careful analysis of the positioning of the six remaining smokestacks of the refi nery on the island, and in particular through examining the patterns of their weathering, as well as analysing old images of smoking chimneys when the works were in full working order, Sambuichi realised that the chimneys were placed in a very critical position, one that protected them from excessive winds and one that also allowed a steady and constant supply of natural resource: wind and air. Therefore, somewhat ironically, this large industrial complex of buildings which, when working, housed a polluting set of processes, was positioned specifi cally in order to capture one thing: the movement of fresh and natural air. Sambuichi chose the largest and most intact chimney of the existing site with which to organise the layout of the site and with which to

![](images/0076b52fc09bd2bd26848988be478fdd8c5350ae14500237fa63f633d7c46f78.jpg)

Figure 4.28 The reuse of the moving   
materials of the site   
ensure that the   
previously polluting   
refi nery has become a model of   
sustainability.

maximise the most fundamental materials of this project – ‘air’ a ‘moving material’.

## Strategy

I don’t think that a building should be independent from its site. I considered the building and the landscape both as one. For me they were totally integrated. In this sense the moving materials then become the humans, the visitors as they interact with the site and the project. <sup>68</sup>

The new museum was organised around the existing chimneystack and took the form of a series of interconnected spaces, each of which was derived from the enhancement of the installation of a particular ‘moving material’. The air-drawing power of the chimney was enhanced by the placement of a glass-covered canopy at its base. Therefore as the air heated up, its density decreased and the fl ow of outside air, up through the chimney was facilitated. This provided the ‘engine’ of one of the ‘moving materials’. This entrance was located at the beginning of the earth gallery: a corridor that faced east and which cooled the air. The dark underground circulation corridor was lit with one major single source of natural light, with the sky refl ected through the space by a series of

carefully placed mirrors. The corridor was used to aid the movement of the visitors as well as cool down the air. The energy hall was located before the chimney hall, and then the sun gallery provided the route out to the café and courtyard, beyond which was the ruin of the refi nery set into the landscape. In the museum the fl ow of air and the movement of people was combined in order to create a continuous fl ow of materials through the museum. In the winter this fl ow of air is revered and instead it is warmed up in the sun gallery, into the energy hall and then out through the smokestack.

Various elements of the installation Hero Dry Cell by Yukinori Yanagi were located in each space. Composed of various actual elements of the Japanese author Yukio Mishima’s house, the energy hall contained Solar Rock, a suspended installation of fragments of his domestic space in Shoto. This comprised a space the size of three tatami mats, windows and door frames, and a single slab of Inujima granite that weighed 44 tonnes. Selected views from this room allow the sight of Icarus Tower hanging windows, doors and light fi ttings as well as a staircase and balustrade positioned on the ground. To complete the domestic mise-en-scène, a urinal and washbasin were placed upon the immaculately raked gravelled fl oor. The sun gallery was open to the elements and housed a timber-framed pavilion with pottery and domestic objects from the house where Mishima transcribed his last manifesto and was called Solar Note. Combined together, all of these spaces surmised the distributed contents of Mishima’s dwelling. Through utilising the strategy of installation the building became a container for the natural ‘moving materials’ of the site. And as each natural element grew in intensity, the suspended elements of the house of Yukio Mishima moved, re-animating the domestic space of the controversial author who had taken his own life at the age of 45.

![](images/7fd58a9c3e4eeecd788340e0127fa7c3c5e585af9e95f5b9ea59d4a590ec4ff9.jpg)  
INUJIMA ART PROJECT SEIRENSHO © SAMBUICHI ARCHITECTS Naoshima Fukutake Art Museum Foundation

## Tactics

It is important to use moving materials, solid materials such as concrete and steel, as well as more ephemeral matter, as these will control and infl uence more moving materials. For example, the combination of glass and the Karami bricks take the heat from the sun and heat air. Heat changes into the energy which moves the air.

– Sambuichi. <sup>69</sup>

## Objects

The fi rst time that Sambuichi visited the refi nery he became aware of the site being populated by an enormous amount of black bricks, strewn across the site. The bricks were the by-products of the smelting process and were considered waste and thus scattered all around the refi nery. After some analysis, Sambuichi realised that the bricks had excellent thermal properties. The 17,000 that were collected were reused in order to become the walls and fl oors of the new museum, and work as effective instruments, in combination with the planning of the museum and its spaces, for capturing and retaining the light and heat of the sun, as well as cooling the space in the summer. The environmental impact of the refi nery was minimised by utilising the bricks, and their thermal properties were such that the whole museum was designed without any air-conditioning or artifi cial heating systems. This reiterated the designer’s commitment to the environmental impact of the building on the island and expressing how its economic value has risen and fallen in tune with the fortune of the island.

## Movement

As well as the movement of ephemera materials such as light and air, the appropriation of the ruins was based on the sequential movement of visitors to the space. The 80 metre long dark subterranean entrance and corridor of the earth gallery, set two metres below ground level, directed visitors through the space, whilst around them the undulating walls extracted heat from the airfl ow. The darkness of the corridor was relieved by the energy hall, which was counterpointed with external views into the chimney hall and then the sun gallery. All of the interior spaces were contrasted with a less directed amble across the semi-ruined site of the almost derelict refi nery.

## Opening

In the energy and chimney hall, Yukinori Yanagi’s installation Hero Dry Cell was composed of suspended windows and doors extracted from the domestic space of Yukio Mishima. The aperture symbolised the critical role that openings and apertures play in the organisation of the building and the way that visitors view it as they pass through and around it.

After almost 100 years of redundancy, the site had decontaminated itself as nature slowly took over and outgrew the buildings of the refi nery. Sambuichi utilised the raw materials of the site, and the context, in order to install a museum that would touch the

earth as lightly as it could in order to leave a lasting legacy for the island and the people of Inujima, and a space that would aid the regeneration of the island.

![](images/58184897a9b544f2f4f509b7bb298589fb97643fb287d6402b771d8d4ebc4e6a.jpg)  
Figure 4.29 Earth Gallery, with Yukinor Yanagi, ‘Hero Dry Cell / Icarus Cell’ (2008).
